Dead Animal Parts: Classification and Ruling
Ruling on dead animal parts with inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify parts of a dead animal into those that are pure (bones, wool, etc.) and those that require tanning (skin), and explain the Hanafi proofs against other schools.

Core Idea
The skin of a dead animal becomes pure through tanning; bones and wool are inherently pure because death only affects the locus of life.

This lesson moves to the second topic in the passage: the ruling on parts of a dead animal (maytah). The matn distinguishes between the skin before tanning (impure, sale invalid) and after tanning (pure, sale valid). It also lists several parts that are always pure: bones, sinews, wool, hair, horn, fur. The commentary provides the rationale: death only takes effect in the locus of life, which is blood and flesh. This leads to a significant dispute with the Shafi'i school, which considers all parts of a dead animal impure. We will explore the Hanafi evidence and the practical outcome of the difference.

Guided Path
1
Separate the skin before and after tanning
The matn says the sale of dead animal skin is invalid before tanning, but after tanning it is valid. Note that tanning (dabgh) purifies the skin.
This applies to any dead animal whose skin can be tanned, except pigs and dogs? Actually, the passage later mentions dog skin is also valid after tanning.
2
Identify the always-pure parts
Bones, sinews, wool, hair, horn, fur are listed as pure. The reason: they have no life for death to affect.
These can be sold and used without any treatment.
3
Understand the rationale
Death only affects the locus of life (blood and flesh). Solid parts remain unaffected, hence pure.
This is a key principle: al-mawt lā yaḥill illā fī maḥall al-ḥayāh.
4
Note the inter-madhhab dispute
Imam al-Shāfi'ī and one narration from Aḥmad consider all parts of a dead animal impure. Imām Mālik distinguishes: bone impure, hair pure.
Hanafi position is that these parts are pure.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Hanafis can sell and use these parts; Shafi'is cannot.
This affects trade, e.g., selling wool from a dead sheep.

(۲)

[٤] قوله : لأن ... الخ ؛ حاصله : أنها طاهرة؛ إذ لا حياة فيها، حتى يحلّها الموت، فإنَّ الموت لا يحل إلا في محل الحياة، وعند الشافعي : لا يجوز بيعها ولا الانتفاع بها ؛ لأنها من أجزاء الميتة، وبه قال أحمد الله في رواية.
وعند مالك : عظم الميتة نجس دون شعرها، وكذلك يجوز عندنا بيع جلد الكلب مدبوغاً، خلافاً للشافعي لله والحسن ، صرح به العلامة العيني في «شرح
And not human hair, for its sale is invalid, and not benefiting from it. And not the skin of a dead animal before its tanning, for its sale is invalid. And its sale and benefiting from it after it (tanning) are valid, like its bones, its sinews, its wool, its hair, its horn, and its fur. For the sale of these things is valid, and likewise benefiting from them—because death does not take effect in these things. And in its gloss by al-Tahawi: '...due to being free from need for it because of awls and needles.' He said in al-Bahr: 'The apparent content of his speech is to prohibit benefiting in the absence of necessity, if sewing can be done with something else.' End. [1] He said: It is invalid because the human being is honored, not demeaned, so it is not permissible for any of his parts to be treated with contempt and degradation. As in al-Hidayah. [2] He said: And not benefiting from it; and it is narrated from Muhammad that he permitted benefiting from it, using as evidence that when he (the Prophet) shaved his head, he distributed his hair among his companions, and they sought blessing through it, as is not hidden from whoever studies books of biography and hadith. So if benefiting were not permitted, he would not have done that. And Allah knows best. [3] He said: It is invalid because it is not used, and it is not property due to its impurity, so the sale is invalid; unlike a impure garment, for its impurity is accidental. [4] He said: Because...; its summary is that they are pure, for there is no life in them for death to affect; death only affects the locus of life. According to al-Shafi'i, it is not permissible to sell them nor benefit from them because they are parts of a dead animal. And Ahmad said the same in one narration. According to Malik, the bone of a dead animal is impure but its hair is not. Similarly, it is permissible according to us to sell the skin of a dog after tanning, contrary to al-Shafi'i and al-Hasan. This was stated explicitly by al-'Allamah al-'Ayni in 'Sharh...
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement followed by commentary. The main matn prohibits the sale and use of human hair, and the skin of a dead animal before tanning. After tanning, the skin and other parts like bones, sinews, wool, hair, horn, and fur become pure and their sale and use are valid. The commentary explains the reasons: human dignity prohibits using human parts; the impurity of dead animal skin before tanning prevents its sale; after tanning it becomes pure. For bones and such, because they have no life, death does not affect them, so they remain pure. This is the Hanafi position and differs from Shafi'is who consider all parts of a dead animal impure. The text also includes a narration from Imam Muhammad that human hair can be used based on the Prophet distributing his hair. However, the relied-upon position is that human hair cannot be sold or used due to respect for humans. The passage also mentions that dog skin after tanning is permissible to sell, which is a Hanafi ruling contrary to Shafi'i.

Key Terms
شعر الآدمي (sha'r al-adami): Human hair; considered to be treated with dignity; cannot be sold or used for any purpose.
جلد الميتة (jild al-maytah): Skin of a dead animal (not slaughtered properly); impure before tanning, pure after.
دبغ (dabgh): Tanning; a process that purifies the skin of a dead animal, making it permissible to use and sell.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on selling the wool of a dead animal?
A) It is invalid because it is part of a dead animal.
B) It is valid because wool has no life and is pure.
C) It is valid only after tanning.
D) It is invalid because it is not a commodity.

Key Takeaway
Solid parts of a dead animal (bones, wool, etc.) are pure and can be sold/used, while skin requires tanning to become pure.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Rulings on Selling Human Hair and Carcass Parts
Ruling with proof on sale of human hair and carcass parts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the sale of human hair and carcass skin, and explain the distinction between living and non-living parts of a dead animal.

Core Idea
Human hair cannot be sold; carcass skin becomes sellable after tanning; bones and wool are unaffected by death.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah addresses a set of related commercial and purity rulings: the prohibition of selling human hair, the invalidity of selling untanned carcass skin, and the permissibility of selling bones, wool, and similar parts. The author provides proof and engages with views of Muhammad al-Shaybani and other schools. Understanding these distinctions is essential for correct application in trade and daily use.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their rulings
List the items mentioned: human hair, carcass skin before tanning, carcass skin after tanning, bones, sinews, wool, hair, horns, camel hair. For each, determine the hukm: permissible to sell/use or not.
The text expressly states 'invalid' for human hair and untanned skin, and 'valid' for the rest after tanning.
2
Examine the rationale for human hair prohibition
The text says: 'because the human is honored, not degraded'. Understand that human body parts are not to be traded or demeaned. Consider the intra-Hanafi difference: Muhammad al-Shaybani reportedly allowed benefiting from hair, citing the Prophet distributing his hair.
The relied-upon position follows Abu Hanifa's view of prohibition.
3
Analyze the carcass skin ruling and the effect of tanning
Untanned skin is not property (due to impurity) so sale is void. After tanning, it becomes pure and sale is valid. Note that tanning changes the legal status.
The comparison with a impure garment: its impurity is incidental, not intrinsic, so it can be purified and sold.
4
Understand why bones, wool, etc. are unaffected by death
These parts lack life; death only affects living tissues. Hence they remain pure and can be sold without prior tanning (though washing may be needed). The text uses this to refute Shafi'i's blanket prohibition.
Maliki position: bones impure, hair pure. Hanafi: both pure.
5
Map the cross-madhhab disputes
Summarize the positions: Shafi'i prohibits all parts of a carcass; Maliki distinguishes bone from hair; Hanafis allow all non-living parts. Note the practical consequence for trade.
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi.

(۲)

And not the hair of a human, and not benefiting from it, and not the skin of a carcass before tanning it, and indeed valid is its sale and benefiting from it after it, like its bone, its sinew, its wool, its hair, its horn, and its camel hair. ... [1] His statement: invalid; because the human is honored not degraded, so it is not permissible that any of his parts be disgraced and degraded. Thus in al-Hidayah. [2] His statement: and not benefiting from it; and it is narrated from Muhammad (may Allah be pleased with him) that it is permissible to benefit from it, using as evidence what is narrated that he (the Prophet) when he shaved his head divided his hair among his companions and they would seek blessings through it... [3] His statement: invalid; except that it is not benefited from, and it is not property due to its impurity, so it is void, unlike a impure garment, for its impurity is incidental. [4] His statement: because... etc.; its summary: they are pure because there is no life in them, such that death could affect them, for death only affects that which has life. According to al-Shafi'i: it is not permissible to sell them nor benefit from them because they are parts of the carcass, and Ahmad (may Allah be pleased with him) said the same in one narration. According to Malik: the bone of a carcass is impure but not its hair. Likewise, it is permissible according to us to sell the skin of a dog after tanning, contrary to al-Shafi'i and al-Hasan, as stated by al-Allama al-Ayni in Sharh
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proofs and inter-madhhab disputes on the sale and use of human hair and parts from dead animals. The core ruling: human hair cannot be sold or used because humans are honored and their body parts are not to be demeaned. A report from Muhammad al-Shaybani suggests permissibility citing the Prophet distributing his hair, but the relied-upon Hanafi position maintains prohibition. Carcass skin before tanning is impure and cannot be sold or used; after tanning it becomes pure and its sale is valid. Parts like bones, sinews, wool, hair, horns, and camel hair are not affected by death because they lack life; thus they can be sold and used after tanning. The text discusses objections from other schools: Shafi'i considers all parts of a carcass impure; Maliki considers bones impure but not hair. The Hanafi position is based on the principle that death only affects living tissues. The commentary also cites al-Tahtawi on the necessity of using needles and awls, implying that using such items (like leather) is only prohibited if one can do without them. The passage concludes with a reference to al-Ayni's treatise.

Key Terms
جلد الميتة (jild al-maytah): The skin of an animal that died without being slaughtered properly (carrion).
الدبغ (al-dibgh): The process of tanning animal skin using specific substances to make it pure and usable.
الانتفاع (al-intifaa'): Benefiting from or using something for a permissible purpose.
الميتة (al-maytah): A dead animal that has not been slaughtered according to Shariah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on selling the bone of a dead animal that was not slaughtered?
A) It is impermissible because it is part of a carcass.
B) It is permissible because death does not affect bones.
C) It is permissible only after tanning the bone.
D) It is makruh because of impurity.

Key Takeaway
Human hair cannot be sold or used because of human dignity; carcass skin becomes permissible after tanning; bones, wool, and similar parts are unaffected by death and can be traded.

Sale of Elephant Bones: Qiyas and Intra-Madhhab Dispute
Ruling on sale of elephant bones with qiyas and intra-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling elephant bones and evaluate the intra-madhhab objection of Muhammad al-Shaybani using qiyas.

Core Idea
Elephant bones are sellable like predator bones; Muhammad's analogy to pig is rejected.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on the sale of elephant bones. The passage presents a clear qiyas (analogy) to other predatory animals (siba'), and an intra-madhhab disagreement with Muhammad al-Shaybani. You will learn the legal reasoning behind the majority view and the importance of identifying the correct effective cause (illa) in qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being sold? Elephant bones. State the ruling: valid sale (sahih).
The matn explicitly says 'sold'.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is qiyas (analogy) to bones of other predators (siba').
Commentary says 'benefited from in reality and legally'.
3
Trace the qiyas
Asl: predator bones; hukm: permissible to sell and use; illa: beneficial and not impure. Far: elephant bones. Hukm: permissible.
The illa is extracted via tahqiq al-manat.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Muhammad al-Shaybani opposes, analogizing elephant to pig (impure).
Intra-Hanafi disagreement.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold the majority view, which is mufta bihi. The Hanafi response: elephant is a predator, not a pig.
The objection is rebutted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والفيل كالسبع يباع عظمه خلافاً لمحمد الله، ولا بيع علو بعد سقوطه، وبيع شخص على أنه أمة وهو عبد
[۱] والفيل كالسبع يباع عظمه خلافاً لمحمد )، حتى يجوز بيع عظمِهِ والانتفاع بعظمِهِ خلافا لمحمد له ، فإنَّه كالخنزير عنده.
(ولا بيع علو بعد سقوطه) : حتَّى إذا كان العلو لرجل، والسفل لرجل فسقطا، أو سقط العلو وحدَهُ ، فباعَ صاحب العلوِّ علوه وحده بطل بيعه ; إذ بعد السقوط لم يبق إلا حقُّ التَّعَلِّي، وهو ليس بمال.
وبيع شخص على أنه أمة وهو عبد) : فإنَّ البيع باطل بخلاف ما إذا اشترى كبشاً فإذا هو نعجة، فإنَّ البيعَ يَنْعَقِدُ ، وللمشتري الخيار، والأصل في ذلك أن الإشارة والتسمية إذا اجتمعتا، ففي مختلفي الجنس يتعلق بالمُسَمَّى
(1) الكنز).
11 ] قوله : كالسبع ; لأنَّه منتفع به حقيقةً، وكان منتفعا به شرعاً، اعتباراً لسائر السباع. كذا في «الكفاية»(۲).
[٢] قوله : وهو ليس بمال ; لأنّ حق التعلّي يتعلق بالهواء، والهواء ليس بمال ; لأنَّ المالَ ما يمكن قبضُهُ وإحرازه ، وإنّما يجوز البيع قبل الانهدام، باعتبار البناء القائم، ولم يبق. كذا في «البناية»(۳).
[٣] قوله : يتعلق بالمسمّى ; لأنَّ التَّسمية أبلغ في التعريف من الإشارة ; لأنَّ الإشارة لتعريف الذات، فإنه إذا قال: هذا. صارت الذات متعينة، والتسمية لإعلام الماهية، وأنه أمر زائد على أصل الذات، فكان أبلغ في التعريف، ونحن نحتاج في مقام التعريف إلى ما هو أبلغ تعريفاً ، فلذلك علقنا الحكم بالمسمى، دون المشار إليه. وأما إذا كان المشار إليه من جنس المسمى كانت العبرة للإشارة ; لأنَّ ما سُمّي وجد في المشار إليه، فصار حق التسمية مقضيّاً بالمشار إليه. كذا في «النهاية»).
And the elephant is like a predator; its bone is sold contrary to Muhammad. And there is no sale of a height after its collapse. And the sale of a person as if she is a female slave while he is a male slave. ... [1] And the elephant is like a predator, its bone is sold contrary to Muhammad; so it is permissible to sell its bone and benefit from its bone, contrary to Muhammad, may Allah be pleased with him, for it is like a pig according to him. (And no sale of a height after its collapse): such that if the upper floor belongs to a man and the lower floor to a man, and they collapse, or the upper floor collapses alone, and the owner of the upper floor sells his upper floor alone, his sale becomes void; because after collapse only the right of ascendancy remains, and that is not property. (And the sale of a person as if she is a female slave while he is a male slave): then the sale is void, unlike if one buys a ram and it turns out to be a ewe, then the sale is concluded and the buyer has an option. The principle in this is that when indication and naming come together, in cases of different genders the ruling attaches to the named. ... [1] His statement: like a predator; because it is benefited from in reality, and it was benefited from legally, by analogy with other predators. Thus in al-Kifaya. [2] His statement: and it is not property; because the right of ascendancy pertains to air, and air is not property, because property is what can be taken possession of and stored. The sale is only permissible before collapse by virtue of the standing building, and it no longer remains. Thus in al-Binaya. [3] His statement: attaches to the named; because naming is more effective in identification than indication; because indication is for identifying the entity, while naming conveys the essence, which is additional to the entity itself. So it is more effective in identification. And we need, in the context of identification, what is more effective in identifying, therefore we attach the ruling to the named, not the indicated. But if the indicated is of the same kind as the named, then consideration is given to the indication, because what was named is found in the indicated, so the right of naming is fulfilled by the indicated. Thus in al-Nihaya.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage presents three distinct rulings from Hanafi fiqh on sales. First, elephant bones: Hanafis consider elephants like predators (e.g., lion, wolf) whose bones can be sold and used, because benefit can be derived from them. Muhammad al-Shaybani dissents, comparing elephant bones to pig (impure). Second, sale of a height (upper floor) after collapse: if a building collapses, the right to rebuild (haqq al-ta'ali) remains but is not property; selling it alone is invalid because air is not property. Before collapse, the building is property. Third, misdescription of a slave: if someone sells a person described as a female slave but the person is actually a male slave, the sale is void because of fundamental error. This contrasts with a ram-e we mistake where the sale is valid with option. The principle: when both physical indication (ishara) and verbal description (tasmiya) are used, and they conflict in gender/species, the description takes precedence because it is more specific. If the indicated item matches the description, then indication prevails. These rulings illustrate the Hanafi attention to property definition and contractual precision.

Key Terms
فيل (fil): Elephant
سبع (sabu'): Predatory animal (e.g., lion, wolf)
عظم (azm): Bone

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school (majority), what is the ruling on selling elephant bones?
A) Void because elephant is impure
B) Valid by qiyas to bones of other predators
C) Valid only if the elephant is dead and bones are cleaned
D) Makruh tahrimi

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits selling elephant bones via qiyas to other predators, rejecting analogies to impurity.

6 min
Sale of a Collapsed Height: Why the Right to Rebuild Is Not Property
Ruling on sale of collapsed upper floor; definition of mal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the sale of a height (upper floor) after collapse is void under Hanafi law, and distinguish property from a mere right to rebuild.

Core Idea
After collapse, only a right to rebuild remains, which is not property; the sale is void.

This lesson covers the Hanafi ruling on selling the upper floor (علو) of a building after it has collapsed. The key distinction is between the physical building (which is property) and the right to rebuild in the same airspace (which is not property). You will learn why such a sale is void and how Hanafi fiqh defines property (mal).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Selling the upper floor (al-uluww) after its collapse. Ruling: void (batil).
The matn says 'no sale of a height after its collapse'.
2
Understand the reason
After collapse, only the right to rebuild (haqq al-ta'ali) remains.
The right to rebuild is not property.
3
Define property (mal)
Mal is what can be possessed (qabd) and stored (ihraz). Air cannot be possessed.
Commentary from al-Binaya.
4
Distinguish before vs. after collapse
Before collapse, the building is property; sale is valid. After collapse, only airspace remains.
The building no longer exists.
5
Apply the ruling
Any sale of the height alone after collapse is batil.
If both floors collapse, each owner retains only a right to rebuild, not property.

[۱] والفيل كالسبع يباع عظمه خلافاً لمحمد )، حتى يجوز بيع عظمِهِ والانتفاع بعظمِهِ خلافا لمحمد له ، فإنَّه كالخنزير عنده.
(ولا بيع علو بعد سقوطه) : حتَّى إذا كان العلو لرجل، والسفل لرجل فسقطا، أو سقط العلو وحدَهُ ، فباعَ صاحب العلوِّ علوه وحده بطل بيعه ; إذ بعد السقوط لم يبق إلا حقُّ التَّعَلِّي، وهو ليس بمال.
وبيع شخص على أنه أمة وهو عبد) : فإنَّ البيع باطل بخلاف ما إذا اشترى كبشاً فإذا هو نعجة، فإنَّ البيعَ يَنْعَقِدُ ، وللمشتري الخيار، والأصل في ذلك أن الإشارة والتسمية إذا اجتمعتا، ففي مختلفي الجنس يتعلق بالمُسَمَّى
(1) الكنز).
11 ] قوله : كالسبع ; لأنَّه منتفع به حقيقةً، وكان منتفعا به شرعاً، اعتباراً لسائر السباع. كذا في «الكفاية»(۲).
[٢] قوله : وهو ليس بمال ; لأنّ حق التعلّي يتعلق بالهواء، والهواء ليس بمال ; لأنَّ المالَ ما يمكن قبضُهُ وإحرازه ، وإنّما يجوز البيع قبل الانهدام، باعتبار البناء القائم، ولم يبق. كذا في «البناية»(۳).
[٣] قوله : يتعلق بالمسمّى ; لأنَّ التَّسمية أبلغ في التعريف من الإشارة ; لأنَّ الإشارة لتعريف الذات، فإنه إذا قال: هذا. صارت الذات متعينة، والتسمية لإعلام الماهية، وأنه أمر زائد على أصل الذات، فكان أبلغ في التعريف، ونحن نحتاج في مقام التعريف إلى ما هو أبلغ تعريفاً ، فلذلك علقنا الحكم بالمسمى، دون المشار إليه. وأما إذا كان المشار إليه من جنس المسمى كانت العبرة للإشارة ; لأنَّ ما سُمّي وجد في المشار إليه، فصار حق التسمية مقضيّاً بالمشار إليه. كذا في «النهاية»).
And the elephant is like a predator; its bone is sold contrary to Muhammad. And there is no sale of a height after its collapse. And the sale of a person as if she is a female slave while he is a male slave. ... [1] And the elephant is like a predator, its bone is sold contrary to Muhammad; so it is permissible to sell its bone and benefit from its bone, contrary to Muhammad, may Allah be pleased with him, for it is like a pig according to him. (And no sale of a height after its collapse): such that if the upper floor belongs to a man and the lower floor to a man, and they collapse, or the upper floor collapses alone, and the owner of the upper floor sells his upper floor alone, his sale becomes void; because after collapse only the right of ascendancy remains, and that is not property. (And the sale of a person as if she is a female slave while he is a male slave): then the sale is void, unlike if one buys a ram and it turns out to be a ewe, then the sale is concluded and the buyer has an option. The principle in this is that when indication and naming come together, in cases of different genders the ruling attaches to the named. ... [1] His statement: like a predator; because it is benefited from in reality, and it was benefited from legally, by analogy with other predators. Thus in al-Kifaya. [2] His statement: and it is not property; because the right of ascendancy pertains to air, and air is not property, because property is what can be taken possession of and stored. The sale is only permissible before collapse by virtue of the standing building, and it no longer remains. Thus in al-Binaya. [3] His statement: attaches to the named; because naming is more effective in identification than indication; because indication is for identifying the entity, while naming conveys the essence, which is additional to the entity itself. So it is more effective in identification. And we need, in the context of identification, what is more effective in identifying, therefore we attach the ruling to the named, not the indicated. But if the indicated is of the same kind as the named, then consideration is given to the indication, because what was named is found in the indicated, so the right of naming is fulfilled by the indicated. Thus in al-Nihaya.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage presents three distinct rulings from Hanafi fiqh on sales. First, elephant bones: Hanafis consider elephants like predators (e.g., lion, wolf) whose bones can be sold and used, because benefit can be derived from them. Muhammad al-Shaybani dissents, comparing elephant bones to pig (impure). Second, sale of a height (upper floor) after collapse: if a building collapses, the right to rebuild (haqq al-ta'ali) remains but is not property; selling it alone is invalid because air is not property. Before collapse, the building is property. Third, misdescription of a slave: if someone sells a person described as a female slave but the person is actually a male slave, the sale is void because of fundamental error. This contrasts with a ram-e we mistake where the sale is valid with option. The principle: when both physical indication (ishara) and verbal description (tasmiya) are used, and they conflict in gender/species, the description takes precedence because it is more specific. If the indicated item matches the description, then indication prevails. These rulings illustrate the Hanafi attention to property definition and contractual precision.

Key Terms
العلو (al-'uluww): The upper floor or storey of a building.
حق التعلّي (haqq al-ta'ali): The right to ascend or rebuild on the same airspace after a collapse.
مال (mal): Property; what can be taken possession of and stored.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is the sale of a collapsed height (al-uluww) void?
A) Because the seller no longer owns the land
B) Because only the right to rebuild remains, which is not property
C) Because the buyer cannot inspect the property
D) Because the sale must include both floors

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the right to rebuild after collapse is not property; selling it alone is a void transaction.

Misdescribed Slave: The Principle of Indication vs. Naming
Ruling on misdescribed slave with principle of ishara vs. tasmiya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that when physical indication and verbal naming conflict in a sale, the naming prevails, and distinguish cases where the item belongs to a different genus.

Core Idea
When indication and naming conflict across genres, the naming prevails because it conveys essence.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on a sale where the subject is misdescribed: the seller says 'she is a female slave' but the person is actually a male. The passage establishes a key contractual principle: when physical indication (ishara) and verbal description (tasmiya) conflict, the description takes precedence if they refer to different genders (genus). You will also learn the contrast with a similar case involving a ram and a ewe.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Selling a person described as a female slave (أمة) but actually a male slave (عبد). Ruling: sale is void (batil).
The matn explicitly says 'the sale is void'.
2
Understand the contrast case
If one buys a ram (كبش) and it turns out to be a ewe (نعجة), the sale is valid with an option.
Different genus? Actually, both are sheep; ram is male, ewe is female within same species.
3
Extract the general principle
When ishara (pointing) and tasmiya (naming) are both used, and they conflict in different genders (jins), the ruling attaches to the named (musamma).
Naming is more precise in definition.
4
Apply the principle to the slave case
Here, the naming says 'female slave' but indication points to a male. Since they differ in gender (different jins?), naming prevails, and since the named does not match the indicated, sale is void.
The named (female slave) does not exist.
5
Contrast with ram/ewe case
If the indicated item matches the named in genus (both sheep), then the indication fulfills the naming, and the sale is valid with option.
Same species, different sex.

Elephant Bones in Sale: The Hanafi Intra-Madhhab Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on elephant bone sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on selling elephant bones and explain the qiyas used by the majority and by Imam Muhammad.

Core Idea
Elephant bones are like predator bones, not pig bones — benefit makes them saleable.

The sale of elephant bones presents an interesting intra-Hanafi dispute. While the majority of Hanafi imams permit it based on analogy to other predatory animals, Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani prohibits it by comparing the elephant to a pig. Understanding the basis of each qiyas reveals how the school determines benefit and impurity in commercial transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and the dispute
State the ruling of the majority Hanafis (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf) and the opposing view of Imam Muhammad.
The text says 'the elephant is like a predator, its bone is sold' — this is the majority position.
2
Analyze the majority's qiyas
Determine the asl (predator), far' (elephant), and illa (benefit) in the majority's analogy.
The benefit is both real (haqiqi) and legal (shar'i) by analogy to other predators.
3
Analyze Muhammad's counter-qiyas
Identify the asl (pig), far' (elephant), and illa (lack of lawful benefit) in Muhammad's analogy.
Muhammad considers the elephant like a pig because it is not normally hunted for benefit.
4
Compare the two analogies
Explain why the Hanafi response rejects the pig analogy — the elephant is a beast of prey with practical uses, and its bones are not impure.
The key difference: pig is najis and has no lawful benefit; elephant bones are used for carving, etc.
5
Determine the practical consequence
State the thamarah al-khilaf: the majority permits sale and use; Muhammad's view prohibits them.
The mufta bihi position is the majority view; elephant bone trade is valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والفيل كالسبع يباع عظمه خلافاً لمحمد الله، حتى يجوز بيع عظمِهِ والانتفاع بعظمِهِ خلافا لمحمد له، فإنَّه كالخنزير عنده. قوله: كالسبع؛ لأنَّه منتفع به حقيقةً، وكان منتفعا به شرعاً، اعتباراً لسائر السباع. كذا في «الكفاية».
The elephant is like a predator, its bone is sold contrary to Muhammad. So selling its bone and benefiting from its bone is permissible, contrary to Muhammad, for he considers it like a pig. His saying: like a predator because it is benefited from in reality and was benefited from legally by analogy to other predators. As in al-Kifayah.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage addresses the permissibility of selling elephant bones. The ruling compares elephants to predators (siba') such as lions, whose bones are permissible to sell and use. Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani disagreed, analogizing elephant bones to pig bones due to the elephant's bulk and lack of similar benefit. However, the majority Hanafi position is that elephants are beasts of prey whose bones have lawful benefit (e.g., for carving or medicinal use), and thus their sale is valid. The commentary from al-Kifayah explains that benefit is both real and legally recognized by analogy to other predators.

Key Terms
السبع (al-sabu'): A predatory animal that attacks others, such as a lion, tiger, or wolf.
انتفاع (intifa'): Benefit or utility derived from a thing, which is a condition for permissibility of sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the elephant is a predator and its bones are benefited from
B) Because Muhammad al-Shaybani allowed it
C) Because the elephant is like a pig in benefit
D) Because bones are always permissible regardless of the animal

Key Takeaway
Elephant bones are permissible to sell and use because they have lawful benefit, analogized to other predator bones, not to pig bones.

Sale of Fallen Upper Floor Invalid
Ruling that sale of an upper floor after collapse is invalid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state why the Hanafi school voids the sale of an upper floor after its collapse and identify the distinction between tangible property and mere rights.

Core Idea
After collapse, only an air-right remains, which is not property — so sale is void.

In Hanafi property law, the object of sale must be a tangible asset (mal) that can be possessed and stored. This lesson examines the case of an upper floor that collapses: what remains is only a right to rebuild, which does not meet the definition of property. The text from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies the ruling and its reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being sold? An upper floor that has collapsed. What is the ruling? The sale is void (batil).
2
Analyze the reason for voidness
After collapse, only a right of elevation (haqq al-ta'alli) remains. This right is not considered property (mal) in Hanafi law.
The rationale: mal must be tangible and capable of possession.
3
Understand the definition of mal
Property is defined as that which can be possessed and stored (ma yumkinu qabdhu wa ihrazuhu). Air rights cannot be possessed.
Reference to al-Binayah: 'mal is what can be possessed and stored.'
4
Distinguish before and after collapse
Before collapse, the upper floor is a tangible building, so sale is valid. After collapse, only a right remains.
The contrast highlights the condition that the sold item must exist as a physical asset.
5
Note the authority
The text cites al-Binayah as support. This is a standard Hanafi reference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا بيع علو بعد سقوطه: حتَّى إذا كان العلو لرجل، والسفل لرجل فسقطا، أو سقط العلو وحدَهُ، فباعَ صاحب العلوِّ علوه وحده بطل بيعه؛ إذ بعد السقوط لم يبق إلا حقُّ التَّعَلِّي، وهو ليس بمال. قوله: وهو ليس بمال؛ لأنّ حق التعلّي يتعلق بالهواء، والهواء ليس بمال؛ لأنَّ المالَ ما يمكن قبضُهُ وإحرازه، وإنّما يجوز البيع قبل الانهدام، باعتبار البناء القائم، ولم يبق. كذا في «البناية».
And no sale of the upper floor after its collapse: So if the upper floor belongs to a man and the lower floor to another man, and both collapse, or only the upper floor collapses, and the owner of the upper floor sells his upper floor alone, his sale is void; for after collapse nothing remains except the right of elevation (haqq al-ta'alli), which is not property (mal). His saying: It is not property because the right of elevation pertains to air, and air is not property; for property is what can be possessed and stored. Sale before collapse was permissible based on the standing building, but it no longer exists. As in al-Binayah.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This ruling states that after the upper floor (aluw) of a building collapses, it cannot be sold separately. Before collapse, the floor is a tangible object that can be sold. After collapse, only a right to rebuild (haqq al-ta'alli) remains, which is attached to the airspace. Since air cannot be physically possessed or stored, it is not considered mal (property) under Hanafi law. Therefore, the sale is void (batil). The same applies if both upper and lower floors collapse. This ruling contrasts with the permissibility of selling the upper floor before collapse, which was based on the existing structure.

Key Terms
العلو (al-'uluww): The upper floor of a building that can be owned separately from the lower floor.
حق التعلّي (haqq al-ta'alli): The legal right to build or elevate above the ground, which is not considered property (mal).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the sale of an upper floor void after it collapses?
A) Because the collapse was caused by negligence
B) Because only the right of elevation remains, which is not considered property
C) Because the lower floor owner owns the airspace
D) Because the sale contract was not properly witnessed

Key Takeaway
The sale of an upper floor is void after collapse because only a right of elevation remains, which is not considered property (mal) in Hanafi law.

Sale with Conflict Between Pointing and Naming: Different Species
Ruling on sale with conflict between ishara and tasmiya; proof from qiyas to ram/ewe case

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when pointing (ishara) and naming (tasmiya) conflict in a sale, and distinguish between cases of same and different species (jins) with the associated consequences.

Core Idea
When pointing and naming conflict, naming prevails if species differ, voiding sale; otherwise pointing prevails, sale stands with buyer's option.

In a sale contract, the seller may both point to an object and describe it. What happens when the description does not match the actual object? This passage from the Hanafi tradition tackles that question, focusing on the critical distinction of whether the error involves a different species (jins) or merely a different attribute within the same species. Understanding this principle is key to mastering sales law in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the specific transaction? A person is sold as a female slave but found to be male. The ruling stated is that the sale is void (batil).
This is the core case of the passage.
2
Locate the proof (dalil)
The proof is a qiyas (analogy) to the case of buying a ram and finding a ewe. The source text contrasts the two scenarios.
The ram/ewe case results in a valid sale with option, not void.
3
Analyze the underlying principle
The distinction hinges on the interaction between ishara (pointing) and tasmiya (naming). When species (jins) are different, the naming governs and the sale fails because the named item does not exist. When species are the same, the pointing governs and the sale is valid but the buyer has an option (khiyar) for the missing description.
This is the legal basis (wajh al-dalalah) of the qiyas.
4
Understand the species distinction
For humans, male and female are considered two separate species (jinsan) due to extreme disparity in purpose and utility. For non-humans, they are one species.
This is the key factor: fahsh al-tafawut (extreme disparity).
5
Determine the practical consequence
Apply the principle: if you buy a 'female slave' and receive a male, the sale is void. If you buy a 'ram' and receive a ewe, the sale is valid with option.
The option (khiyar) allows the buyer to cancel the sale.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبيع شخص على أنه أمة وهو عبد: فإنَّ البيع باطل بخلاف ما إذا اشترى كبشاً فإذا هو نعجة، فإنَّ البيعَ يَنْعَقِدُ، وللمشتري الخيار، والأصل في ذلك أن الإشارة والتسمية إذا اجتمعتا، ففي مختلفي الجنس يتعلق بالمُسَمَّى. ويبطل لانعدام المُسَمَّى، وفي متحدي الجنس يتعلق بالمشار إليه، ويَنْعَقِدُ لوجودِ المشار إليه، لكن المشتري بالخيار؛ لفوات الوصف، فالذَّكَرُ والأُنْثَى في بني آدم جنسان؛ لفحش التَّفاوت، والاختلاف في الأغراض، وفي غير بني آدم جنس واحد. [1] يتعلق بالمشار إليه؛ لأنّ الجمع بين الإشارة والتسمية هناك ممكن، بأن يجعل الإشارة للتعريف والتَّسمية للترغيب، حتى لو قال: بعت هذا العبد الخباز، فإذا هو ليس بخباز، كان البيع صحيحاً، وكان الوصف للترغيب، وإذا كان بخلاف جنسِ المسمى يتعلق العقد بالمُسَمّى؛ لأنَّ العقد يبتني على المسمّى، وهو المقصود، والبناء على المقصودِ واجب. كذا في «الكفاية»، نقلا عن فخرِ الإسلام. [2] بالخيار؛ كما إذا اشترى عبداً على أنه خبّاز، فإذا هو كاتب حيث ينعقد البيع، ويتخيرُ المشتري لفوات الوصف، وذكر في «الظهيرية»: ويثبت الخيار إذا كان الموجودُ أنقص، وذكر صاحب «المحيط»: إنَّ للمشتري الخيار من غير تقيد بكونه أنقص، وهو الصحيح؛ لفوات المقصود، والمدلول عليه بالاشتراط. كذا في «الكفاية». [3] لفحش التفاوت؛ فإنَّ المبتغى من العبدِ الاستخدام خارج البيت كالتجارة والزراعة وغيرهما، والمبتغى من الأمة الاستخدام داخل الدار كالطبخ والكنس والاستفراش والاستيلاد، والأخيران لا يصح لهما العقد، فظهر التفاوت بينهما فاحشاً. والفُحش بالضم: از حد گذشتن بدى، والتفاحش مثله، يقال: فحش الأمر، وتفاحش. كذا في «الصراح»، وفي «المصباح المنير»: «فحش الشيء فحشاً: مثل قبح قبحاً، وزناً ومعنى، وفي لغة من باب قتل، وهو فاحش، وكلُّ شيء جاوزه الحد فهو فاحش، ومنه غبن فاحش إذا جاوزت الزّيادة ما يعتادُ مثله». انتهى. [4] جنس واحد؛ للتَّقارب في الأغراض؛ لأنَّ الغرض الكلّي من الحيوان
And the sale of a person described as a female slave but he is a male slave: Then the sale is void, unlike if someone bought a ram and it turned out to be a ewe, then the sale is concluded and the buyer has an option. The principle in this is that when pointing (ishara) and naming (tasmiya) come together, in cases of different species (jins) the ruling is attached to the named (musamma). It becomes void due to the absence of the named, while in the same species (mutahid al-jins) the ruling is attached to the pointed-to (mushar ilayh), and it is concluded due to the existence of the pointed-to, but the buyer has an option due to the loss of the description. So male and female among humans are two species due to the extreme disparity and difference in purposes, while in non-humans they are one species. [Commentary sections from the text]
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage explains the Hanafi ruling on a sale where the seller describes an item (e.g., a female slave) but the actual item is different (a male slave). The key principle is the interaction between pointing (ishara) and naming (tasmiya) in a contract. When they conflict, if the pointed-to object and the named description are of different species (jins), the naming prevails and the sale is void because the named item does not exist. If they are of the same species, the pointing prevails, the sale is valid, but the buyer has a choice (khiyar) due to the missing description. For humans, male and female are considered different species because of vastly different intended uses (outside work vs domestic roles). For non-human animals, male and female are one species since the primary purpose (e.g., meat, labor) is similar. The commentary elaborates that when species match, the pointing identifies the object, and the naming is seen as a description for incentive, so the contract stands with an option for the buyer if the description is lacking. This ruling is rooted in the works of Fakhr al-Islam and al-Kifayah.

Key Terms
الإشارة (al-ishara): Pointing to a specific object in a sale contract, e.g., 'I sell this'.
التسمية (al-tasmiya): Naming or describing the object in a sale, e.g., 'a female slave'.
الجنس (al-jins): Species or category; here, humans are divided into two genders (jinsan) for legal purposes due to extreme disparity in utility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone buys a 'female slave' but finds a male slave, what is the ruling according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) The sale is void because the named item (female) does not exist
B) The sale is valid but the buyer has an option to cancel
C) The sale is valid and the buyer must accept because the description was for incentive
D) The sale is suspended until the buyer agrees

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi sales law, when pointing and naming conflict, the naming governs if species differ, voiding the contract; otherwise, the pointing governs, making the sale valid with an option for the buyer.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Gender Misdescription in Sale: Human vs Non-Human
Ruling with proof via qiyas using purposes (aghrad)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule distinguishing human from non-human gender misdescription in sales and explain the khiyar al-wasf ruling.

Core Idea
In Hanafi sale, human genders are separate genera (sale void if misdescribed); animal genders are one genus (sale valid with option).

When you buy an item that is both pointed to and named, a conflict between the two can arise if the description is wrong. This passage clarifies that the legal consequence depends on whether the genus of the misdescribed attribute is the same or different. The Hanafi school uses a unique criterion: the purpose (gharad) of the item, not biology, determines whether male and female are the same genus. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying khiyar al-wasf in sales of humans versus animals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage contrasts two cases: (1) when the named genus differs from the actual genus (e.g., selling a 'slave-girl' that turns out to be male) – sale is void; (2) when the genera are the same but the specific gender differs (e.g., selling a 'ram' that is a ewe) – sale is valid but buyer has option.
2
Understand the 'illa (effective cause)
The 'illa for treating human genders as separate genera is 'gross disparity in purposes' (fuhsh al-tafawut). The text explains that a male slave is used for outdoor work, while a female is used for indoor tasks including intimacy – these purposes differ fundamentally. For animals, purposes converge (eating, riding, carrying), so they are one genus.
3
Apply the khiyar ruling
When the genera are the same, the buyer gets khiyar al-wasf (option due to missing description). The correct view (mufta bihi) is that this option is absolute, not restricted to when the actual attribute is inferior – because the intended purpose is lost regardless.
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute: some restrict khiyar to inferiority, but the majority holds it absolute.
4
Recognize the evidence base
The ruling is derived via qiyas based on consideration of purposes (istihsan). No direct textual evidence is cited; the reasoning is that contracts are built on the intended object (al-musamma), so if the named is absent, the contract is void.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويبطل لانعدام المُسَمَّى ، وفي متحدي الجنس يتعلق بالمشار إليه، ويَنْعَقِدُ لوجودِ المشار إليه، لكن المشتري بالخيار" ؛ لفوات الوصف، فالذَّكَرُ والأُنْثَى في بني آدم جنسان ؛ لفحش التَّفاوت ، والاختلاف في الأغراض، وفي غير بني آدم جنس واحد". [1] قوله : يتعلق بالمشار إليه ؛ لأنّ الجمع بين الإشارة والتسمية هناك ممكن ، بأن يجعل الإشارة للتعريف والتَّسمية للترغيب ، حتى لو قال : بعت هذا العبد الخباز، فإذا هو ليس بخباز، كان البيع صحيحاً ، وكان الوصف للترغيب، وإذا كان بخلاف جنسِ المسمى يتعلق العقد بالمُسمّى ؛ لأنَّ العقد يبتني على المسمّى، وهو المقصود، والبناء على المقصودِ واجب. كذا في «الكفاية» (١) ، نقلا عن فخرِ الإسلام. [٢]قوله : بالخيار ؛ كما إذا اشترى عبداً على أنه خبّاز، فإذا هو كاتب حيث ينعقد البيع، ويتخيرُ المشتري لفوات الوصف، وذكر في «الظهيرية»: ويثبت الخيار إذا كان الموجودُ أنقص، وذكر صاحب «المحيط»: إنَّ للمشتري الخيار من غير تقيد بكونه أنقص، وهو الصحيح ؛ لفوات المقصود، والمدلول عليه بالاشتراط. كذا في «الكفاية»(٢). [٣] قوله : لفحش التفاوت ؛ فإنَّ المبتغى من العبدِ الاستخدام خارج البيت كالتجارة والزراعة وغيرهما، والمبتغى من الأمة الاستخدام داخل الدار كالطبخ والكنس والاستفراش والاستيلاد، والأخيران لا يصح لهما العقد، فظهر التفاوت بينهما فاحشاً. والفُحش بالضم: از حد گذشتن بدى ، والتفاحش مثله ، يقال : فحش الأمر،. وتفاحش. كذا في «الصراح»، وفي «المصباح المنير»: «فحش الشيء فحشاً : مثل قبح قبحاً، وزناً ومعنى، وفي لغة من باب قتل ، وهو فاحش، وكلُّ شيء جاوزه الحد فهو فاحش، ومنه غبن فاحش إذا جاوزت الزّيادة ما يعتادُ مثله». انتهى (۳) [٤]قوله : جنس واحد ؛ للتَّقارب في الأغراض ؛ لأنَّ الغرض الكلّي من الحيوانِ الأكل والركوب والحمل، والذكر والأنثى في ذلك سواء، والمعتبر في أنهما جنسان مختلفان أو متحدان تفاوت الأغراض وتقاربهما ، دون أصل المادة ، فإذا وقعت الإشارة إلى : ذُكِرَ بتسمية، فإن كان ذلك ما يكون الذكر والأنثى منه جنسين : كبني آدم ، مبيع فالعقد يتعلق بالمسمّى، ويبطل بانعدامه. فإذا قال : بعتك هذه الجارية فإذا هو غلام بطل البيع ؛ لفواتِ التّسميةِ التي هم أبلغ في التعريف من الإشارة، وإن كان ما يكون جنساً واحداً، فالعقد يتعلق بالمشار إليه، وينعقد بوجوده؛ لأنّ العبرة حينئذ للإشارة لا للتّسمية ؛ لأن ما سمّي وُجد في المشار إليه. فصار حق التسمية مقضيّاً بالمشار إليه، فإذا باع كبشاً فإذا هو نعجة انعقد البيع، لكنّه يتخيَّر ؛ لفوات الوصف المرغوب. كذا في «العناية» (٢) وغيرها من المبسوطات.
And it is void due to the non-existence of the named (item). And in the case of a single genus, it is attached to the pointed-to (item), and it is concluded due to the existence of the pointed-to (item), but the buyer has the option due to the loss of the description. So the male and female in humans are two genera due to gross disparity and difference in purposes, and in other than humans they are one genus. [1] His saying: 'it is attached to the pointed-to' because combining indication and naming is possible there by making the indication for identification and the naming for encouragement, such that if he said, 'I sold this slave, the baker,' and he is not a baker, the sale is valid, and the description is for encouragement. And if it is contrary to the genus of the named, the contract is attached to the named because the contract is based on the named, which is the intended object, and building on the intended object is obligatory. As in al-Kifaya, transmitted from Fakhr al-Islam. [2] His saying: 'has the option' as if he bought a slave on condition that he is a baker, and he turns out to be a scribe, the sale is concluded and the buyer has the option due to the loss of the description. And it is mentioned in al-Zahiriyya: the option is established if the existing (attribute) is inferior. And the author of al-Muhit mentioned: the buyer has the option without restriction of it being inferior, and that is correct due to the loss of the intended purpose indicated by the stipulation. As in al-Kifaya. [3] His saying: 'due to gross disparity' because what is sought from a male slave is use outside the house like trade, agriculture, etc., and what is sought from a female slave is use inside the house like cooking, sweeping, bedding, and bearing children; the last two are not valid for the contract, so the disparity between them appears gross. Al-Fuhsh with dammah: exceeding the limit of evil, and al-tafahush is similar, it is said: fahasha al-amr and tafahasha. As in al-Sirah, and in al-Misbah al-Munir: 'The thing became fahish (gross) like qabuha, in weight and meaning, and in a dialect from the chapter of qatala, and it is fahish, and everything that exceeds its limit is fahish, including gross overcharging when the increase exceeds what is customary.' End. [4] His saying: 'one genus' due to the closeness of purposes because the overall purpose from animals is eating, riding, and carrying, and the male and female are equal in that. And what is considered in their being two different genera or one is the disparity and closeness of purposes, not the original substance. So when indication occurs with naming, if that (thing) is such that the male and female are two genera – like humans – then the contract is attached to the named and becomes void with its non-existence. So if he says, 'I sold you this slave-girl' and it turns out to be a boy, the sale is void due to the loss of the naming which is stronger in identification than the indication. And if it is one genus, then the contract is attached to the pointed-to and is concluded with its existence because the consideration then is for the indication not the naming, since what was named is found in the pointed-to. So the right of naming becomes fulfilled by the pointed-to. So if he sells a ram and it turns out to be a ewe, the sale is concluded, but he has the option due to the loss of the desirable description. As in al-Inaya and other extended works.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Hanafi school on the effect of gender discrepancy in a sale when the object is identified by both pointing and naming. The base ruling states: if the named genus differs from the pointed-to object, the contract is attached to the named (as per the primary intention), so the sale is void if the named is absent. If the genus is the same (e.g., a male animal sold as a female animal in non-humans), the contract attaches to the pointed-to object and is valid, but the buyer has an option (khiyar) due to the loss of the stipulated description. The commentator expands on this: In humans, male and female are considered separate genera because their purposes (aghrad) differ grossly – a male slave is used for outdoor work, a female for indoor work including sexual intercourse and childbearing, which cannot be contracted with a male. Therefore, selling a slave-girl who turns out to be a boy voids the sale. In non-human animals (e.g., sheep), male and female are one genus because the general purpose (eating, riding, carrying) is the same; thus selling a ram that turns out to be a ewe is valid but the buyer has an option due to the missing desirable quality. The commentator also clarifies that the option is established when the actual attribute is inferior, and the correct view is that the option is general, not restricted to inferiority, because the intended purpose is lost. The passage uses lexical explanations of 'fuhsh' (grossness). This ruling is based on istihsan and the principle that contracts are built on the intended object.

Key Terms
المُسَمَّى (al-musammā): The named item in a contract, i.e., the specific object designated by verbal description.
المشار إليه (al-mushār ilayhi): The pointed-to item in a contract, identified by physical indication (e.g., pointing).
جنس (jins): Genus or species, used here to determine whether male and female are considered the same category for sale purposes.
الخيار (al-khiyār): The option given to the buyer when the purchased item lacks a stipulated quality.
فحش التفاوت (fuhsh al-tafāwut): Gross disparity in purposes, used as the 'illa to differentiate human genders from animal genders.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why are male and female considered separate genera in humans for the purpose of sale according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because of biological differences.
B) Due to gross disparity in their purposes (aghrad) – male used outside, female used inside including marital relations.
C) Because the Prophet ﷺ said so.
D) Because the naming (tasmia) is stronger than indication (ishara).

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi sales law, the determination of whether male and female are the same genus depends on the closeness of their purposes (aghrad), not biology; this leads to void sales for humans and valid sales with khiyar for non-humans.

Seller Repurchasing at Lower Price: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
States the Hanafi ruling on repurchase before payment and the Shafi'i rebuttal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a seller repurchasing his sold item at a lower price before full payment, explain the Shafi'i objection based on qiyas, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
Repurchase at a lower price before payment is fasid to block ribā, despite ownership transfer.

This passage presents a classic inter-madhhab dispute over a seller buying back his sold item at a discount before the original price is fully paid. While the Shafi'i school holds the second sale valid based on ownership via possession, the Hanafi school rules it invalid (fasid) as a preventive measure against usury. Understanding this dispute reveals how the Hanafi school employs istihsan to override qiyas where ethical considerations demand.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The core scenario: a seller sells an item (e.g., a slave-girl) for a price, the buyer takes possession, then the seller buys it back from the buyer at a lower price before receiving the original price in full.
The condition is that the item has not diminished in substance and the two prices are of the same genus.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The second sale is fasid (invalid) according to the Hanafi school. Identify the hukm level and the rationale: preventing a means to ribā.
The Hanafi position is introduced with 'عندنا' indicating it is the mufta bihi.
3
Present the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i holds the second sale is permissible (jā'iz) based on qiyas: ownership was completed by possession, so the buyer may sell to anyone, including the seller.
The text describes the Shafi'i view as 'al-qiyas' (the analogy).
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Hanafi law, the second sale is void, so the buyer retains the item and must pay the original price; under Shafi'i, the seller recovers the item at a lower price, effectively benefiting from the unpaid debt.
This consequence highlights why the Hanafi school sees the transaction as a ribā loophole.
5
Note the conditional exception
If the repurchase is for an equal or higher price, it is permissible because any excess goes to the buyer, eliminating the suspicion of usury.
This exception confirms the rationale: blocking a benefit from the unpaid debt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع البيع الفاسد والمكروه وشراء ما باع بأقل مما باعَ قبل نقدِ ثمنه الأَوَّل [1]قوله : وشراء؛ مرفوع معطوف على قوله : وبيع شخص»؛ أي لا يجوز شراء البائع لنفسه أو لغيره من المشتري ، أو من وكيله أو من وارثه ما باع بثمن حالٍ أو مؤجل بنفسه أو بوكيله بأقل ما باع ، قبل نقد ثمنه الأول : أي كل الثمن الأوَّل أو بعضه، وإن بقي من ثمنه درهم. كما في «السراج الوهاج». وصورة هذه المسألة : أنه باع جارية مثلاً بألف حالة أو نسيئة فقبضها المشتري ، ثمَّ اشتراها البائع من المشتري قبل نقدِ الثمن الأول بالأقلّ، فالبيع الثاني فاسد عندنا ، وعند الشافعي : يجوز، وهو القياس ؛ لأنَّ الملك فيه قد تم بالقبض، فيجوز بيعه (1) أي لا يجوز شراء البائع لنفسه أو لغيره من المشتري أو من وكيله أو من وارثه ما باع بثمن حال أو مؤجل بنفسه أو بوكيله إن كان المبيع لم ينقص ذاته واتحد الثمنان جنسا، وقيد الشراء بكونه بأقل مما باع ؛ لأنه لو كان بمثله أو أكثر منه جاز ؛ لأن الفضل في الأكثر يحصل للمشتري والمبيع داخل في ضمانه. ينظر : فتح باب العناية» (۲ : ۳۳۹). (۲) (العناية» (٦ : ٦٨).
The Book of Sale: Invalid and Disliked Sale. And [the rule of] the seller buying back what he sold for less than what he sold before paying the first price. [1] His saying: 'and buying' – it is raised as a conjunction to his saying: 'and a person selling' i.e., it is not permissible for the seller, for himself or for another, from the buyer, or from his agent or his heir, what he sold for a spot or deferred price, by himself or by his agent, for less than what he sold, before paying his first price – meaning all of the first price or part of it, even if a dirham remains from his price. As in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. The form of this case: he sold, for example, a slave-girl for a thousand in cash or on credit, and the buyer took possession, then the seller bought her from the buyer before paying the first price for less. The second sale is invalid (fāsid) according to us (Hanafis), and according to al-Shafi'i: it is permissible, and that is qiyas because ownership has been completed by possession, so selling it is permissible. (1) i.e., it is not permissible for the seller for himself or for another from the buyer or from his agent or from his heir what he sold for a spot or deferred price by himself or by his agent, provided the sold item has not diminished in its substance and the two prices are of the same genus. And the condition of buying is that it is for less than what he sold; because if it were equal or more, it would be permissible, because the excess would go to the buyer and the sold item is still within his guarantee. See: Fath Bab al-Inaya 2:339. (2) al-Inaya 6:68.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding a seller who buys back the very item he sold at a lower price before he has received the original price. The Hanafi ruling (stated as 'عندنا') is that the second sale is invalid (fāsid). The Shafi'i ruling (stated as 'عند الشافعي') is that it is permissible (jā'iz). The Shafi'i view is described as 'al-qiyas' (the analogy), meaning they see it as the logical analogy: since ownership passed to the buyer upon possession, the buyer can sell it back; the original seller's outstanding right is only a debt, which does not affect the validity of the sale. The Hanafi school, however, prohibits this as a means of blocking a loophole that could lead to usury (ribā) or injustice, because the seller might be indirectly recovering the item while still being owed the price, effectively obtaining a benefit from the unpaid debt. The conditions for this ruling: the item must not have diminished in substance, the two prices must be of the same genus, and the repurchase must be for less than the original price. If it is equal or more, it is permissible because the excess goes to the buyer. The commentator references 'Fath Bab al-Inaya' and 'al-Inaya' for further detail.

Key Terms
ثمن (thaman): The price in a sale; here it refers to the original price (thaman awwal) and the repurchase price.
البيع الفاسد (al-bayʿ al-fāsid): An invalid sale that is not legally binding; in Hanafi Fiqh, a fasid sale is one that is defective but can be corrected by removing the defect; it is different from batil (void).
القَياس (al-qiyās): Analogical reasoning; here the Shafi'i use qiyas to argue the second sale is valid because ownership is complete.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the primary reason for invalidating (fasid) a seller's repurchase of his sold item at a lower price before full payment?
A) The seller does not own the item anymore.
B) It is a means to ribā (usury).
C) The buyer has not yet taken possession.
D) The contract is a trick (hila).

Key Takeaway
A seller repurchasing his sold item at a lower price before the original price is fully paid is invalid (fasid) in the Hanafi school to prevent ribā, while the Shafi'i school considers it valid based on ownership transfer.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
The Core Prohibition: Seller Buying Back at a Lower Price Before Payment
Explaining the prohibition and proof for seller buying back at lower price.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a seller cannot repurchase the sold item at a lower price before the original price is paid, and explain the dalil from the hadith of Aisha.

Core Idea
Seller buying back at a lower price before payment is fasid — the hadith of Aisha declares it nullifies jihad.

This lesson focuses on the central ruling: it is prohibited for a seller to buy back an item at a lower price before receiving the original payment. The proof comes from a well-known hadith of Aisha (RA), which condemns such a transaction with severe warnings. Understanding this ruling requires careful attention to the conditions laid out by our Hanafi masters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act: a seller buys back his sold item from the buyer at a lower price before the original sale price is paid. The ruling: this sale is fasid (invalid) and prohibited.
Fasid means the sale is defective but can be corrected if the deficiency is removed.
2
Examine the hadith evidence
The author cites a hadith where a woman sold a slave girl to Zayd ibn Arqam for 800, then bought her back for 600 before receiving payment. Aisha (RA) said this nullifies Zayd's jihad unless he repents.
The hadith is mawqoof (stopped at Aisha) but accepted as proof by the Hanafis.
3
Understand the rationale
Such a severe warning cannot be given for a permissible act; it indicates the contract is invalid. Reason alone would not attach such consequence to a minor sin; Aisha must have heard it from the Prophet (SAW).
The text argues that the warning proves the prohibition is based on revelation, not mere opinion.
4
Note the essential conditions
(1) The buyback price must be lower than the original sale price. (2) It must be before payment of the original price. (3) The second price must be of the same type (e.g., both cash, same currency). (4) The sold item must be unchanged.
If any condition is missing, the ruling may change.

بأي قدر كان من الثمن، كما إذا باعه من غير البائع، أو منه بمثل الثَّمن للأول أو بأكثر أو بعرض أو بأقل بعد النقد. وإنما منعنا جوازَهُ لما أخرجَهُ عبد الرزاق في «مصنّفه»(۱) : أخبرنا معمر والثوري، عن أبى اسحاق السبيعي عن امرأته أنها دخلت على عائشة رضي الله عنها في نسوة، فسألتها امرأةٌ فقالت: يا أم المؤمنين ؛ كانت لي جارية فبعتُها من زيد بن أرقم بثمانمئة ، ثم ابتعتها منه بستمئة ، فنقدتُه الستمئة وكتب عليه ثمانمئة. فقالت عائشة رضي الله عنها : بئس ما اشتريت وبئس ما اشترى ، أخبري زيد بن أرقم أنه قد أبطل جهاده مع رسول الله له إلا أن يتوب، فقالت المرأة لعائشة : أرأيت إن أخذت رأس مالي، ورددت عليه الفضل فقالت : فَمَن جَاءَهُ مَوْعِظَةٌ مِّن رَّبِّهِ فَانتَهَى ، فله ما سلف (۲) وأخرجه الدارقطني (۳) ، والبَيْهَقِيُّ (4) في «سننهما » : عن يوسف بن أبي اسحاق الله عنها، فأتتها أم محبة، رضي الهمداني عن العالية : قالت : كنت قاعدة عند عائشة فقالت : إنِّي بعتُ زيد بن أرقم جارية إلى عطاء، فذكر بنحوه. والعالية : امرأة معروفة جليلة القدر، ذكرها ابن سعد في «الطبقات»)، فلا تلتفت إلى ما قال بعضُهم (٦) من أنها مجهولة، لا يقبل خبرها ، وإن شئت زيادة التفصيل فارجع إلى «نصب الراية» للعلامة الزَّيْلَعِي. فهذا الوعيد الشديد دليل على فسادِ هذا العقد، وإلحاق هذا الوعيد لهذا الصنع لا يهتدي إليه العقل ؛ إذ شيءٌ من المعاصي دون الكفر لا يبطل شيئاً من الطاعات إلا أن يثبت شيء من ذلك بالوحي ، فدل على أنها قالته سماعاً. والدليل العقلي على عدم جوازه مذكور في الشرح فلا نعيده. وإنما ترك فاعل الشراء ليشمل شراءَ مَن لا تقبل شهادته للبائع كالأصول والفروع ومكاتبه فهو أيضاً بمنزلة شراء البائع عند الإمام الله خلافاً لهما في غير العبدِ والمكاتب، وكذا الحكم لو باعه وكالة عن غيره أو اشتراه بطريق الوكالة لغيره إذا كان هو البائع. ومحل كلامه شراء الكلِّ أو البعض، وخرج شراء وارث البائع ووكيله عند الإمام خلافاً لهما ، وأما شراء البائع ممن اشترى من مشتريه أو الموهوب له، أو الوصي له، فجائز اتِّفاقاً ؛ لأنَّ اختلاف سبب الملك كاختلاف العين. كذا في «مجمع الأنهر»(1) و«كمال الدراية»(۲)، وغيرهما. وقيد بقبوله أقل ؛ لأنه لو اشتراه بمثل ما باع أو بأكثر منه جاز ؛ لأنَّ الربح يحصل للمشتري، والمبيع قد دخل في ضمانه، ويفهم من قوله : «أقل ما باع» ؛ أن يكون الثمن الثاني من جنس الثَّمن الأول، فلو باعه بألف ثمَّ اشتراه من المشتري بثوب قيمته أقل من الألف جاز، ولو باعه بدنانير ثمَّ اشتراه بدراهم أقل قيمةٍ منها قبل نقد الثمن الأول لم يجز عندنا استحساناً، وعند زفر له قياساً. وإنما قال : قبل نقد ثمنه الأوَّل ؛ لأنه لو وقع هذا الشّراءُ بعد نقد ثمنه الأوّل صح اتفاقاً، ولا بُدَّ هاهنا من قيد آخر ، وهو أن يكون المبيع على حاله بحيث لم يتغير ؛ إذ لو تغير لم يكن الحكم كذلك. ولا بُدَّ أيضاً أن يقعَ الشِّراءُ من البائع بنفسه أو وارثه حتى لو باع بنفسه ثمَّ وكُلَ وكيلاً بالشراء بأقل جاز على الموكل عند أبي حنيفة الله ، وعلى الوكيل عند أبي يوسف ه ، وكان للموكل بيع فاسد عند محمد الله ، كذا صرَّحَ به العلامة البرْجَنْدِيُّ في «شرح النقاية»، هذا وإن طال الكلام ، لكن لا يخلو عن الفوائد المفيدة في المرام.
By any amount of the price, such as if he sold it to someone other than the seller, or to him for the same price as the first, or more, or for goods, or less after payment. We have only prohibited its permissibility because of what Abd al-Razzaq narrated in his 'Musannaf' (1): Ma'mar and al-Thawri informed us from Abu Ishaq al-Sabi'i from his wife that she entered upon Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) among some women, and a woman asked her saying: O Mother of the Believers, I had a slave girl and I sold her to Zayd ibn Arqam for eight hundred, then I bought her back from him for six hundred, and I paid him the six hundred and he wrote down eight hundred against me. So Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) said: How bad is what you bought and how bad is what he sold. Inform Zayd ibn Arqam that he has nullified his jihad with the Messenger of Allah unless he repents. Then the woman said to Aisha: What do you think if I take my capital and return the excess to him? She said: 'Then whoever receives an admonition from his Lord and desists, what has passed is for him' (2). And al-Daraqutni (3) and al-Bayhaqi (4) narrated it in their Sunan from Yusuf ibn Abi Ishaq... from al-Aliyah who said: I was sitting with Aisha and she said: I sold Zayd ibn Arqam a slave girl on credit, and he mentioned similar. And al-Aliyah is a well-known woman of great rank, mentioned by Ibn Sa'd in 'al-Tabaqat' (5), so do not pay attention to what some (6) said that she is unknown and her report is not accepted. If you want more detail, refer to 'Nasb al-Rayah' by Allama al-Zayla'i. This severe threat is evidence of the invalidity of this contract, and attaching such threat to this action cannot be reached by reason, for no sin short of disbelief nullifies any acts of obedience unless that is established by revelation, so this indicates that she said it from hearing (direct transmission). The rational evidence for its impermissibility is mentioned in the Sharh, so we will not repeat it. And the doer of the purchase was left general to include the purchase of one whose testimony is not accepted for the seller, like ascendants, descendants, and his mukatab, for this is also like the purchase of the seller according to Imam Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding other than the slave and mukatab. Similarly, the ruling if he sold by agency for another or bought via agency for another when he is the seller. The scope of his statement is the purchase of all or part, and excluded is the purchase by the seller's heir and agent according to Imam, contrary to the two. As for the seller buying from one who bought from his buyer, or the donee, or the executor, it is permissible by consensus, because difference in cause of ownership is like difference in the object itself. So it is in 'Majma' al-Anhur' (1) and 'Kamal al-Diraya' (2) and others. And it is conditioned on it being less; because if he bought it for the same as he sold or more, it is permissible, because profit is achieved for the buyer and the sold item has entered into his liability. And it is understood from his saying 'less than what he sold' that the second price must be of the same type as the first price. So if he sold it for a thousand then bought it from the buyer with a garment worth less than a thousand, it is permissible. And if he sold it for dinars then bought it with dirhams of lesser value before payment of the first price, it is not permissible according to us by istihsan, and according to Zufar by qiyas. And he only said 'before payment of his first price' because if this purchase occurred after payment of his first price, it is valid by agreement. And another condition is necessary here: that the sold item be in its original state, unchanged, because if it has changed, the ruling is not so. And it is also necessary that the purchase be from the seller himself or his heir, such that if he sold himself then appointed an agent to buy at less, it is permissible for the principal according to Abu Hanifa, and for the agent according to Abu Yusuf, and the principal has an invalid sale according to Muhammad. Thus stated by Allama al-Barjandi in 'Sharh al-Nuqayah'. This, though the discussion is long, is not without beneficial points in the aim.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling that a seller cannot buy back the sold item from the buyer at a lower price before the original price has been paid. The author first states the general principle: if the seller buys back at any lower amount (whether cash or goods), it is prohibited. Then he provides the proof: a hadith from Aisha (RA) where a woman sold a slave girl to Zayd ibn Arqam for 800 (dirhams?) and later bought her back for 600, paying 600 cash but recording a debt of 800. Aisha condemned this action, saying it nullifies jihad unless he repents. This severe warning indicates the contract is invalid (fasid). The author explains that such a warning cannot be reached by reason alone; it must be based on revelation, so Aisha must have heard it from the Prophet (SAW). Then he mentions that the rational evidence is in the Sharh. The author then expands on who is considered the 'buyer' in this ruling: it includes anyone whose testimony is not accepted for the seller, such as parents, children, and a mukatab (slave under contract to buy freedom). According to Imam Abu Hanifa, this is like the seller buying himself, but Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differ regarding other than a slave or mukatab. The ruling also applies if the seller acts through an agent. The author clarifies that buying from a sub-purchaser, donee, or executor is permissible by consensus because the chain of ownership differs. A key condition is that the buyback price must be of the same type as the original price (e.g., both cash, both same currency). If different types, Zufar says it's permissible by qiyas, but the Hanafi school says it's not permissible by istihsan. The prohibition only applies before the original price is paid; after payment, it is valid. Also, the sold item must be unchanged. Finally, if the seller appoints an agent to buy back, Abu Hanifa says it's permissible for the principal, Abu Yusuf says for the agent, and Muhammad says the principal has an invalid sale. This shows the nuanced differences within the Hanafi school on this issue.

Key Terms
بيع فاسد (bay' fasid): An invalid sale that has a deficiency in a condition; it is not permissible but may become valid if the deficiency is rectified (e.g., after payment).
نقد الثمن (naqd al-thaman): Paying the price; here specifically the payment of the original sale price by the buyer to the seller.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has contracted with his master to purchase his freedom in installments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What did Aisha (RA) say about the woman who sold a slave girl for 800 and bought her back for 600 before payment?
A) She said it was permissible if the excess is returned.
B) She condemned Zayd ibn Arqam, saying it nullifies his jihad unless he repents.
C) She allowed it because the woman paid the 600 immediately.
D) She said the sale is valid but the profit is disliked.

Key Takeaway
The seller's buyback at a lower price before payment is prohibited (fasid) based on the hadith of Aisha and supported by the principle that such a severe warning cannot be for a permissible act.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate

Scope and Intra-Hanafi Differences on Buyback Prohibition
Explaining the scope and intra-Hanafi differences regarding the seller buying back at a lower price.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the persons whose purchase is treated like the seller's own purchase according to Imam Abu Hanifa versus the Sahibayn, and apply the ruling to agency scenarios.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa extends the prohibition to relatives and mukatab; Sahibayn restrict it to slaves and mukatab only.

The previous lesson established the core prohibition of seller buyback at a lower price before payment. Now we delve into the scope: who is considered the 'seller' for this ruling? Imam Abu Hanifa has a broader view than his two companions. This lesson also covers conditions like same currency, unchanged state, and the nuances of agency.

Guided Path
1
Identify who is considered 'seller' in the ruling
The text says 'the doer of the purchase was left general to include purchase by one whose testimony is not accepted for the seller'—this includes ascendants (parents), descendants (children), and the mukatab.
These persons are legally akin to the seller because of close relationship or dependence.
2
Understand Imam Abu Hanifa's view
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that purchase by ascendants, descendants, and mukatab is exactly like the seller's own purchase, so the prohibition applies.
This is the position adopted in the text.
3
Understand the Sahibayn's view
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad restrict this only to a slave (abd) and mukatab. They do not include other relatives like parents or children.
The practical difference: if a son buys back from his father's buyer at a lower price, Abu Hanifa says invalid; Sahibayn say valid.
4
Apply to agency cases
If the seller appoints an agent to buy back at a lower price: Abu Hanifa says permissible for the principal; Abu Yusuf says for the agent; Muhammad says the principal's sale is fasid.
This shows the range of opinions within the school.
5
Note additional conditions
The buyback must be from the seller himself or his heir. Buying from a sub-purchaser (who bought from the buyer) is permissible by consensus. The item must be unchanged; if changed, ruling differs.
These conditions are important for correct application.
