By any amount of the price, such as if he sold it to someone other than the seller, or to him for the same price as the first, or more, or for goods, or less after payment. We have only prohibited its permissibility because of what Abd al-Razzaq narrated in his 'Musannaf' (1): Ma'mar and al-Thawri informed us from Abu Ishaq al-Sabi'i from his wife that she entered upon Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) among some women, and a woman asked her saying: O Mother of the Believers, I had a slave girl and I sold her to Zayd ibn Arqam for eight hundred, then I bought her back from him for six hundred, and I paid him the six hundred and he wrote down eight hundred against me. So Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) said: How bad is what you bought and how bad is what he sold. Inform Zayd ibn Arqam that he has nullified his jihad with the Messenger of Allah unless he repents. Then the woman said to Aisha: What do you think if I take my capital and return the excess to him? She said: 'Then whoever receives an admonition from his Lord and desists, what has passed is for him' (2). And al-Daraqutni (3) and al-Bayhaqi (4) narrated it in their Sunan from Yusuf ibn Abi Ishaq... from al-Aliyah who said: I was sitting with Aisha and she said: I sold Zayd ibn Arqam a slave girl on credit, and he mentioned similar. And al-Aliyah is a well-known woman of great rank, mentioned by Ibn Sa'd in 'al-Tabaqat' (5), so do not pay attention to what some (6) said that she is unknown and her report is not accepted. If you want more detail, refer to 'Nasb al-Rayah' by Allama al-Zayla'i. This severe threat is evidence of the invalidity of this contract, and attaching such threat to this action cannot be reached by reason, for no sin short of disbelief nullifies any acts of obedience unless that is established by revelation, so this indicates that she said it from hearing (direct transmission). The rational evidence for its impermissibility is mentioned in the Sharh, so we will not repeat it. And the doer of the purchase was left general to include the purchase of one whose testimony is not accepted for the seller, like ascendants, descendants, and his mukatab, for this is also like the purchase of the seller according to Imam Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding other than the slave and mukatab. Similarly, the ruling if he sold by agency for another or bought via agency for another when he is the seller. The scope of his statement is the purchase of all or part, and excluded is the purchase by the seller's heir and agent according to Imam, contrary to the two. As for the seller buying from one who bought from his buyer, or the donee, or the executor, it is permissible by consensus, because difference in cause of ownership is like difference in the object itself. So it is in 'Majma' al-Anhur' (1) and 'Kamal al-Diraya' (2) and others. And it is conditioned on it being less; because if he bought it for the same as he sold or more, it is permissible, because profit is achieved for the buyer and the sold item has entered into his liability. And it is understood from his saying 'less than what he sold' that the second price must be of the same type as the first price. So if he sold it for a thousand then bought it from the buyer with a garment worth less than a thousand, it is permissible. And if he sold it for dinars then bought it with dirhams of lesser value before payment of the first price, it is not permissible according to us by istihsan, and according to Zufar by qiyas. And he only said 'before payment of his first price' because if this purchase occurred after payment of his first price, it is valid by agreement. And another condition is necessary here: that the sold item be in its original state, unchanged, because if it has changed, the ruling is not so. And it is also necessary that the purchase be from the seller himself or his heir, such that if he sold himself then appointed an agent to buy at less, it is permissible for the principal according to Abu Hanifa, and for the agent according to Abu Yusuf, and the principal has an invalid sale according to Muhammad. Thus stated by Allama al-Barjandi in 'Sharh al-Nuqayah'. This, though the discussion is long, is not without beneficial points in the aim.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling that a seller cannot buy back the sold item from the buyer at a lower price before the original price has been paid. The author first states the general principle: if the seller buys back at any lower amount (whether cash or goods), it is prohibited. Then he provides the proof: a hadith from Aisha (RA) where a woman sold a slave girl to Zayd ibn Arqam for 800 (dirhams?) and later bought her back for 600, paying 600 cash but recording a debt of 800. Aisha condemned this action, saying it nullifies jihad unless he repents. This severe warning indicates the contract is invalid (fasid). The author explains that such a warning cannot be reached by reason alone; it must be based on revelation, so Aisha must have heard it from the Prophet (SAW). Then he mentions that the rational evidence is in the Sharh. The author then expands on who is considered the 'buyer' in this ruling: it includes anyone whose testimony is not accepted for the seller, such as parents, children, and a mukatab (slave under contract to buy freedom). According to Imam Abu Hanifa, this is like the seller buying himself, but Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differ regarding other than a slave or mukatab. The ruling also applies if the seller acts through an agent. The author clarifies that buying from a sub-purchaser, donee, or executor is permissible by consensus because the chain of ownership differs. A key condition is that the buyback price must be of the same type as the original price (e.g., both cash, both same currency). If different types, Zufar says it's permissible by qiyas, but the Hanafi school says it's not permissible by istihsan. The prohibition only applies before the original price is paid; after payment, it is valid. Also, the sold item must be unchanged. Finally, if the seller appoints an agent to buy back, Abu Hanifa says it's permissible for the principal, Abu Yusuf says for the agent, and Muhammad says the principal has an invalid sale. This shows the nuanced differences within the Hanafi school on this issue.

Key Terms
بيع فاسد (bay' fasid): An invalid sale that has a deficiency in a condition; it is not permissible but may become valid if the deficiency is rectified (e.g., after payment).
نقد الثمن (naqd al-thaman): Paying the price; here specifically the payment of the original sale price by the buyer to the seller.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has contracted with his master to purchase his freedom in installments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, whose purchase is treated like the seller's own purchase in the buyback prohibition?
A) Only the seller himself
B) Ascendants, descendants, and mukatab
C) Only slaves and mukatab
D) All blood relatives

Key Takeaway
Imam Abu Hanifa extends the buyback prohibition to ascendants, descendants, and mukatab; the Sahibayn restrict it to slaves and mukatab, and agency cases introduce further differences.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate

Buying Back Sold Item at Lower Price Before Payment: Ruling and Conditions (Hanafi)
Ruling on invalidity of seller buying back at lower price before payment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a seller repurchasing his sold item at a lower price before payment, identify the conditions that trigger invalidity, and distinguish the intra-Hanafi differences on this issue.

Core Idea
Buying back before payment at a lower price is invalid (fāsid) because profit without risk is prohibited.

This passage from the Book of Sale (Kitab al-Buyu') discusses a specific scenario: the seller attempts to repurchase the item he just sold, but at a lower price, before the buyer has paid the original price. The Hanafi school deems this transaction invalid (fāsid) based on the principle that profit must be paired with liability (al-ghunm bi'l-ghurm). The text also explores nuanced conditions and disagreements among the Hanafi masters, illustrating the school's commitment to preventing hidden riba.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Determine the basic legal ruling for the seller buying back the sold item at a lower price before the first price is paid. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, this sale is invalid (fāsid).
The ruling is based on a rational proof and the principle that profit requires bearing risk.
2
Understand the conditions for invalidity
List the specific conditions that must be present for the second sale to be invalid: (1) occurs before payment of first price, (2) second price is lower, (3) second price is of the same type (jins) as the first, (4) the sold item remains unchanged, (5) the purchaser is the seller himself or his heir.
If any condition is missing, the sale may be permissible.
3
Recognize exceptions and permissible scenarios
Identify when the purchase is valid: after payment, or at equal/higher price, or when the second price is of a different type (by istihsan). Also valid if purchased from a third party (e.g., buyer's subsequent buyer).
These exceptions show the principle that profit without risk is prohibited only when the seller still has financial interest.
4
Explore intra-Hanafi differences
Note the disagreements between Abu Hanifa and the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). For example, regarding purchases from relatives whose testimony is not accepted, and agency scenarios. Abu Hanifa holds a stricter position; the disciples differ in certain cases.
This intra-madhhab ikhtilaf is a key feature of Hanafi fiqh, reflecting nuanced ijtihad.
5
Contrast with external views
Compare the Hanafi position with that of Imam Zufar, who allowed the sale by qiyas, and understand why the Hanafi school adopts istihsan to prohibit it.
This shows the role of istihsan as a distinctive Hanafi source of law.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage from the section on invalid and disapproved sales discusses the ruling of a seller buying back what he sold at a lower price before receiving payment. It begins by noting that the ruling is based on revelation and a rational proof which is detailed elsewhere. The key ruling: if a seller buys back his sold item at a price lower than the original sale price before the original price is paid, the second sale is invalid (fāsid) according to Imam Abu Hanifa. However, the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ in certain cases, such as when the buyer is a relative whose testimony is not accepted, or when the purchase is through an agent. The passage clarifies that the invalid portion applies only to the purchase of the original item; other transactions are valid. Specific conditions are elaborated: the second price must be of the same type as the first (e.g., both in cash or both in kind), and the purchase must occur before the first price is paid. If the price is paid later, the purchase becomes valid. Additionally, the sold item must remain unchanged. The passage also discusses the scenario where a seller appoints an agent to buy back at a lower price, with differing views among the Hanafi masters: Abu Hanifa deems it permissible for the principal, Abu Yusuf for the agent, and Muhammad considers it invalid for the principal. This nuanced discussion highlights the Hanafi commitment to preventing unjust profit (riba-like gain) without corresponding risk (ghurm).

Key Terms
فاسد (fāsid): An invalid sale that is not completely void but defective due to a prohibited element; it requires rescission.
قبل نقد الثمن (qabl naqd al-thaman): Before the payment of the purchase price; a condition for the invalidity of the second sale.
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas in certain cases; here it prohibits the sale when the second price is of a different type.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if a seller sells an item for 1000 dinars and then buys it back from the buyer for 800 dirhams (of lesser value) before the 1000 dinars are paid, what is the ruling?
A) The sale is valid because the price is in different currency.
B) The sale is invalid (fasid) by istihsan, because the second price is of a different type and lower.
C) The sale is valid because the buyer can set any price.
D) The sale is invalid only if the price is in the same currency.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a seller cannot repurchase his sold item at a lower price before payment because this generates profit without corresponding risk, considered akin to riba.

Buying Back Sold Item with Another at Same Price Before Payment
Inter-madhhab dispute on combined sale validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on a combined purchase where the seller buys back his sold item together with another at the same price before payment, and distinguish it from the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Profit without risk is invalid; apportioning the price reveals an effective lower price for the original item.

In this lesson, we examine a nuanced sale scenario where a seller reacquires his own sold item together with a new item at the same total price before receiving payment. This transaction, while appearing neutral, is ruled as partially invalid in the Hanafi school due to the prohibition of profit without liability. We will unpack the reasoning and compare it with the Shafi'i view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and parties
Recognize the scenario: a seller sells an item to a buyer, then before payment, the seller buys back that item together with another item from the same buyer at the same total price.
The original sale price is the same as the combined purchase price.
2
State the Hanafi ruling on each item
The purchase of the originally sold item is invalid (fasid), while the purchase of the other item is valid. The total price is apportioned according to the values of the two items.
The invalid portion is due to the seller effectively buying his own item at a lower price.
3
Explain the legal basis: al-ghunm bi'l-ghurm
Profit must be paired with liability. Since the seller did not bear risk for the original item after selling it (the price was not yet paid and thus not in his liability), any profit from that item is prohibited.
This maxim is central to Hanafi sales law.
4
Analyze the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i considers the single price sufficient, viewing the contract as one unit. The Hanafi response is that apportioning the price reveals the seller is profiting without risk.
The practical consequence: Hanafis require rescission of the original item's purchase.
5
Determine the practical consequence
In Hanafi fiqh, the combined sale must be rectified by invalidating the purchase of the original item. The buyer keeps the other item at its apportioned price.
If the sale involved a single item, the entire contract would be invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Essence of Nihaya for the Support of Ri'aya. And purchasing what he sold together with another item that he did not sell, for the same first price in what he sold, and valid in what he did not sell. It is profit as long as he did not guarantee: meaning the price, which is fifteen; because when the seller did not receive it, it did not enter his liability, and profit is in exchange for risk, so the profit is prohibited, thus this sale is invalid, contrary to al-Shafi'i. [2] (1) And purchasing what he sold together with another item that he did not sell for the same first price in what he sold, and valid in what he did not sell: meaning if someone sold a thing for fifteen and did not take the price, then purchased it together with another thing for fifteen, the sale is invalid for the first sold item and valid for the other item. The price is apportioned according to their values, so it is valid for the other item with its share of the price, which is fifteen. [1] His statement: 'It is profit...' because the price, as long as it is not received, did not enter the seller's liability. [2] His statement: 'And purchasing...' The case: He sold a slave-girl for five hundred, and the buyer took possession, then he purchased her and another slave-girl together before payment of the price for five hundred. The purchase of the one he did not sell from him is valid, and of the other – the one he sold – is invalid. The conditions considered in the first case apply here. It is required that it be before payment of the first price, and that the seller purchases from the buyer, such that if he purchased from another, it is valid. But in the first case, it is required that the second price be less; here it can be less or equal. [3] His statement: 'So the sale is invalid' for the first sold item because when the buyer sold two items – one of which is what he purchased from the seller the first time for fifteen, and the other is what he purchased from him – it is inevitable to allocate part of the price in the second sale for the item not purchased from the seller, making the seller a buyer of the other at less than what he sold, which is invalid with us. This meaning does not exist for the second sold item because he did not purchase it from him such that he sold at less, so it is valid due to the absence of the corrupting factor. (1) In Asna al-Matalib: Sale from the seller before delivery is not valid unless compensation is made with the exact item or its like if lost or if it is in the liability. Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage addresses a variant of the previous ruling: the seller buys back his sold item together with a different item at the same total price before the first price is paid. The commentary opens with a quote from the matn: 'And purchasing what he sold together with another item...' It then explains that if the seller sold an item for fifteen and later, before receiving payment, buys back that item plus another item for fifteen, the sale is invalid (fāsid) for the originally sold item but valid for the new item. The profit from the original item is prohibited because the seller did not bear any liability for it (ghurm). The price is apportioned according to the values of the two items. The passage highlights a disagreement with al-Shafi'i, who apparently allows such a transaction. The commentator then gives a concrete example: a seller sells a slave-girl for five hundred, the buyer takes possession, then the seller buys back that slave-girl together with another slave-girl for five hundred before payment. The purchase of the originally sold slave-girl is invalid; the other is valid. Conditions from the earlier case apply: the purchase must be before payment, and from the buyer himself. However, here the second price can be less or equal (unlike the previous case where it had to be lower). The reasoning is that in the second sale, part of the price necessarily corresponds to the new item, so the seller is effectively buying back his own item at a lower price, which is the prohibited element. This ruling is based on the principle of 'al-ghunm bi'l-ghurm' (profit requires risk).

Key Terms
الغُنْمُ بإزاء الغُرْم (al-ghunm bi'izā' al-ghurm): Profit is paired with risk; a legal maxim used here to invalidate profit without liability.
فاسد (fāsid): Invalid sale that must be rescinded due to a defective element (here, prohibited profit).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where a seller sells an item for 500, then before payment buys that item and another item together for 500, what is the Hanafi ruling on the purchase of the originally sold item?
A) It is valid because the total price is the same.
B) It is invalid (fasid) because the seller is effectively buying his own item at a lower price.
C) It is void (batil) and the entire contract is null.
D) It is valid only if the second price is higher.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that profit without liability is prohibited, thus a combined purchase at the same price is invalid for the originally sold item but valid for the new item.

Ruling on Buying Back Sold Item with Another Before Payment
Clarifies the invalidity of repurchasing one's sold item with an extra item for the same price before payment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on repurchasing a sold item together with another item before payment and explain the reasoning based on the principle al-ghunm bi iza' al-ghurm.

Core Idea
Profit without liability is prohibited: repurchasing one's own sold item before payment is fasid because the price is not yet in your risk.

This lesson examines a specific transactional scenario: a seller sells an item, but before receiving payment, buys it back together with an additional item for the same total price. The Hanafi school distinguishes between the two items, declaring the repurchase invalid while allowing the purchase of the new item. The key legal principle at work is that profit requires corresponding liability (al-ghunm bi iza' al-ghurm).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Exactly what is being ruled upon? The act: repurchasing the sold item along with another for the same price before payment. The ruling: fasid (invalid) for the original item, sahih (valid) for the additional item.
2
Locate the dalil
What is the proof cited by the Hanafi school? The maxim 'al-ghunm bi iza' al-ghurm' (profit is paired with liability).
This is a legal maxim derived from the general principles of fiqh, not a direct textual proof from Quran or Sunnah.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does this maxim prove the ruling? The seller did not receive the price, so he bears no risk/liability; therefore any profit from the repurchase is prohibited, making the sale fasid.
The profit here is the potential gain from reselling the repurchased item at a higher price.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Does any other school disagree? Yes, Imam al-Shafi'i holds the entire transaction as valid (presumably because he does not apply the maxim with the same strictness or interprets the conditions differently).
The text mentions this disagreement briefly but does not provide the Shafi'i proof.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Which position is relied upon? The Hanafi position is presented as the mufta bihi (operative ruling) in this text, based on the sound application of the maxim.
No explicit tarjih by later authorities is given here, but the Hanafi position is stated as the authoritative one.

And buying what one sold with another item not sold by him for the first price regarding what was sold, and it is valid regarding what was not sold. Meaning: whoever sold something for fifteen and did not take the price, then bought it with another item for fifteen, the sale is invalid for the first item and valid for the other item, so the price is divided according to their values, so it is valid for the other item with its share of the price, which is fifteen. 'So it is profit' until 'not taken into liability': i.e., the price, which is fifteen; because when the seller did not receive it, it did not enter his liability, and indeed profit is paired with liability, so the profit is forbidden, so this sale is invalid, contrary to al-Shafi'i. [1] His saying: 'So it is profit... etc.' because the price, as long as it is not received, does not enter the seller's liability. [2] His saying: 'And buying... etc.' The scenario of the issue: he sold a slave-girl for five hundred, and the buyer took possession, then he bought her and another slave-girl with her before payment of the price for five hundred. So the purchase of the one he did not sell to him is valid, and regarding the other, which is the one he sold to him, it is invalid. The conditions considered in the first issue are considered here. It is required that it be before payment of the first price, and that the seller buy it from the buyer only; if he bought it from someone else, it is permissible. But in the first issue it is required that the second price be less; here it may be less or equal. [3] His saying: 'So the sale is invalid' regarding the first item; because when the buyer sold the two items, one of which is what he bought from the seller the first time for fifteen, and the other is what he bought from him, it is necessary that in the second sale some of the price be put against what he did not buy from the seller, so the seller becomes a buyer of the other for less than what he sold, and that is invalid according to us. This meaning does not exist regarding the second item; because he did not buy it from him so that he sold it for less, so it is valid due to the absence of the invalidating factor. (1) In Asna al-Matalib (2) (84): 'Selling from the seller before taking possession is not valid unless the compensation is in kind of the item sold or its equivalent if it is destroyed, or if it is in the dhimma.' And see: Hashiyat Qalyubi wa 'Umayra (2:264) and others.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn (core text) regarding a specific transaction. The matn states the ruling: if someone sells an item for a price (e.g., 15), does not yet receive the price, then buys back that same item along with another item for the same total price (15), the sale is invalid (fasid) for the original item but valid for the additional item. The commentator explains that the 15 is allocated proportionally to the values of the two items, so the additional item is validly purchased with its share. The key reason is that the seller did not take liability for the price (since he didn't receive it), and profit requires liability (ghurm). This profit is thus haram, making the sale fasid. This is contrary to al-Shafi'i, who apparently permits it. The commentator then provides a detailed example: a seller sells a slave-girl for 500, the buyer takes possession, then before payment the seller buys back that slave-girl along with another for 500. The purchase of the second slave-girl is valid, but the repurchase of the first is invalid. Conditions: the repurchase must be before payment of the first price, and it must be from the same buyer (not a third party). In the first scenario (selling an item then buying it back with another), the second price must be less; in the slave-girl example, it may be less or equal. The invalidity in the first item is because the buyer is effectively selling the two items, one of which he originally bought from the seller; part of the price must be allocated to that, making the seller buy back his own sold item for less than he sold it, which is fasid. This does not apply to the second item. The footnote clarifies that selling from the seller before taking possession is invalid unless the payment is in kind or equivalent. The second lesson continues on page 143.

Key Terms
الغُنْمُ بإزاء الغُرْم (al-ghunm bi iza' al-ghurm): A legal maxim meaning 'profit is paired with liability'—one cannot take profit without bearing corresponding risk.
البيع الفاسد (al-bay' al-fasid): An invalid sale that is not fundamentally prohibited but deficient in a condition such that it can be rectified; here it refers to the repurchase of the original item.
ضمان (daman): Liability or guarantee; here, the seller's liability for the price received, which he has not yet assumed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on a person who sells an item for 15, does not receive the price, then buys it back along with another item for the same 15?
A) The entire transaction is valid because the price is the same.
B) The sale is invalid for the original item but valid for the new item.
C) The sale is invalid for both items because of uncertainty.
D) The sale is valid only if the second price is higher.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school invalidates the repurchase of a sold item before payment because profit requires liability (al-ghunm bi iza' al-ghurm).

Invalidity of Fixed Deduction and Dispute over Container Weight
Explains the invalidity of a fixed deduction for container weight and the buyer's word in a dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a condition to deduct a fixed weight from a container invalidates a sale and state the rule that the buyer's word is taken in a dispute over the container's identity or weight.

Core Idea
A fixed deduction contradicts the contract; actual weight must be deducted. In disputes, the buyer's word is taken.

This passage from the commentary addresses two practical issues in the sale of oil in a container. First, it explains that a condition to deduct a fixed poundage per container makes the sale invalid because it conflicts with the contract's requirement to deduct the actual weight. Second, it clarifies that if the parties dispute the identity or weight of the container, the buyer's statement is accepted. The commentator also responds to an objection about the invalidity spreading to other parts of the contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
The passage makes two rulings: (a) the sale with a fixed deduction condition is fasid, and (b) in a dispute about the container, the buyer's word is accepted. Identify each and note the legal category.
2
Analyze the reasoning for invalidity
The author says the condition is not required by the contract (لا يقتضيه العقد). Explain what the contract requires (deduction of actual weight). Why is the condition contradictory? Because it fixes a weight that may not match reality.
3
Examine the dispute rule
The buyer's word is taken because he is the possessor of the container (قابض) and the dispute is either about the container's identity or the price. If about identity, the buyer as possessor's word is accepted. If about price, the buyer denies the increase and as denier his word is taken with oath.
4
Understand the response to the objection
The objection: why doesn't the invalidity spread to the entire contract? Answer: the invalidity here is weak (ضعيف مجتهد فيه) and confined to its place, unlike the strong invalidity in combining free and slave which spreads.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزيت على أن يُوزَنَ بظرفِهِ، ويُطْرَحَ عنه بكل ظرف كذا رطلاً: إنَّما يَفْسُدُ لأنَّه شرط لا يقتضيه العقد، بل مقتضى العقد أن يُطْرَحَ بازاءِ الظُّرْفِ مقدار وزنه، كما في المسألة الثانية، وهي ما قال: بخلاف الشَّرط طرح وزنِ الظُّرْفِ عنه، وإن اختلفا في نفس الظرف وقدْرِه، فالقولُ للمشتري: أي اشترى سَمْناً في زق، ورَدَّ الظرف، وهو عشرة أرطال فقال البائع: الزِّقُ غير هذا، وهو خمسةً أرطال، فالقول للمشتري. فإن قيل: ينبغي أن يفسد العقد في الذي لم يبعه منه عند الإمام، كما لو جمع بين حر وعبد وباعهما صفقة واحدة، فالعقد في العبد فاسد. أجيب: بأنَّ الفساد في الجمع بين الحر والعبد قوي مجمع عليه، فيتعدى محله، وفيما نحن فيه ضعيف مجتهد فيه، فيقتصر على محله، فلا يتعدى كما لو جمع بين عبد ومدبر، وقد مرَّ بيانه سابقاً، فتذكر. [١] قوله: في زق: بالكسر يوستي ست براي شراب و جز آن که موي آنرا بریده باشندنه برکندیده. كذا في منتهى الأرب. [٢] قوله: فالقول للمشتري: لأنَّ هذا الاختلاف إمّا في تعيين الزّق المقبوض، أو مقدارِ الثَّمن، فإن كان الأوَّل فالمشتري قابض والقول قوله، سواء كان ضميناً كالغاصب، أو أميناً كالمودع، وإن كان الثاني فهو في الحقيقة اختلاف في الثمن، فيكون القولُ للمشتري أيضاً؛ لأنه ينكر الزيادة، والقولُ للمنكر مع يمينه، ولا يتحالفان. وإن اختلفا في الثمن؛ لأنَّ اختلافهما في الثمن ثبت تبعاً لاختلافهما في الزق، والاختلاف في الزّقِّ لا يوجب التحالف؛ لأنه ليس بمعقودٍ به، ولا معقود عليه، وكذا الاختلاف فيما ثبت تبعاً؛ لأنَّ حكم التبع يخالف حكم الأصل. كذا في «الكفاية»، وغيرها.
And oil on condition that it be weighed with its container, and deducted from it for every container such-and-such a pound: it becomes invalid only because it is a condition not required by the contract; rather, the requirement of the contract is to deduct the amount equal to the weight of the container, as in the second issue, which is what he said: 'Unlike the condition of deducting the weight of the container.' And if they differ regarding the actual container and its quantity, the word is with the buyer. That is: he bought clarified butter (samn) in a water skin, and returned the container, which is ten pounds, and the seller said: 'The water skin is not this, it is five pounds,' so the word is with the buyer. For it is said: 'It should be that the contract becomes invalid in the item not sold by him according to Imam [Abu Hanifa], just as when one combines a free person and a slave and sells them in one bundle, the contract regarding the slave is invalid.' The answer: that the invalidity in combining a free person and a slave is strong and agreed upon, so it extends beyond its place; but in our case it is weak and subject to ijtihad, so it is confined to its place, so it does not extend, just as if one combined a slave and a mudabbar, and its explanation has preceded, so remember. [1] His saying: 'in a water skin (ziq)': with kasra… [Persian explanation]… as in Muntaha al-Arab. [2] His saying: 'the word is with the buyer': because this difference is either regarding the identification of the water skin received, or regarding the amount of the price. If the first, then the buyer is the one in possession and his statement is taken, whether he is a guarantor like a usurper or a trustee like a depositary. If the second, then it is actually a difference regarding the price, so the word is also with the buyer because he denies the increase, and the word of the denier is taken with his oath, and they do not take oaths mutually. If they differed regarding the price, then the difference regarding the price is established as subsidiary to their difference regarding the water skin, and a difference regarding the water skin does not necessitate mutual oaths because it is not the subject of the contract nor the object of sale; likewise a difference regarding what is subsidiary because the ruling of a subsidiary differs from the ruling of the principal. As in al-Kifayah and others.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn regarding the sale of oil (or ghee) in a container where the condition is to weigh it with its container and then deduct a fixed poundage per container. The commentator explains that such a condition makes the sale invalid (fasid) because it is a condition that goes against what the contract requires. The proper requirement is to deduct the actual weight of the container, as in the second scenario where they simply agree to deduct the container's weight. However, if they differ about the actual container and its weight, the buyer's word is taken. The example: buyer bought samn in a water skin, returns the container which he claims is 10 pounds; seller says it's a different container of only 5 pounds. The ruling: the buyer's statement is accepted. The passage then addresses an objection: why doesn't this invalidity affect the other part of the contract (the previously unsold item) as in the case of combining a free man and a slave? The answer: the invalidity in the free+slave case is strong and agreed upon, so it spreads to the entire contract; but here the invalidity is weak and subject to ijtihad, so it is confined to its own place. It is compared to the case of combining a slave and a mudabbar (a slave promised freedom upon owner's death). The margin notes define 'ziq' as a water skin and explain why the buyer's word is preferred: because the dispute is either about the identity of the container (the buyer is the possessor, so his word is taken with his oath) or about the price (the buyer denies the increase, so his word is taken). There is no mutual oath (tahaluf) because the container is not the subject or object of the sale; the dispute is subsidiary.

Key Terms
زق (ziq): A water skin made from animal hide, specifically one that has been cut but the hair not removed; used for storing liquids like oil or ghee.
قبول القول للمشتري (qabul al-qawl li al-mushtari): The buyer's statement is taken as true (with his oath) in a dispute about the container or price, unless there is evidence to the contrary.
التحالف (al-tahāluf): Mutual oath-taking between parties when a dispute arises regarding a sale; here it is not required because the subject of dispute is not directly the contract subject.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the sale of oil invalid when the condition is to deduct a fixed poundage per container rather than the actual weight?
A) Because the container is not owned by the seller.
B) Because it is a condition that contradicts the contract's requirement.
C) Because the oil cannot be measured accurately.
D) Because the buyer cannot verify the weight.

Key Takeaway
A condition that contradicts the contract's requirement renders the sale invalid; the valid approach is to deduct the actual weight, and in disputes, the buyer's word is accepted.

Invalid Condition in Sale of Oil: Weighing with Container and Estimating Weight
Ruling on invalid condition in sale of oil weighed with container

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a sale conditioned with weighing oil together with its container and deducting a fixed weight, and apply the rule for resolving disputes about the container's identity or weight.

Core Idea
A condition estimating container weight invalidates the sale due to uncertainty; disputes over the container itself are resolved by the buyer's word.

In this passage, the commentator addresses a common sale practice: oil sold with the condition that it be weighed together with its container and then a fixed weight per container is deducted. This condition introduces uncertainty because the actual container weight is replaced by an estimate. The Hanafi school rules such a sale invalid (fasid). The passage also discusses what happens if buyer and seller later disagree about which container was used or its weight, establishing a clear rule for such disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the invalidating condition
Read the opening: the sale is conditioned on weighing oil with its container and deducting a fixed weight per container. This condition is not required by the contract and introduces uncertainty about the actual quantity of oil, making the sale fasid.
Jahalah (uncertainty) in the price or subject matter invalidates a sale in Hanafi fiqh.
2
Contrast with the valid condition
The passage contrasts this with a valid condition: simply deducting the actual weight of the container. That is permissible because the compensation for the container is known (its actual weight), whereas an estimated deduction is not.
The key is knowledge of the container's weight as compensation (iwad).
3
Follow the qiyas argument
An objection is raised: if this condition invalidates part of the sale, why doesn't it spread to the whole contract, like selling a free person with a slave (where the slave contract is invalid and spreads)? The answer: the invalidity in the free/slave case is strong and agreed upon (ijma'), so it spreads. Here, the invalidity is weak and subject to ijtihad, so it is confined to its locus.
This distinction is crucial: strong invalidity spreads; weak invalidity does not.
4
Analyse the dispute resolution rule
When buyer and seller later disagree about the container itself (e.g., its identity or weight), the buyer's word is taken. Reason: the buyer is the possessor (if he has the container) or the denier of an excess claim. This disagreement is about the container, not the price directly, so mutual oaths (tahaluf) are not required.
The rule of qawl al-mushtari (buyer's statement) applies here because the container is not the subject of the contract nor the price.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزيت على أن يُوزَنَ بظرفِهِ، ويُطْرَحَ عنه بكل ظرف كذا رطلاً، بخلاف الشرط طرح وزن الظرف عنه، وإن اختلفا في نفس الظرف وقدره، فالقول للمشتري. إنَّما يَفْسُدُ؛ لأنَّه شرط لا يقتضيه العقد، بل مقتضى العقد أن يُطْرَحَ بازاءِ الظُّرْفِ مقدار وزنه، كما في المسألة الثانية، وهي ما قال: (بخلاف الشَّرط طرح وزنِ الظُّرْفِ عنه، وإن اختلفا في نفس الظرف وقدْرِه، فالقولُ للمشتري): أي اشترى سَمْناً في زق، ورَدَّ الظرف، وهو عشرة أرطال فقال البائع: الزِّقُ غير هذا، وهو خمسةً أرطال، فالقول للمشتري. فإن قيل: ينبغي أن يفسد العقد في الذي لم يبعه منه عند الإمام، كما لو جمع بين حر وعبد وباعهما صفقة واحدة، فالعقد في العبد فاسد. أجيب: بأنَّ الفساد في الجمع بين الحر والعبد قوي مجمع عليه، فيتعدى محله، وفيما نحن فيه ضعيف مجتهد فيه، فيقتصر على محله، فلا يتعدى كما لو جمع بين عبد ومدبر، وقد مرَّ بيانه سابقاً، فتذكر. [١] قوله: في زق؛ بالكسر يوستي ست براي شراب و جز آن که موي آنرا بریده باشندنه برکندیده. كذا في منتهى الأرب. [٢] قوله: فالقول للمشتري؛ لأنَّ هذا الاختلاف إمّا في تعيين الزّق المقبوض، أو مقدارِ الثَّمن، فإن كان الأوَّل فالمشتري قابض والقول قوله، سواء كان ضميناً كالغاصب، أو أميناً كالمودع، وإن كان الثاني فهو في الحقيقة اختلاف في الثمن، فيكون القولُ للمشتري أيضاً؛ لأنه ينكر الزيادة، والقولُ للمنكر مع يمينه، ولا يتحالفان. وإن اختلفا في الثمن؛ لأنَّ اختلافهما في الثمن ثبت تبعاً لاختلافهما في الزق، والاختلاف في الزّقِّ لا يوجب التحالف؛ لأنه ليس بمعقودٍ به، ولا معقود عليه، وكذا الاختلاف فيما ثبت تبعاً؛ لأنَّ حكم التبع يخالف حكم الأصل. كذا في «الكفاية» وغيرها.
And oil to be weighed with its container, and deducted from it for each container such and such pounds, contrary to the condition of deducting the weight of the container. And if they disagree about the container itself and its amount, the statement is of the buyer. It is invalid because it is a condition that the contract does not require; rather, the requirement of the contract is to deduct in lieu of the container its weight amount, as in the second issue, which he said: (Contrary to the condition of deducting the weight of the container from it, and if they disagree about the container itself and its amount, the statement is of the buyer): meaning he bought samn in a skin container, and he returned the container, and it was ten pounds, so the seller said: the skin is not this, it is five pounds, so the statement is of the buyer. If it is said: It should be that the contract is invalid in what he did not sell from it according to Imam Abu Hanifa, as if he combined a free person and a slave and sold them as one lump sum, then the contract for the slave is invalid. The answer: Because invalidity in combining a free and a slave is strong, agreed upon, so it spreads to its locus, but in our case it is weak and subject to ijtihad, so it is confined to its locus and does not spread, as if he combined a slave and a mudabbar, and its explanation has passed previously, so remember. [1] His statement: 'in a skin container': (Persian explanation)... [2] His statement: 'the statement is of the buyer': because this disagreement is either about identification of the taken skin container or about the amount of price. If the first, then the buyer is the possessor and his statement is accepted, whether he is a guarantor like a usurper or a trustee like a depositee. If the second, then in reality it is a disagreement about the price, so the statement is of the buyer as well because he denies the excess, and the statement is of the denier with his oath, and they do not take mutual oaths. And if they disagree about the price? because their disagreement about the price is subsidiary to their disagreement about the skin container, and disagreement about the skin container does not necessitate mutual oaths because it is not a subject of contract nor an object of contract; similarly, disagreement about what is established as subsidiary because the ruling of the subsidiary opposes the ruling of the principle. As in al-Kifayah and other works.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from Hanafi fiqh regarding a sale of oil (or similar liquid) where the buyer stipulates that the oil will be weighed together with its container, and then a fixed weight per container will be deducted. The commentator explains that this condition invalidates the sale (fasid) because the contract requires that the actual weight of the container be deducted as compensation (iwad) for it, not an estimated amount. The condition is therefore not demanded by the contract and introduces uncertainty (jahalah). The passage contrasts this valid condition: to simply deduct the weight of the container, which is permissible. Then it addresses a scenario where the buyer and seller disagree about the container itself: if the buyer returns a container and the seller claims it is not the one originally sold, the buyer's word is taken. The commentator then answers an objection: one might think that if the condition invalidates a part of the sale (like the oil in the first container), the whole contract should be invalid, analogous to selling a free man and a slave together (where the contract for the slave is invalid). The answer is that the invalidity in the free/slave case is strong and agreed upon, so it spreads; here it is weak and subject to ijtihad, so it is confined to its locus. The passage also discusses the rules of taking oaths when there is a dispute: the buyer's word is taken because he is the possessor or denier, and mutual oaths are not required since the dispute is about the container itself, not the price.

Key Terms
ظرف (zarf): The container in which the oil is sold (e.g., a skin or vessel).
زق (ziqq): A skin container used for liquids like oil or ghee.
فساد (fasad): Invalidity of a sale due to a condition that contradicts the contract’s requirement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the sale of oil with the condition 'weighed with its container and deduct such and such pounds per container' become fasid (invalid) according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) Because the condition is not mentioned in the contract.
B) Because the condition requires weighing the container, which is impossible.
C) Because the condition introduces uncertainty (jahalah) about the price, as the container's weight is estimated, not actual.
D) Because the buyer and seller will inevitably disagree about the container.

Key Takeaway
A sale condition that estimates container weight instead of deducting actual weight invalidates the sale due to uncertainty; disputes about the container itself are resolved by the buyer's word.

Ruling on Sale of Watercourse vs. Road
Ruling on sale/gift of watercourse vs. road with internal ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the validity of selling a watercourse and a road, and apply the preferred Hanafi position on selling rights of passage and drainage.

Core Idea
Certainty of subject matter determines validity; road is known by custom, watercourse is uncertain.

This passage clarifies a key distinction in Hanafi sales law: why selling a watercourse (masil) is invalid while selling a road (tariq) is valid, and how the same logic applies to gifting. The ruling hinges on whether the subject matter is known or unknown. The text also explores the sale of rights separately, revealing an internal disagreement within the school that is resolved by preferring the prohibition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main subjects and their base rulings
The text states that the sale and gift of a watercourse (masil) is invalid, and the sale and gift of a road (tariq) is valid. Note that both sale and gift are treated similarly.
2
Distinguish between selling the physical substance vs. selling the right
The commentator explains that two possibilities exist: selling the actual land (raqabah) of the watercourse/road, or selling the right of drainage (haqq al-tasil) / right of passage (haqq al-murur). Each has different considerations.
3
Analyze the reason for invalidity of the watercourse
If selling the substance, the amount of water that flows is unknown (majhul) because it varies, creating uncertainty (jahalah) that invalidates the sale. If selling the right of drainage, it is either on ground (unknown place) or on roof (attached to air, not property).
Exception: if boundaries are specified, sale of watercourse substance may be valid per al-Ayni.
4
Analyze the validity of the road
The road is known by custom: its width is the width of the largest door, and its length extends to the through street. Even if not specified, it is estimable, so sale is valid.
5
Examine the internal ikhtilaf on selling the right of passage
Two narrations exist: Ibn Samalah permits it (based on need and it being a known right attached to a lasting entity), while al-Ziyadaat prohibits it. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth corrected the prohibition, making it the preferred view.
The preferred view is that selling rights individually is not permissible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبطل بيع المسيل وهبته، وصحا في الطريق) : أي صح البيع والهبة في الطريق، قيل : إن أُريد رقبةُ المسيلِ والطَّريقِ فمقدار ما يسيِّلُه الماء مجهول، فلا يجوز فيه البيع والهبة، وأمَّا الطريق فمعلوم ، وإن لم يُبَيِّن ، فهو مُقَدَّر بعرض باب الدَّارِ كذا في «باب القسمة»، فيجوز فيه البيع والهبة، وإن أريد حق المسيل، فإن كان علي الأرض فمجهولٌ لِمَا مَرَّ ، وإن كان على السطح، فهو حقُّ التَّعلِّي، فهو متعلّق بعين لا يبقي ، وحَقُّ المرور فيه روايتان : حق ١. وجه البطلان غير مال. ٢. ووجه الصحة الاحتياج به ، وهو حق معلوم متعلق بعين باق. [١] قوله : قيل ؛ حاصله : أن المسألة تحتمل وجهين : ١. بيع رقبة المسيل والطريق : أي نفسهما وذاتهما. ٢. وبيع حق التسبيل في المسيل، وحق المرور في الطريق. فإن أريد الأوّل فمقدار المسيل : أي قدر ما يسيّله الماء مجهول ؛ لأنّ مقدار ما يشغله الماء يختلفُ بقلة الماء وكثرته فيبطل البيع ؛ لجهالة المبيع، وهذا إذا لم يُبين الطول والعرض، أما إذا علم حدوده وموضوعه فجازَ البيع. كما صرح به العَيْني. وأما الطريق فمعلوم ؛ لأنَّ له طولاً وعرضاً معلوماً، فإنَّ عرضه مقدار باب الدار، وطوله إلى السكَّةِ النافذة غالباً، فيجوزُ البيع والهبة، وهذا إذا بينهما، وإذا لم يبينهما فيجوز أيضاً ؛ لأنه يقدّر بعرض باب الدار العظمى. كذا في «النهاية». وإن أريد الثاني، فإن كان حقُّ التسييل على الأرض فمجهول لجهالة محله ؛ لأنه لا يعلم قدر ما يشغله من الماء، وإن كان حق التسييل على السطح فهو التعلّي، فلا يجوز بيعه باتفاق الروايات ؛ لأنه متعلّق بالهواء، فليس بمال، ولكونه مجهولاً لاختلافه بقلة الماء وكثرته. وحق المرور فيه روايتان : في رواية ابن سماعة له، وفي رواية الزيادات : لا يجوز، وصححه الفقيه أبو الليث بأنه حق من الحقوق، وبيع الحقوق بالإنفراد لا يجوز. كذا في «الدرر»، وفي رواية : يجوز.
And the sale of the watercourse and its gift is invalid, and they are valid in the road. Meaning: the sale and gift are valid for the road. It is said: If what is intended is the substance of the watercourse and the road, then the amount of water that flows through it is unknown, so sale and gift are not permissible; as for the road, it is known, even if not specified, it is estimated by the width of the door, as in the chapter on partition, so sale and gift are permissible. If what is intended is the right of drainage, then if it is on ground, it is unknown for what has passed; if it is on a roof, it is the right of elevation, so it is attached to a non-lasting entity, and the right of passage has two narrations: 1. The aspect of invalidity: it is not property. 2. The aspect of validity: need for it, and it is a known right attached to a lasting entity. [1] His statement: 'It is said'; summary: the issue has two aspects: 1. Sale of the substance of the watercourse and road: i.e., themselves and their essences. 2. Sale of the right of drainage in the watercourse and the right of passage in the road. If the first is intended, then the extent of the watercourse (the amount of water it drains) is unknown, because the amount of water it carries varies with low and high water, so the sale is invalid due to ignorance of the sold item; this if length and width are not specified. But if its boundaries and location are known, the sale is permissible, as al-Ayni stated. As for the road, it is known because it has known length and width: its width is the width of the door, and its length extends to the through street usually, so sale and gift are permissible. This is if they are specified; if not specified, they are also permissible because it is estimated by the width of the largest door. As in al-Nihayah. If the second is intended, then if the right of drainage is on ground, it is unknown due to ignorance of its place because it is not known how much water it will occupy; if the right of drainage is on a roof, it is the right of elevation, so its sale is not permissible by agreement of the narrations because it is attached to air, so it is not property, and due to its being unknown because it varies with low and high water. The right of passage has two narrations: in the narration of Ibn Samalah, it is permissible; in the narration of al-Ziyadaat, it is not permissible, and al-Faqih Abu al-Layth corrected that it is a right, and selling rights individually is not permissible. As in al-Durar, and in one narration it is permissible.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage addresses the validity of selling or gifting a watercourse (masil) and a road (tariq). The main ruling states that the sale of a watercourse and its gift are invalid, while the sale and gift of a road are valid. The commentator explains that this depends on what exactly is being sold. Two interpretations are considered: (1) selling the physical substance (raqabah) of the watercourse/road, i.e., the land itself used for drainage or passage, and (2) selling the right of drainage (haqq al-tasil) or the right of passage (haqq al-murur). If the physical substance is intended, the watercourse is invalid because the amount of water that flows is unknown, creating uncertainty (jahalah) about the subject matter. However, if the watercourse's boundaries are specified, it may be valid as per al-Ayni. The road, on the other hand, has known dimensions based on custom: its width is the width of the door (usually the largest door in that locality) and its length extends to the through street, so it is known and can be sold. Even if not explicitly specified, it is estimated by the width of the door. If the right of drainage is intended, there is a further distinction: if the right is on ground, it is still unknown; if it is on a roof, it is a right of elevation (ta'alli) attached to air, which is not considered property (mal), so its sale is invalid by agreement. For the right of passage, there are two narrations within the Hanafi school: one permitting its sale (riwayat Ibn Samalah) and one prohibiting it (riwayat al-Ziyadaat). The preferred position (as corrected by al-Faqih Abu al-Layth) is that it is not permissible because selling rights individually is not allowed. The text also references sources like al-Nihayah, al-Durar, etc.

Key Terms
مسيل (masil): A watercourse or drainage channel, often a right to drain water across another's land.
طريق (tariq): A road or right of way, typically a path for access.
تعلّي (ta'alli): The right of elevation, i.e., to build above or use the airspace.
رقبة (raqabah): The corporeal substance or physical thing itself, as opposed to the right or benefit.
جاهلية (jahalah): Uncertainty or ignorance about an essential element of the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi position in this passage, what is the ruling on selling the right of passage (haqq al-murur) independently?
A) It is valid because it is a known right and people need it.
B) It is invalid because it is a right and selling rights individually is not permissible.
C) It is valid only if the road's width is specified.
D) It is invalid only if the right is attached to air.

Key Takeaway
Certainty of the subject matter is essential for a valid sale; the road is known by custom, while the watercourse and its rights are uncertain or non-property, rendering their transfer invalid.

Muslim Appointing Dhimmi to Sell Wine or Pork – Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on agency for prohibited items

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on a Muslim appointing a dhimmi to sell wine or pork, explain the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and apply the tarjih (preferred view).

Core Idea
A Muslim may appoint a dhimmi to sell wine or pork according to Abu Hanifa, but the Sahibayn rule it invalid.

This passage delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of a Muslim appointing a dhimmi (a protected non-Muslim) to engage in transactions involving wine or pork. While these items are strictly prohibited and impure for Muslims, the dhimmi can legally own and trade them according to his own religion. The issue hinges on whether the principal's incapacity to deal directly with these items renders the agency invalid. Abu Hanifa holds it permissible but severely disliked, while the Sahibayn deem it invalid. The commentary provides proofs, refutations, and clarifies the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and ruling of Abu Hanifa
Identify the scenario: a Muslim appoints a dhimmi to sell or buy wine or pork. According to Abu Hanifa, the appointment is permissible (ja'iz) but severely disliked (makruh tahriman). Note the required disposition of the proceeds.
Abu Hanifa's ruling is from the Zahir al-Riwayah.
2
Identify the opposing view of the Sahibayn
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that such an appointment is invalid (la yajuz). Their principle: 'One cannot authorize what one cannot do oneself'.
The Sahibayn's view is more precautionary.
3
Analyse the core principle conflict
Abu Hanifa argues that the agent acts with his own legal capacity (huwa al-akid), so the principal's incapacity is irrelevant. The Sahibayn focus on the principal's lack of direct authority over the item.
The debate centers on the nature of agency: is it delegation of capacity or of authority?
4
Examine the refutations and responses
The commentary provides counterexamples (sub-agent, judge, executor) to refute the Sahibayn's principle, and rejects the analogy to marriage agency for a Magian because in marriage the agent is a mere messenger.
Counterexamples show that in many cases one can authorize another even if one cannot act personally.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position for fatwa is that of the Sahibayn, i.e., invalid, due to the principle of avoiding prohibited transactions and the weight of later scholars' preference.
Al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah and later masters prefer the Sahibayn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمر المسلم ببيع خمر، أو خنزير، أو شرائهما ذميًا. قوله : وأَمْرُ المسلم.... الخ ; أي إن وكَّل مسلم ذميّاً ببيع خمر أو خنزير أو شرائهما جاز عند الإمام له، ويكره هذا التوكيل أشد كراهية، فإن كان خمراً يخللها، وإن كان خنزيراً يسيبه، وإن كان ثمن خمر تصدَّق به ; لأنه عوض الخمر، فتمكن الخبث فيه. كذا في «الكفاية». وقيد بـ«المسلم» ; لأنه لو وكَّل النَّصراني نصرانياً يصح بالاتفاق. وقيد بقوله : ذميّاً» ; لأنَّه لو وكل مسلم مسلماً لا يجوز بالاتفاق. وبقوله : «ببيع خمر» ; لأنه لو وكُل المسلم ذمّيّاً بهبة الخمر وتسليمها لا يجوز; لأنّ أحكام العقد ترجع إلى الموكل، فصار كالرسول. كذا في «النهاية». وعندهما: لا يجوز ; لأن الموكل لا يليه بنفسه، فلا يُولي غيره. وله : إن العاقد، وهو الوكيل يتصرف بأهليته. قوله : لأن...الخ ; يعني أنَّ الموكَّل المسلم ليس له ولاية التصرُّف بالذي أَمرَه للذمي فلا نوليه غيره ; لأن المسلم لا يملك بنفسه، فلا يُمَلك غيرَه ; لأنَّ عليك ما لا يملك لا يجوز، ألا ترى أنَّ المسلم إذا وكُل مجوسيّاً بأن يزوجه مجوسية لا يصح العقد. وهذا الدليل منقوض : ١. بالوكيل بشراء عبدٍ بعينه إذا وكُل آخر بشرائه، فإنه يثبت الملك للوكيل الأول، وهو بنفسه لا يلي الشراء لنفسه. ٢. وبالقاضي إذا أمر ذميّاً ببيع خمرٍ أو خنزير خَلْفَه ذمي آخر، وهو لا يلي التصرُّفَ بنفسه. ٣. وبالذمي إذا أوصى إلى مسلم وقد تركهما، فإنَّ الوصي يوكل ذميّاً بالبيع والقسمة، وهو لا يلي ذلك بنفسه. والقياس على تزويج المجوسي مدفوع بأن حقوق العقد في النكاح ترجع إلى الموكل والوكيل سفير لا غير. كذا في «المنح». قوله : وله إنَّ العاقد...الخ ; حاصله : إنّ الوكيل إنما يملك التصرُّف لكونه حُراً عاقلاً بالغاً ; ولهذا يستغنى عن الإضافة إلى الموكل، وترجع الحقوق إليه، والحاجة إلى الموكل ; لانتقال حكم التصرُّف إليه، والموكَّلُ أهل لانتقال ملك الخمر إليه إرثاً، وصورة الإرث بأن أسلم النصراني وله خنازير وخمور، ومات قبل تسييب الخنازير وتخليل الخمر، وله وارث مسلم يملكها. كذا في «الكفاية».
And the command of a Muslim to sell wine, or pork, or to buy them through a dhimmi. His statement: 'And the command of a Muslim...' i.e., if a Muslim appoints a dhimmi to sell wine or pork or to buy them, it is permissible according to Imam (Abu Hanifa), and this appointment is severely disliked. If it is wine, he should turn it into vinegar; if it is pork, he should set it free; if it is the price of wine, he should give it in charity because it is the exchange for wine, so impurity is entrenched in it. So in 'al-Kifaya'. And it is restricted by 'Muslim' because if a Christian appoints a Christian, it is permissible by agreement. And restricted by his statement 'dhimmi' because if a Muslim appoints a Muslim, it is not permissible by agreement. And by his statement 'to sell wine' because if a Muslim appoints a dhimmi to gift wine or deliver it, it is not permissible because the rulings of the contract revert to the principal, so he becomes like a messenger. So in 'al-Nihaya'. And according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is not permissible because the principal does not have authority over it himself, so he cannot authorize someone else. And for him (Abu Hanifa): Indeed the contractor, i.e., the agent, acts with his own legal capacity. His statement: 'Because...' i.e., the Muslim principal has no authority to deal with what he commanded the dhimmi to do, so he cannot authorize someone else; because a Muslim does not own it himself, so he cannot make someone else own it; because what one does not own is not permissible. Do you not see that if a Muslim appoints a Magian to marry a Magian woman for him, the marriage is not valid. This proof is refuted: 1. By an agent to buy a specific slave if he appoints another to buy it, then ownership is established for the first agent, and he himself does not have the capacity to buy for himself. 2. By a judge who orders a dhimmi to sell wine or pork behind him another dhimmi, and he does not have the capacity to act himself. 3. By a dhimmi who appoints a Muslim as executor and has two properties, then the executor appoints a dhimmi to sell and divide, and he does not have that capacity himself. And the analogy to the marriage of a Magian is repelled because the rights of the contract in marriage revert to the principal, and the agent is merely a messenger. So in 'al-Minah'. His statement: 'And for him indeed the contractor...' The summary: Indeed the agent has the capacity to act by virtue of being free, rational, and adult; for this reason he is independent of the principal's authorization, and the rights revert to him, and the need for the principal is for the transfer of the ruling of the act to him, and the principal is eligible for the transfer of ownership of wine to him by inheritance, and an example of inheritance is that a Christian converts to Islam and has pigs and wine, and dies before freeing the pigs and turning the wine into vinegar, and he has a Muslim heir who inherits them. So in 'al-Kifaya'.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of a Muslim appointing a dhimmi (non-Muslim subject of the Islamic state) to buy or sell wine or pork. The matn states the ruling briefly, and the commentary expands on the positions of the founding imams. According to Abu Hanifa (referred to as al-Imam), such an appointment is permissible (ja'iz) but severely disliked (makruh tahriman). The reason for dislike is that these items are impure and prohibited for Muslims. However, the appointment is legally valid because the dhimmi can lawfully own and trade in these items according to his own religion. The agent acts on his own capacity ('aqd bi-ahliyatihi). If the agent receives wine as payment, the principal must turn it into vinegar; if pork, set it free; if the price is wine, the principal must give it away in charity because it is impure wealth. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn) disagree and hold that the appointment is invalid because the Muslim principal cannot directly deal in these items himself, so he cannot authorize another to do so (al-wakil la yalihi bi-nafsihi). The commentary then presents Abu Hanifa's proof: the agent is the contractor and his capacity is sufficient; the principal's need is only for the transfer of ownership to him, and the principal is eligible to inherit such items if they come into his possession through inheritance (e.g., if a Christian relative dies with wine and pork, a Muslim heir can inherit them). The commentary also refutes objections by showing that similar agencies are valid even when the principal cannot do the act himself, such as an agent for buying a specific slave appointing a sub-agent, or a judge ordering a dhimmi to sell wine, or an executor appointing a dhimmi for sale and division. The analogy to marriage agency for a Magian is rejected because in marriage the agent is merely a messenger.

Key Terms
خمر (khamr): Alcoholic beverage, specifically wine, which is impure and prohibited for Muslims.
خنزير (khinzir): Pig, an impure animal whose meat is prohibited and whose sale is invalid for Muslims.
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim subject of an Islamic state who is under protection and allowed to practice his religion.
توكيل (tawkīl): The act of appointing an agent (wakil) to perform a legal act on one's behalf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), why is a Muslim's appointment of a dhimmi to sell wine invalid?
A) Because the wine is impure and cannot be sold under any circumstances.
B) Because the Muslim principal cannot sell wine himself, so he cannot authorize another to do so.
C) Because a dhimmi cannot be an agent for a Muslim in any transaction.
D) Because the contract of sale requires the seller to own the item, which the dhimmi does not.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, although Abu Hanifa permits a Muslim to appoint a dhimmi to sell wine or pork, the mufta bihi position of the Sahibayn invalidates such agency to prevent involvement in prohibited transactions.

Conditions Valid and Invalid in a Sale Contract
Ruling on two types of conditions in sale contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between conditions required by the contract (valid) and conditions not required with no benefit (invalid) in a Hanafi sale, and state the legal effect of each.

Core Idea
A condition inherent to the contract is valid; a useless condition invalidates the sale.

This lesson introduces the Hanafi classification of conditions in a sale contract. The matn provides a clear dichotomy: conditions that the contract necessarily implies (like transfer of ownership) are valid, while conditions that serve no purpose for either party render the sale fasid. Understanding this principle is essential for evaluating contractual stipulations in Islamic commercial law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The passage divides conditions into two types: those required by the contract (yaytaqlahu al-aqd) and those not required with no benefit (la yaytaqlahu wa la naf'a).
2
Analyze the valid condition
The example given is 'ownership for the buyer' (milk al-mushtari). This condition is inherent to the sale's purpose, so it is valid and does not affect the contract's soundness.
The contract exists to transfer ownership, so this condition is part of its essence.
3
Analyze the invalid condition
The example is 'the buyer must not sell the purchased animal' (la yabi' al-dabbah al-mubi'ah). This condition is not required by the contract and offers no benefit to either party, so it makes the sale fasid.
Imposing a restriction on the buyer's right to resell has no legitimate purpose in the contract.
4
Apply the rule
Given any condition in a sale, classify it: (1) required by contract? → valid; (2) not required but beneficial? → valid (per other Hanafi texts); (3) not required and no benefit? → fasid.
The third type is explicitly declared invalid by this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والبيع بشرط يقتضيه العقدُ : كشرط الملك للمشتري، أو لا يقتضيه ولا نفع فيه لأحد : كشرط أن لا يبيع الدابة المبيعة.
And the sale with a condition that the contract requires, such as the condition of ownership for the buyer, or a condition that the contract does not require and in which there is no benefit for anyone, such as the condition that he not sell the purchased animal.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the matn that classifies conditions in a sale contract. It provides two categories: (1) Conditions that the contract itself necessitates (yaytaqlah al-aqd), meaning they are inherent to the contract's purpose—like the buyer acquiring ownership of the item. Such conditions are valid and do not affect the contract's soundness. (2) Conditions that are not required by the contract and provide no benefit to either party (la yaytaqlahu wa la naf'a fihi li ahad)—for example, a condition that the buyer cannot resell the purchased animal. Such conditions are invalid because they impose an unnecessary restriction with no mutual benefit. According to the Hanafi school, including such a condition renders the sale fasid (corrupt) because it contravenes the principle that the contract should be free from stipulations that are not part of the contract's essence. The commentator (al-Marghinani) notes that this ruling is an 'asl (principle) that covers many branches (furu').

Key Terms
شرط (shart): A stipulation or condition in a contract that modifies the rights or obligations of the parties.
بيع (bay'): A contract of sale: transfer of ownership of a specific item in exchange for compensation.
ملك (milk): Ownership; the legal right to possess, use, and dispose of a thing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following conditions in a sale is considered valid according to the Hanafi school?
A) The buyer must not sell the purchased item.
B) The seller reserves the right to use the item for one day after sale.
C) The buyer acquires ownership of the item upon payment.
D) The buyer must donate a portion of the price to charity.

Key Takeaway
A condition in a sale that is inherent to the contract is valid; a condition that is not required and has no benefit invalidates the sale.

Muslim Agent Appointing Non-Muslim Sub-Agent
Intra-Hanafi dispute on sub-agency with a non-Muslim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on a Muslim agent delegating to a non-Muslim sub-agent and evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
A Muslim agent can delegate to a non-Muslim sub-agent according to Abu Hanifa, but not according to the Sahibayn.

This passage examines a specific intra-Hanafi dispute: may a Muslim agent (wakil) appoint a non-Muslim (dhimmi) as a sub-agent? The answer hinges on whether the agent acts in his own capacity or merely as a conduit for the principal. Both positions are argued with proofs and counterexamples, revealing a rich debate on the nature of agency.

Guided Path
1
State Abu Hanifa's position
Identify that Abu Hanifa deems the sub-agency valid because the agent acts with his own capacity (hurr, aqil, baligh).
2
Identify the Sahibayn's view and their proof
The Sahibayn argue it is invalid via qiyas: a Muslim cannot marry a Magian woman himself, so he cannot delegate that; similarly, he cannot sell wine, so cannot delegate to a dhimmi.
The key analogy (asl) is marriage to a Magian, where the principal lacks capacity.
3
Analyse Abu Hanifa's rebuttal
He provides three counterexamples (buying a specific slave, judge's order, executor) where the principal cannot act directly yet delegation is valid, and distinguishes marriage because the agent is merely a safir.
These rebuttals weaken the qiyas.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
If the sub-agent acts, the contract is valid (Abu Hanifa) or invalid (Sahibayn). The mufta bihi is the Sahibayn's view.

Source Text

وعندهما: لا يجوز؛ لأن الموكل لا يليه بنفسه، فلا يُولي غيره. وله: إن العاقد، وهو الوكيل يتصرف بأهليته. [1] قوله: لأن...الخ؛ يعني أن الموكل المسلم ليس له ولاية التصرف بالذي أمره للذمي فلا نوليه غيره؛ لأن المسلم لا يملك بنفسه، فلا يملك غيره؛ لأن من لا يملك لا يجوز, ألا ترى أن المسلم إذا وكل مجوسياً بأن يزوجه مجوسية لا يصح العقد. وهذا الدليل منقوض: 1. بالوكيل بشراء عبد بعينه إذا وكل آخر بشرائه، فإنه يثبت الملك للوكيل الأول، وهو بنفسه لا يلي الشراء لنفسه. 2. وبالقاضي إذا أمر ذمياً ببيع خمر أو خنزير خلفه ذمي آخر، وهو لا يلي التصرف بنفسه. 3. وبالذمي إذا أوصى إلى مسلم وقد تركها، فإن الوصي يوكل ذمياً بالبيع والقسمة، وهو لا يلي ذلك بنفسه. والقياس على تزويج المجوسي مدفوع بأن حقوق العقد في النكاح ترجع إلى الموكل والوكيل سفير لا غير. And according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): It is not permissible, because the principal does not undertake it himself, so he does not delegate another. And for him (Abu Hanifa): Indeed the contractor, who is the agent, acts with his own capacity. [1] He said: 'Because...etc.'; meaning the Muslim principal does not have the authority of disposition over what he commanded the dhimmi to do, so we do not delegate another; because the Muslim does not own it himself, so he cannot make another own; because the one who does not own, it is not permissible. Do you not see that if a Muslim appoints a Magian as agent to marry a Magian woman, the contract is not valid. And this proof is refuted: 1. By an agent to buy a specific slave if he appoints another to buy it, for the ownership is established for the first agent, and he himself does not undertake the purchase for himself. 2. And by a judge if he orders a dhimmi to sell wine or pork, then another dhimmi appoints him, and he does not undertake the disposition himself. 3. And by a dhimmi if he bequeaths to a Muslim and has left them, then the executor appoints a dhimmi for sale and division, and he does not undertake that himself. (2) He said: 'And he (Abu Hanifa): Indeed the contractor...etc.'; the summary: The agent only has the power of disposition because he is free, sane, and adult; hence he is independent of attribution to the principal, and rights revert to him, and the need for the principal is for the transfer of the ruling of disposition to him, and the principal is eligible for the transfer of ownership of wine to him by inheritance. The picture of inheritance is that a Christian becomes Muslim and he has pigs and wine, and dies before letting the pigs loose and making the wine into vinegar, and he has a Muslim heir who owns them.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that a Muslim agent cannot appoint a non-Muslim (dhimmi) as a sub-agent because the principal cannot perform the act himself directly (e.g., a Muslim cannot sell wine or marry a Magian to a Muslim). Abu Hanifa argues that the agent acts with his own capacity, not as a mere conduit; thus the sub-agent's non-Muslim status does not matter as long as the agent could personally do the act. The text then presents three objections to the Sahibayn's qiyas (analogy to the marriage case) and refutes them, explaining that marriage rights belong solely to the principal, whereas in other transactions the agent can transfer rights. Finally, it clarifies that the agent's capacity depends on his own qualifications, not the principal's, and gives an example where a Muslim can inherit wine from a Christian convert who died before disposal.

Key Terms
الوكيل (al-wakīl): An agent appointed to carry out a transaction on behalf of another.
الموكل (al-muwakkil): The principal who appoints the agent.
الذمي (al-dhimmī): A non-Muslim living under Muslim protection (dhimma) with certain rights and obligations.
السفير (al-safīr): A messenger who merely conveys the offer and acceptance without any independent capacity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because the sub-agent must be a Muslim by default.

Key Takeaway
A Muslim agent cannot delegate to a non-Muslim sub-agent according to the relied-upon Hanafi position (Sahibayn), even though Abu Hanifa permits it based on the agent's independent capacity.
