Classification of Conditions in Sales
Expansion on types of conditions in sales and their rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify conditions in a sale into the five Hanafi categories and state the ruling (sahih or fasid) for each type.

Core Idea
Conditions in sales are valid unless they add an uncountered benefit, which constitutes riba.

In Hanafi fiqh, not every condition added to a sale invalidates it. Scholars have classified conditions into those that are inherent, harmonious, permitted by shari'a, customary, or corrupting. This lesson unpacks this classification from 'Umda al-Ri'aya, revealing the balance between contractual freedom and the prohibition of riba.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main divisions
Conditions are split into those necessitated by the contract (yaqtadīhi al-ʿaqd) and those that are not. The first division is always valid.
Necessitated conditions include transfer of ownership; they add nothing new.
2
Classify the non-necessitated conditions
They are further divided into four subtypes: harmonious (mulāʾim), shari'a-permitted (like khiyar), customary (mutaʿārif), and those that provide a benefit without consideration (which is fasid).
The first three subtypes are valid; the fourth is corrupt.
3
Understand the proof for each category
For the valid types, the text cites istihsan (juristic preference) overriding qiyas. For the corrupt type, the proof is that it leads to riba.
Istihsan is a key Hanafi principle used here to permit beneficial conditions.
4
Analyze the objection and response
The text anticipates an objection that the benefit is not homogenous to the sold item, and answers that the benefit is property (mal) that can be compensated, so its presence without compensation is riba.
This clarifies the broad Hanafi definition of riba.
5
Apply the classification to new scenarios
Using the five categories, determine the ruling for any new condition in a sale.
Practice with different examples to master the taxonomy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۳) قوله : والبيع؛ هذا أصل شامل لفروع كثيرة، واعلم أن الشرائط على أنواع : ١ . منها : ما يقتضيه العقد، والمراد به ما يجب بمجرَّدِ العقد وإن لم يشترط به. ٢. وعكسه ما لا يقتضيه العقد. مثال الأول : كشرط أن يملك البائع المبيع للمشتري ، أو كشرط أن يملك المشتري الثمن للبائع وعلى هذا القياس، وهو لا يفسد العقد ؛ لثبوته بدون الشرط، فلا يفيد الشرط إلا التأكيد. وأما ما لا يقتضيه العقد : ۱) فقد يكون ملائماً : كشرط أن يرهنه المشتري شيئاً بعينه، أو يعطيه كفيلاً، وهو لا يفسد العقد أيضاً. ٢) وقد لا يكون ملائماً للعقد ، لكنَّ الشرع ورد بجوازه كالخيار والأجــل رخصة وتيسيراً، وهو لا يفسد العقد أيضاً ؛ لأنه لما ورد الشرعُ بِهِ دل على أنه من باب المصلحة دون المفسدة، وهذا تقرير الاستحسان، أما القياس فهو أن يفسد لكونه مخالفاً لمقتضى العقد، وهو ثبوت الملك في الحال في العوضين. ٣) وقد يكون متعارفاً : كمَن اشترى بغلاً بشرط حذوه، وهو أيضاً لا يفسد كما سيأتي إن شاء الله تعالى. ١. ومنها : ما ينتفي فيه الاقتضاء والملائمة والتعارف ، وورود الشرع فيه منفعة لأحد المتعاقدين كالبيع بشرط أن يهبه المشتري أو البائع ، أو يقرضه كذا، أو فيه منفعة للمعقود عليه، وهو من أهل الاستحقاق كالعبد إذا بيع على أن يعتقه المشتري ، أو يدبره ، فالبيع فاسد ؛ لأنّ فيه زيادة عارية عن العوض، فيؤدّي إلى الربا ؛ لأنهما لما قصد المقابلة بين المبيع والثمن، خلا الشَّرطُ . عن العوض، وهو رباً؛ لأنه عبارة عن فضل خال عن العوض. فإن قيل : لا يطلق الزيادة إلا على المجانس للمزيد عليه، والمشروط منفعة، فكيف يكون ربا؟ أجيب : بأنه مال جاز أخذ العوض عليه، ولم يعوض عنه بشيء في العقد، فيكون ربا.
(3) His statement: 'And the sale...' This is a principle encompassing many branches. Know that conditions are of types: 1. Among them: what the contract necessitates. What is meant is what is obligatory by the mere contract even if not stipulated. 2. Its opposite is what the contract does not necessitate. Example of the first: like the condition that the seller makes the buyer own the sold item, or that the buyer makes the seller own the price, and similarly. It does not corrupt the contract because it is established without the condition; the condition only adds emphasis. As for what the contract does not necessitate: 1) It may be harmonious: like the condition that the buyer gives something specific as collateral, or gives a guarantor, this also does not corrupt the contract. 2) It may not be harmonious to the contract, but the Law has permitted it, like the option (khiyar) and deferment (ajal) as a concession and facilitation. This also does not corrupt the contract, because when the Law permits it, it indicates that it is of beneficial interest (maslaha) not harmful (mafsada). This is a statement of istihsan. As for qiyas, it would corrupt because it opposes the requirement of the contract, which is immediate ownership of both countervalues. 3) It may be customary: like someone buying a mule with the condition of its shoeing; this also does not corrupt, as will come. 1. Among them: what lacks both necessity, harmony, and custom, and the Law permits it for the benefit of one of the contracting parties, like a sale with the condition that the buyer or seller gives a gift, or gives a loan of such-and-such, or for the benefit of the subject of the contract, and it is deserving, like a slave sold on condition that the buyer frees him or makes him a mudabbar—then the sale is corrupt (fasid), because it contains an increase free from consideration, leading to riba. Because when they intend exchange between the sold item and the price, the condition is devoid of consideration, and it is riba, because it is an excess free from consideration. If it is said: 'Increase is only applied to what is homogenous to what is increased upon, and the conditioned is a benefit, so how can it be riba?' The answer: because it is property for which consideration is permissible to be taken, and nothing was compensated for it in the contract, so it is riba.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, expanding on conditions in sales. It classifies conditions into several types: (1) conditions inherent in the contract (like transfer of ownership) – these do not invalidate; (2) conditions not inherent but harmonious (like collateral) – also valid; (3) conditions permitted by shari'a (like khiyar al-shart or deferred payment) – valid by istihsan despite conflicting with qiyas; (4) customary conditions (like shoeing a mule) – valid; (5) conditions not inherent, not harmonious, not customary, but providing a benefit to one party without consideration (e.g., gift or loan condition) – these make the sale corrupt (fasid) because they constitute riba. The text explains that such conditions are an excess without compensation, which is the definition of riba. An objection that benefits are not homogenous to the sold item is refuted: such benefits are property (mal) that can be compensated, and when no compensation is given, it is riba. The ruling relies on istihsan (juristic preference) to permit the beneficial conditions (types 2-4) despite qiyas, and on clear prohibition to invalidate usurious conditions.

Key Terms
يقتضيه العقد (yaqtadīhi al-ʿaqd): A condition that the contract itself requires, such as transfer of ownership.
الاستحسان (al-istihsān): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas when a stronger evidence (like general good) supports a different ruling.
الربا (al-ribā): Usury or interest; any excess in an exchange without proper compensation.
ملائم (mulāʾim): Harmonious; a condition that does not contradict the nature of the contract.
متعارف (mutaʿārif): Customary; a condition commonly accepted by people in transactions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following conditions would make a sale corrupt (fasid) according to the Hanafi classification?
A) The seller stipulates that the buyer gives a collateral.
B) The buyer stipulates a three-day option (khiyar).
C) The seller stipulates that the buyer gives him a gift.
D) The buyer stipulates that the seller provides a guarantee.

Key Takeaway
Conditions in sales are classified into five categories; those that add an uncountered benefit are corrupt (fasid) due to riba, while others are valid.

Valid Conditions in a Sale: Inherent and Compatible
Defining valid conditions that align with or complement the contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between conditions that are inherent to the contract (muqtada al-aqd) and compatible conditions (mulaim), and state that both do not invalidate the sale.

Core Idea
A condition that confirms the contract's effect or harmonizes with it is valid and does not corrupt the sale.

In Hanafi fiqh, not every condition added to a contract is invalid. Some conditions are already part of the contract's essence, while others support its purpose. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains two categories of valid conditions: those inherent and those compatible. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating the validity of sales with additional stipulations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Recognize that conditions fall into either 'what the contract necessitates' (muqtada al-aqd) or 'compatible' (mulaim). The passage defines both.
2
State the ruling for each
Both types do not invalidate the sale. Inherent conditions are already required; compatible conditions do not oppose the contract's purpose.
3
Apply the classification
Given a condition, determine whether it is inherent, compatible, or neither. If inherent or compatible, the sale is valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع/ البيع الفاسد والمكروه
١ . منها : ما يقتضيه العقد، والمراد به ما يجب بمجرَّدِ العقد وإن لم يشترط به. ٢. وعكسه ما لا يقتضيه العقد.
مثال الأول : كشرط أن يملك البائع المبيع للمشتري ، أو كشرط أن يملك المشتري الثمن للبائع وعلى هذا القياس، وهو لا يفسد العقد ؛ لثبوته بدون الشرط، فلا يفيد الشرط إلا التأكيد.
وأما ما لا يقتضيه العقد :
۱) فقد يكون ملائماً : كشرط أن يرهنه المشتري شيئاً بعينه، أو يعطيه كفيلاً، وهو لا يفسد العقد أيضاً.
Book of Sale/ Invalid and Disliked Sale. 1. Among them: what the contract necessitates, meaning what is obligatory by mere contract even if not stipulated. 2. And its opposite is what the contract does not necessitate. Example of the first: like a condition that the seller makes the buyer own the sold item, or that the buyer makes the seller own the price, and on this analogy, and it does not invalidate the contract because it is established without the condition, so the condition only confirms. As for what the contract does not necessitate: 1) It may be compatible: like a condition that the buyer pawns something specific to him, or gives him a guarantor, and it also does not invalidate the contract.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage defines two categories of conditions in a sale: (1) conditions inherent in the contract itself, such as the transfer of ownership of the sold item and the price. These are already part of the contract's effect; stipulating them merely confirms what is already required and does not invalidate the sale. (2) Conditions not part of the contract but compatible with it, such as a condition that the buyer provide a pawn or a guarantor. These are also valid because they harmonize with the contract's purpose. In Hanafi fiqh, such conditions are permissible and do not render the sale invalid. This classification helps distinguish between valid and invalid conditions.

Key Terms
مقتضى العقد (muqtada al-ʿaqd): What the contract inherently requires, such as transfer of ownership, without any stipulation.
ملائم (mulāʾim): A condition that is harmonious with the contract's purpose, such as a guarantor or pawn.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Condition that the buyer provides a guarantor
C) Condition that the seller must not sell to anyone else
D) Condition that the buyer pays with a loan

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi sale contracts, conditions that are already part of the contract's effect or that support its purpose are valid and do not corrupt the sale.

Conditions Permitted by Sharia and Custom: Valid by Istihsan
Exploring two categories of conditions valid by istihsan despite qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on conditions like option and deferment in contracts and explain how istihsan overrides qiyas in these cases.

Core Idea
Conditions permitted by Sharia or custom are valid even if they contradict the contract's immediate effect, based on istihsan.

In this lesson, we examine two types of conditions that do not invalidate a sale despite conflicting with the contract's inherent requirement of immediate ownership. The author explains that Sharia-permitted conditions, such as option and deferment, are valid as concessions, while customary conditions, like buying a mule with its bit, are also upheld. The underlying legal principle is istihsan, which prioritises benefit over strict qiyas. By understanding this, you will see how Hanafi fiqh accommodates practical needs without compromising the contract's integrity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of valid conditions
The passage mentions conditions permitted by Sharia (option and deferment) and customary conditions (buying a mule with its bit). Recognise that both are valid despite opposing qiyas.
2
Understand the qiyas argument
Qiyas would invalidate these conditions because they contradict the contract's immediate transfer of ownership. Identify this as the default ruling of analogy.
Qiyas is based on the illah of contradiction to immediate ownership.
3
Apply istihsan to override qiyas
The author invokes istihsan: the Sharia's permission indicates benefit, not harm. Custom also indicates general acceptance. Istihsan is the stronger evidence that takes precedence.
Istihsan is a fundamental Hanafi tool for deriving rulings where qiyas leads to hardship.
4
State the Hanafi ruling
The sale is valid with either type of condition. This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.
The condition itself does not render the contract fasid or batil.
5
Note the practical consequence (thamarah)
In practice, a buyer can stipulate an option period or a deferred payment, and such a contract is enforceable, even though strict qiyas would have considered it invalid.
This flexibility is a mercy and a facilitation for everyday transactions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢) وقد لا يكون ملائماً للعقد ، لكنَّ الشرع ورد بجوازه كالخيار والأجــل رخصة وتيسيراً، وهو لا يفسد العقد أيضاً ؛ لأنه لما ورد الشرعُ بِهِ دل على أنه من باب المصلحة دون المفسدة، وهذا تقرير الاستحسان، أما القياس فهو أن يفسد لكونه مخالفاً لمقتضى العقد، وهو ثبوت الملك في الحال في العوضين.
٣) وقد يكون متعارفاً : كمَن اشترى بغلاً بشرط حذوه، وهو أيضاً لا يفسد كما سيأتي إن شاء الله تعالى.
2) It may not be compatible with the contract, but the Sharia has permitted it, such as option and deferment, as a concession and facilitation, and it also does not invalidate the contract; because since the Sharia has permitted it, it indicates that it is a matter of benefit without harm, and this is the determination of istihsan. As for qiyas, it would invalidate because it contradicts the requirement of the contract, which is immediate establishment of ownership in both countervalues. 3) And it may be customary: like one who buys a mule with the condition of its bit, and it also does not invalidate, as will come, God willing.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This lesson covers two more categories of conditions that do not invalidate a sale: those permitted by Sharia, such as stipulating an option period (خيار) or deferred payment (أجل), and those that are customary, like buying a mule with its bit. The author explains that although these conditions are not part of the contract's inherent requirements, Sharia has allowed them as a concession (رخصة). This is based on istihsan (juristic preference) over qiyas, which would deem such conditions invalid because they delay or alter immediate ownership. Customary conditions are also valid because they are widely accepted and do not cause dispute. In Hanafi law, custom (عرف) is a recognized source, and conditions based on custom are upheld.

Key Terms
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas in favor of a stronger evidence, such as Sharia permission or custom.
خيار (khiyār): The option to rescind or confirm a sale within a specified period.
أجل (ajal): Deferred payment term in a sale.
متعارف (mut'āraf): Customarily accepted practice among people.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because the Sharia has permitted it as a concession
C) Because it is compatible with the contract
D) Because it does not affect ownership

Key Takeaway
Conditions are valid in Hanafi contracts if permitted by Sharia or established by custom, overriding strict qiyas through istihsan.

Conditions That Invalidate Sale: Benefit Without Consideration
Analyzing conditions that lead to riba in sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify conditions that invalidate a sale due to riba and distinguish between valid and void conditions based on claimability (matalaba).

Core Idea
A condition that benefits one party without compensation invalidates the sale; benefit to a non-claimable subject matter is void but does not invalidate.

In sales, not every condition is allowed. This lesson explains when a condition in a sale violates the principle of exchange and leads to riba, and when it can be ignored without harming the contract. We will examine scenarios involving benefit to parties, subject matter, and third parties, applying the key concept of claimability (matalaba).

Guided Path
1
Identify the beneficiary of the condition
Determine who receives the benefit from the condition: one of the contracting parties, the subject matter of the sale, or a third party.
Benefit to the seller or buyer can be a gift, loan, or any non-monetary gain.
2
Check for compensation in the price
If the benefit is not offset by a corresponding increase in the price or other consideration, it constitutes an 'excess without compensation' (ziyada khaliya 'an al-'iwad).
The absence of compensation triggers riba analysis.
3
Assess claimability of the beneficiary
If the benefit goes to the subject matter, ask: Can the subject matter demand enforcement of the condition? Only humans (or legal persons) can claim; animals cannot.
Claimability (matalaba) is the decisive factor in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Apply the ruling
If the condition benefits a party or a claimable subject matter without compensation, the sale is fasid (invalid). If the condition benefits a non-claimable subject matter, the condition is void but the sale remains valid.
Exception: benefit to a third party has ikhtilaf; the preferred view is non-invalidation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١. ومنها : ما ينتفي فيه الاقتضاء والملائمة والتعارف ، وورود الشرع فيه منفعة لأحد المتعاقدين كالبيع بشرط أن يهبه المشتري أو البائع ، أو يقرضه كذا، أو فيه منفعة للمعقود عليه، وهو من أهل الاستحقاق كالعبد إذا بيع على أن يعتقه المشتري ، أو يدبره ، فالبيع فاسد ؛ لأنّ فيه زيادة عارية عن العوض، فيؤدّي إلى الربا ؛ لأنهما لما قصد المقابلة بين المبيع والثمن، خلا الشَّرطُ . عن العوض، وهو رباً؛ لأنه عبارة عن فضل خال عن العوض.
فإن قيل : لا يطلق الزيادة إلا على المجانس للمزيد عليه، والمشروط منفعة، فكيف يكون ربا؟
فيكون ربا.
أجيب : بأنه مال جاز أخذ العوض عليه، ولم يعوض عنه بشيء في العقد،
أو يكون فيه منفعة للمعفو عليه، لكنه ليس من أهل الاستحقاق ، كمَن اشترى دابَّةٌ بشرط أن لا يبيعها أو إرسالها في المرعى، فالبيع جائز، والشرط باطل ؛ لأنه انعدمت المطالبة من جهة الدابة ، فلا يؤدّي إلى الربا، ولا إلى المنازعة، وهو ظاهر من المذهب. وروي عن أبي يوسف له أنه يبطل به البيع ؛ لأنّ في هذا الشرط ضرراً على المشتري، من حيث يتعدَّرُ عليه التصرُّف في ملكه، والشرط الذي فيه ضرر كالشرط الذي فيه منفعة لأحد المتعاقدين، ولكنا نقول : لا معتبر بالشرط إلا بالمطالبة، وهي تتوجه بالمنفعة في الشرط دون الضرر. كذا في «النهاية».
٢. أو يكون فيه منفعة للأجنبي: كبيع بشرط أن يقرض البائع أو المشتري فلاناً كذا : وفيه اختلاف المشايخ ، ذكرَ القُدُورِيُّ الله أنه يفسد، واختار المصنف له تبعاً لصاحب «الهداية» (۱) عدم الفساد، والتحقيق في «البحر الرائق»).

أجيب في صورة اشتراط ما يقتضيه العقدُ ليس بشرط حيث أفاد ما أفاده العقد
فإن قيل : إذا لم يفسد الشرط المتعارف العقد يلزم أن يكون العرف قاضياً على الحديث.
أجيب : بأنه ليس بقاض عليه، بل على القياس ؛ لأنَّ الحديث معلول بوقوع النزاع المخرج للعقد عن المعقود به، وهو قطع المنازعة، والعرف ينفي النزاع ، فكان موافقاً لمعنى الحديث ، فلم يبق من الموانع إلا القياس على ما لا عرف فيه بجامع كونه شرطاً، والعرف قاض عليه. كذا في «المنح».
1. Among them: that in which necessity, compatibility, and custom are absent, and Sharia sees benefit for one of the contracting parties, like a sale with a condition that the buyer or seller gifts something, or lends something, or there is benefit to the subject matter, and it is eligible for rights, like a slave sold on condition that the buyer frees him or makes him mudabbar, so the sale is invalid; because there is an increase devoid of compensation, leading to riba; since they intended exchange between the sold item and the price, the condition is devoid of compensation, and it is riba because it is an excess devoid of compensation. If it is said: increase is only applied to the homogenous of the increased, and the condition is a benefit, so how can it be riba? It is riba. Answer: because it is wealth for which taking compensation is permissible, and nothing was compensated for it in the contract. Or there is benefit to the subject matter, but it is not eligible for rights, like one who buys an animal with a condition that he not sell it or let it graze in pasture, so the sale is valid and the condition is void; because claim (for fulfillment) from the animal is non-existent, so it does not lead to riba or dispute, and that is apparent from the madhhab. It is narrated from Abu Yusuf that it invalidates the sale; because this condition harms the buyer by preventing him from disposing of his property, and a condition that harms is like a condition that benefits one of the contracting parties. But we say: consideration in a condition is only through claimability, and that applies to benefit in the condition, not to harm. Thus in al-Nihaya. 2. Or there is benefit to a third party: like a sale with a condition that the seller or buyer lends to so-and-so such-and-such; and there is disagreement among the mashayikh. Al-Quduri mentioned that it invalidates, and the author chose following the author of al-Hidayah non-invalidation, and the verification is in al-Bahr al-Raiq. If it is said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) prohibited a sale and a condition, and its absoluteness precludes permissibility even in conditions that the contract necessitates. Answer in the case of stipulating what the contract necessitates: it is not a condition because it conveys what the contract conveys. If it is said: If a customary condition does not invalidate the contract, then custom would be overriding the hadith. Answer: It does not override the hadith, rather it overrides qiyas; because the hadith is subject to the occurrence of dispute that removes the contract from its intended purpose, and that is preventing dispute, and custom negates dispute, so it is in agreement with the meaning of the hadith; so no obstacle remains except qiyas on that which has no custom, by the commonality of being a condition, and custom overrides it. Thus in al-Munh.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This extensive lesson deals with conditions that invalidate a sale because they provide a benefit to one party without any corresponding increase in the price or compensation. These are conditions where the contract's essence, compatibility, custom, and Sharia permission are all absent. The author identifies three main categories: (1) benefit to one of the contracting parties, such as a condition that the buyer gives a gift or loan to the seller – these invalidate the sale because they constitute an excess (زيادة) without consideration, leading to riba. (2) Benefit to the subject matter if it is eligible for rights, like a slave to be freed – also invalid. (3) Benefit to the subject matter if it is not eligible for rights, like an animal with a condition not to sell it – in this case, the sale is valid but the condition is void, because the animal cannot claim enforcement. This is the Hanafi position, although Abu Yusuf held that such a condition invalidates the sale due to harm to the buyer. The author refutes this by stating that validity of a condition depends on whether it can be claimed (مطالبة), which applies to benefits, not harm. (4) Benefit to a third party, like a condition that one of the parties lends to someone else – here there is ikhtilaf: al-Quduri says it invalidates, but the author (following al-Hidayah) says it does not invalidate. Finally, the lesson addresses two objections to the entire framework: (a) the hadith 'the Prophet forbade a sale and a condition' seems to invalidate even conditions that are part of the contract. The answer is that such conditions are not really conditions since they merely reaffirm what the contract already provides. (b) If customary conditions are valid, does that mean custom overrides the hadith? No, custom does not override the hadith; it overrides the qiyas that would otherwise invalidate such conditions, because custom prevents the very dispute that the hadith aims to avoid.

Key Terms
ربا (ribā): An excess without consideration in a contract of exchange.
زيادة خالية عن العوض (ziyāda khāliya ʿan al-ʿiwaḍ): An excess that is not compensated for, leading to riba.
أهل الاستحقاق (ahl al-istiḥqāq): A person who has the capacity to claim a right, such as a slave who can be freed.
مطالبة (muṭālaba): The ability to demand fulfillment of a condition in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The sale is invalid and the condition is valid
B) The sale is valid and the condition is void
C) Both sale and condition are valid
D) Both are invalid

Key Takeaway
Conditions that provide an uncompensated benefit to a claimable entity invalidate the sale; otherwise the condition is void but the sale is valid.

Conditions Benefiting Non-Eligible Parties or Third Parties in Sales
Explaining which conditions in a sale invalidate it and which do not, with Hanafi disputes and custom arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between conditions that invalidate a sale and those that do not, applying the Hanafi principles of the possibility of claim (mutalaba) and custom (urf).

Core Idea
A condition that cannot be claimed does not invalidate the sale; custom and dispute prevention govern validity.

In Hanafi sales law, not every extra condition corrupts the contract. The key is whether the condition leads to dispute or involves a benefit that one contractor can claim. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya examines two scenarios: conditions benefiting a non-eligible party (like an animal) and conditions benefiting a third party (like a loan). The author navigates internal Hanafi differences and aligns custom with the intent of hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the condition
Determine the sale contract and the specific condition added. Is the condition for a benefit to a party that can claim it?
2
Determine if the condition's beneficiary is eligible to claim
If the beneficiary is a non-eligible party (e.g., an animal), the condition is void but the sale is valid because no claim arises. If the beneficiary is a human third party, consider the ikhtilaf.
3
Apply the principle of possibility of claim (mutalaba)
Hanafis emphasize that the only relevant factor is whether a claim can be made to enforce the condition. If not, no dispute occurs.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Yusuf argued that a condition harming the buyer (e.g., not to sell) should invalidate, but Abu Hanifa held that without claim, the sale stands. The mufta bihi follows Abu Hanifa.
5
Evaluate the role of custom (urf)
Customary conditions that do not cause dispute are valid even if not part of the essential contract. Custom does not override the hadith but aligns with its rationale.

وفي «المصباح»: «حذوتُ النَّعل بالنَّعل : قدَّرتُها بها وقطعتها على مثالها
Or there is benefit for the one being forgiven, but he is not eligible for entitlement, like one who buys an animal with the condition that he not sell it or send it to pasture; the sale is valid, and the condition is void because the claim from the animal is nonexistent, so it does not lead to riba or dispute, and this is apparent in the madhhab. It is reported from Abu Yusuf that the sale is invalidated by it because this condition harms the buyer, as it prevents him from disposing of his property, and a condition that contains harm is like a condition that contains benefit for one of the contractors. But we say: the only consideration in a condition is the claim, and the claim is directed by the benefit in the condition, not by the harm. Thus in al-Nihaya. 2. Or there is benefit for a third party: like a sale with the condition that the seller or buyer lend so-and-so such-and-such; the early scholars differed. al-Quduri stated that it corrupts, and the author chose, following the author of al-Hidaya, that it does not corrupt; and the verification is in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. If it is said: The Messenger of Allah forbade a sale and a condition, and its absolute nature implies non-permissibility even in a condition necessitated by the contract. The answer is that stipulating what the contract necessitates is not a condition, since it conveys what the contract conveys. If it is said: If the customary condition does not corrupt the contract, then custom would override the hadith. The answer: it does not override it, but rather analogy; because the hadith is defective due to the occurrence of dispute that removes the contract from its purpose, and custom removes dispute, so it agrees with the meaning of the hadith; thus no obstacle remains except analogy to that which has no custom, by the commonality of being a condition, and custom overrides it. Thus in al-Minh. The Book of Sales / Corrupt and Disliked Sales. Contrary to a condition not required by the contract, in which there is benefit for one of the two contractors, or for the sold item that is entitled to it. [1] Contrary to a condition not required by the contract, in which there is benefit for one of the two contractors, or for the sold item that is entitled to it i.e. the sold item is eligible for benefit by being a human; so it becomes clear that his statement 'no benefit in it for anyone' he meant for any of the two contractors, and the sold item is entitled to benefit, so if the benefit is for the sold item that is not entitled to benefit, like the condition that the sold animal not be sold, this condition does not corrupt. [{] Like the condition that the seller cut it and sew it as a garment, or shape it as a sandal, or give him a share. [1] His statement: a condition not required by the contract, and not suitable, and the Sharia has not permitted it, and it is not customary; the benefit of these restrictions is apparent; for if the condition is suitable for the contract, or the Sharia permitted it, or it is customary, then the sale is valid as you saw earlier. [2] His statement: eligible for entitlement of benefit, i.e. one who is entitled to a right from another by being human. [3] His statement: like the condition... etc., i.e. as if one bought a cloth with the condition that the seller cut it and sew it as a garment, or bought leather with the condition that the seller shape it as a sandal for the buyer, or bought a sandal on condition that the seller make a strap for it. These are examples of a condition not required by the contract with benefit for the buyer, so it corrupts; because if part of the price is in exchange for the stipulated work, it is a hire stipulated in a sale; and if nothing is in exchange for it, it is a loan stipulated in it, and the prohibition of two transactions in one has been reported. [ ] His statement: or shape it: making it into a sandal and thong? as in al-Sirah: one says: hadhan nal haza or hazaa: measured the sandal; similarly: hadhan qadha bi qadha; and hadha li na'lan: he made a sandal; and hadha al-rajul na'lan: the man made a sandal. As in Muntaha al-Arab. And in al-Misbah: 'hadaun na'la bi na'l: I measured it by it and cut it according to its example.'
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, a commentary on al-Wiqaya, discusses conditions in sales and their effect on validity. The author first addresses a condition that benefits a non-eligible party, like an animal that cannot claim compensation. If someone buys an animal on condition that he not sell it or keep it in pasture, the sale is valid but the condition is void because the animal cannot enforce the condition, preventing dispute and riba. Abu Yusuf argued it should invalidate due to hardship on the buyer, but the Hanafi position is that only the possibility of claim matters, not harm. The second case is a condition benefiting a third party, e.g., a loan to someone. Here, there is ikhtilaf: al-Quduri deemed it invalid, but the author follows al-Hidaya in upholding the sale, as long as it doesn't cause dispute. Then an objection from the hadith 'forbidding sale and condition' is raised; the response is that if the condition is customary or does not negate the contract's purpose, it's permissible. Custom does not override the hadith but aligns with its rationale of preventing dispute. Finally, on page 149, the text contrasts conditions that are not part of the contract and benefit one party—these invalidate the sale because they constitute an additional contract (hire or loan) bundled with the sale, violating the prohibition of 'two transactions in one.' Examples include a seller tailoring cloth or making sandals. The passage emphasizes that custom, lack of dispute, and alignment with the contract's purpose determine validity, not mere benefit or harm.

Key Terms
شرط (shart): A stipulation or condition added to a sale contract beyond its essential elements.
مطالبة (mutalaba): The legal right to claim a benefit or enforcement of a condition.
عرف (urf): Customary practice that influences the validity of conditions in contracts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a sale where someone buys an animal with the condition that he not sell it?
A) The sale is invalid because the condition restricts the buyer's ownership.
B) The sale is valid but the condition is void because the animal cannot enforce it.
C) The sale is valid and the condition is binding.
D) The sale is corrupted because it benefits the buyer.

Key Takeaway
A condition that brings benefit to a party unable to claim it does not invalidate the sale; custom and prevention of dispute are key.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Conditions that Invalidate a Sale in Hanafi Law
Ruling with proof on conditions that invalidate a sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which conditions in a sale invalidate it (fasid) and distinguish the valid exception based on customary practice (istihsan).

Core Idea
A sale is invalid if it includes a condition that benefits a party or a human slave and is not required by the contract or customary.

Sales often include conditions that serve the interests of one party. However, not all conditions are permissible. The Hanafi school strictly limits the validity of conditions to those that are inherent to the contract, permitted by revelation, or widely customary. In this lesson, we examine the key examples and the role of istihsan in preserving customary practices.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition and its effect
The text defines a harmful condition as one not required by the contract, not compatible, not permitted by revelation, and not customary. Your task is to recognize such a condition and determine if it provides benefit to a contractor or to a human slave.
If the benefit is for a non-human object (e.g., animal), the condition does not invalidate.
2
Apply the general rule: sale becomes fasid
Once you identify a condition that meets the criteria, the ruling is that the sale becomes fasid (invalid). Understand why: it amounts to two transactions in one (sale plus hiring or loan) which is prohibited.
The prohibition of 'safqatayn fi safqah' is the primary textual proof.
3
Recognize the exception: custom overrides via istihsan
The text provides an exception for making a strap for sandals. Although the condition benefits the buyer, it is valid due to widespread custom (ta'amul) and istihsan. Note that custom is considered practical consensus that outweighs analogy.
This does not apply to all custom; only established, widespread practices.
4
Analyze the slave-related conditions
Conditions benefiting a human slave (emancipation, tadbir, kitaba) also invalidate because they bind the buyer to a specific disposition. However, if the buyer actually fulfills the condition (e.g., emancipates), the sale becomes valid after the fact.
This nuance shows that the condition's effect can cure the initial invalidity.
5
Summarize the mufta bihi ruling
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that such conditions render the sale fasid unless overridden by custom. This is the standard Hanafi view from the Zahir al-Riwayah.
No intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf is recorded in this text.

But if the buyer emancipates him after purchasing him with the condition of emancipation, the sale is valid such that the price becomes obligatory, because the condition of emancipation in itself is not compatible with the contract—since it requires freedom in disposition, not obligation as a duty—but in terms of its ruling it is compatible, because emancipation is the ultimate end of ownership.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement from the super-commentary 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah on Sharh al-Wiqayah. It discusses the ruling on conditions in sales that invalidate the contract (tujʿaluhu fasidan). The central rule is: a condition that is not required by the contract (i.e., not from the essence of the sale), not compatible with the contract, not permitted by revelation, and not customary—if it provides a benefit to either party (buyer or seller) or to the sold item when the sold item is a human capable of claiming benefit—then the sale becomes fasid (invalid). The text clarifies the phrase 'or for the sold item that deserves it' means the sold item is a human being (Adamī) who can legally seek benefit. If the benefit is for a non-human object (like an animal), the condition does not invalidate the sale, e.g., stipulating that a horse not be sold. Several examples are given: (1) Buyer buys cloth on condition that the seller cuts and sews it into a qaba (garment)—benefit to buyer. (2) Buyer buys leather on condition that the seller makes sandals for him—benefit to buyer. (3) Buyer buys sandals on condition that the seller makes a strap (shirāk) for them—this is analogous to the previous but the commentary adds a special ruling for sandals. The reasoning: if part of the price is in exchange for the labor, it amounts to a hiring (ijārah) embedded in a sale, which is like two transactions in one (ṣafqah fī ṣafqah), prohibited by hadith. If no part of the price is for the labor, it is like a loan (iʿārah) embedded in a sale, also prohibited. The text then makes an exception for the sandal condition: by istiḥsān (juristic preference), it is valid because of widespread custom (taʿāmul), which is considered a form of practical consensus (ijmāʿ ʿamalī) and avoids hardship (ḥaraj). Analogy (qiyās) would not allow it, but custom overrides qiyās. Further examples: (4) Seller stipulates to use the sold slave for a month—benefit to seller; this invalidates. (5) Buyer stipulates to emancipate (ʿitq), make mudabbar (tadbīr), or make a kitāba contract (mukātabah) on the sold slave—benefit to the slave, who is eligible for benefit; these also invalidate because they bind the buyer to a specific disposition contrary to the general freedom of ownership. However, an important nuance is added: if the buyer actually emancipates the slave after the purchase under such an invalid condition, the sale becomes valid and the price is due, because the condition's effect (emancipation) is compatible with the end of ownership, even though the condition itself was not compatible. This shows that fulfillment of the condition after the fact can cure the invalidity.

Key Terms
شرط لا يقتضيه العقد (shart lā yaqtaḍīhi al-ʿaqd): A condition that is not from the essence of the contract nor compatible with it; it is not permitted by revelation and not customary.
صفقة في صفقة (ṣafqah fī ṣafqah): Two transactions combined into one, e.g., a sale with a hiring or loan condition. This is prohibited by hadith.
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference based on custom (taʿāmul) and practical consensus, overriding qiyas.
تعامل (taʿāmul): Customary practice among people, considered a form of practical consensus (ijmāʿ ʿamalī) that takes precedence over qiyas.
نعل (naʿl): Sandal or shoe; the text specifically discusses a condition of making a strap for sandals, which is valid by istiḥsān.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The sale is valid because the condition is customary.
B) The sale is fasid because the condition benefits the seller and is not required by the contract.
C) The sale is batil because it involves riba.
D) The sale is makruh tanzihi but valid.

Key Takeaway
A sale is fasid when it includes a stipulation that is not inherent to the contract, not customary, and benefits a party or a human slave; however, established custom can override through istihsan.

Conditions in Sale: The Case of Freeing a Slave and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Explains rulings on conditions in sale of a slave: sandal, usage, freeing, tadbir, kitabah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on conditions not implied by a sale contract and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute on the effect of actual freeing after a conditional sale.

Core Idea
A condition not required by the contract invalidates the sale (fasid), but actual fulfillment of the condition may cure the defect under Abu Hanifa's view.

This passage from `Umdat al-Ri`aya deals with the validity of conditions in a sale that are not implied by the contract. It distinguishes between conditions benefiting the buyer, seller, or the sold item, and uses the case of making a sandal (allowed by istihsan), using the slave (invalid), and freeing/tadbir/kitabah (invalid but with a dispute on cure). The key legal problem is whether a subsequent act can rectify a contract that was initially invalid due to an impermissible condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
State that conditions not implied by the contract (shart la yaqtadih al-'aqd) generally invalidate the sale, making it fasid. The exception is the sandal condition, allowed by istihsan due to customary practice.
The general rule is based on the principle that the contract should not impose obligations beyond the exchange.
2
Categorize the conditions
Classify the conditions in the text: (a) benefiting the buyer – sandal (allowed), (b) benefiting the seller – usage for a month (invalid), (c) benefiting the sold item – freeing, tadbir, kitabah (invalid initially but potentially curable).
Conditions benefiting a party outside the contract are problematic because they add a non-consensual element.
3
Focus on the freeing condition
Notice the intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa holds that if the buyer actually frees the slave after the conditional sale, the sale becomes valid and the price is due. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) maintain the sale remains invalid and the buyer pays market value.
The dispute hinges on whether a subsequent act can cure a defective contract.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's reasoning
Abu Hanifa argues that the condition's form is defective, but its ruling (freeing) harmonizes with the contract because freeing terminates ownership, which is the ultimate purpose of ownership. The actual freeing realizes the harmony, overcoming the initial defect.
This is an application of istihsan: preferring the spirit over the letter.
5
Contrast with the Sahibayn's reasoning
The Sahibayn argue that a contract that is void ab initio cannot be cured by subsequent acts. They analogize to destroying the slave by death or sale: if the slave is destroyed, the sale remains void. Freeing is just another form of destruction of the seller's right.
Their view is stricter on contractual certainty.
6
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that of Abu Hanifa, as supported by Al-Marghinani in al-Kifayah. The sale becomes valid upon actual freeing, and the buyer pays the agreed price.
This ruling is preferred because it aligns with istihsan and avoids hardship.

وصح في النعل استحساناً أو يستخدمه شهراً ، أو يعتقَهُ، أو يُدَبِّرَهُ ، أو يُكاتِبَهُ أي يجعل للنعل شراكاً، هذا نظير شرط لا يقتضيه العقد وفيه نفع المشتري، (وصح في النَّعل استحساناً : إنّما يجوز في النَّعل للتعامل" والقياس أنه لا يجوز". (أو يستخدمه شهراً) : أي يستخدمه البائع شهراً، وهذا نظير شرط لا يقتضيه العقد وفيه نفع البائع. (أو يعتقه، أو يُدَبِّرَهُ ، أو يُكاتِبَهُ) : هذا نظير شرط لا يقضيه العقـد وفـيـه نـفـع للمبيع، وهو أهل لاستحقاق النفع. وقدرها». انتهى
قوله شراكاً : شراك بالكسر ، بند نعل ازددال، شرك أشرك جمع، وأشركت نعلي : أى جعلتُ لها شراكاً، وشركتها كذلك. كذا في «الصراح». قوله : للتعامل؛ لأنَّ التِّعاملُ يرجُحُ على القياس ؛ لكونه إجماعاً عملياً ؛ ولأنَّ في الخروج عنه حرجاً بيناً. قوله : لا يجوز؛ لما فيه من النفع للمشتري مع كون العقد لا يقتضيه. قوله : أو يستخدمه شهراً ؛ يعني أو كمن باع عبداً على أن يستخدمه البائعُ شهراً مثلاً ، فهذا شرط لا يقتضيه العقد، وفيه منفعة للبائع فيفسد ؛ ولأنه صفقة في صفقة، وهو منهي عنه، وتقريره مرَّ آنفاً. قوله : هذا؛ أي كلُّ واحدٍ من الإعتاق والتدبير والكتابة نظير شرط لا يقتضيه العقد؛ لأنَّ اقتضاء العقدِ أن يكون المشتري بخيراً في جميع التصرفات في المشترى ، لا أن يكون ملزماً على تصرُّف خاص كالإعتاق أو التدبير أو الكتابة، والشرط يقتضي ذلك الإلزام، وفيه نفع للمبيع. فإن الفن يعجبه أن لا تتداوله الأيدي، وهو أهل لاستحقاق النفع على غيره؛ لكونه آدميّاً، فيفسد البيع، فلو أعتقه المشتري بعدما اشتراه بشرط العتق، صح البيعُ حتى يجب الثمن، لأنَّ شرط العتق من حيث ذاته لا يلائم العقد؛ لاقتضائه الإطلاق في التصرف، لا الإلزام وجوباً، لكن من حيث حكمه يلائمه؛ لأنَّ العتق منتهى الملك، فإنَّ الملك في الآدمي يثبت إلى العتق، والشيء يتقرر بانتهائه. وكل ما هو مقرر الشيء مصحح له؛ ولهذا لو اشترى عبداً فأعتقه، ثم علم بعیب به رجع المشتري إلى البائع بالنقصان، بخلاف ما لو باعه فإذا استهلكه فقد تقرر الفساد بوجودِ صورة الشرط دون الحكم، فإذا أعتقه تحققت الملائمة بحكم العتق، وهو انتهاء الملك به، فترجَّحَ جانبُ الجواز فكانَ الحال قبل ذلك موقوفاً بين أن يبقى فاسداً كما كان، أو ينقلب إلى الجواز بالإعتاق. كذا في «الكفاية»). وقالا : يبقى فاسداً كما كان، حتى يجب عليه القيمة؛ لأنَّ البيع قد وقع فاسداً فلا ينقلب جائزاً، كما إذا أتلف بوجه آخر؛ من الموت والقتل والبيع.
And it is valid regarding the sandal by istihsan, or he uses him for a month, or he frees him, or he makes him mudabbar, or he makes him mukatab, meaning he makes a strap for the sandal. This is analogous to a condition not required by the contract and in it is benefit for the buyer. (And it is valid regarding the sandal by istihsan: it is only permissible regarding the sandal due to customary practice, and by analogy it is not permissible.) (Or he uses him for a month): meaning the seller uses him for a month, and this is analogous to a condition not required by the contract and in it is benefit for the seller. (Or he frees him, or makes him mudabbar, or makes him mukatab): this is analogous to a condition not required by the contract and in it is benefit for the sold item, and it is worthy of deserving benefit. And its measure ended. He said "strap": strap with kasrah, the lace of a sandal... He said: for customary practice; because customary practice outweighs analogy because it is a practical consensus, and because departing from it involves manifest hardship. He said: it is not permissible; because of the benefit for the buyer while the contract does not necessitate it. He said: or he uses him for a month; meaning like one who sells a slave on condition that the seller uses him for a month, for example. So this is a condition not required by the contract, and in it is benefit for the seller, so it invalidates; and because it is a transaction within a transaction, which is prohibited. He said: this; meaning each one of freeing, tadbir, and kitabah is analogous to a condition not required by the contract. Because what the contract requires is that the buyer have free choice in all dispositions regarding the purchased item, not that he be obligated to a specific disposition like freeing, tadbir, or kitabah. The condition necessitates that obligation, and in it is benefit for the sold item. For the art is pleased that hands do not circulate it, and it is worthy of deserving benefit from others because it is human, so the sale becomes invalid. If the buyer frees him after purchasing him with the condition of freeing, the sale becomes valid such that the price is obligatory, because the condition of freeing in itself does not harmonize with the contract due to its requiring absolute disposal, not obligatory obligation, but from the perspective of its ruling it harmonizes because freeing is the termination of ownership. For ownership in a human being is established until freeing, and a thing is established by its termination. And everything that establishes a thing validates it. For this reason, if one buys a slave and frees him, then discovers a defect in him, the buyer returns to the seller for the reduction, unlike if he sold him. So if he consumes him, the invalidity is established by the existence of the form of the condition without the ruling. When he frees him, the harmony is realized by the ruling of freeing, which is the termination of ownership through it, so the side of permissibility prevails. The situation before that was suspended between remaining invalid as it was, or turning into permissibility through freeing. Thus in al-Kifayah. And they two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: it remains invalid as it was, so he must pay the value, because the sale occurred invalidly, so it does not turn into valid, just as if he destroyed it in another way: by death, killing, or sale.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage addresses conditions in a sale contract that are not implied by the contract (شرط لا يقتضيه العقد). The base text (matn) lists several conditions and their rulings. The commentator expands each condition and explains the underlying reasoning, including a significant intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of actual freeing after a conditional sale. The ruling on making a sandal (na'l) for the slave is allowed by istihsan (juristic preference) due to customary practice, though qiyas (analogy) would prohibit it. Conditions that benefit the seller (using the slave for a month) or benefit the sold item (freeing, tadbir, kitabah) invalidate the sale because they impose an obligation beyond the contract. However, a key exception is raised: if the buyer actually frees the slave after purchasing him with a condition to free, does the sale become valid? Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the sale becomes valid and the price is due, because the act of freeing harmonizes with the contract (since ownership ends with freeing), and this harmony overcomes the initial defect. The two companions (Sahibayn) disagree, maintaining that the sale remains invalid and the buyer must pay the market value (قيمة) because the contract was void from the start. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school is that of Imam Abu Hanifa, as indicated by the preference for istihsan and the analogy of the buyer discovering a defect after freeing.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference: departing from analogy (qiyas) in favor of a more equitable ruling, based on consensus, custom, or necessity.
شرط لا يقتضيه العقد (shart la yaqtadih al-'aqd): A condition that is not implied by the nature of the contract and imposes an additional obligation or benefit not inherent in the exchange.
التعامل (al-ta'amul): Customary practice among people; used here to justify the permissibility of the sandal condition despite qiyas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens to a sale of a slave with a condition that the buyer frees him, if the buyer actually frees him?
A) The sale remains invalid and the buyer must pay the market value.
B) The sale becomes valid and the buyer pays the agreed price.
C) The sale is void from the start and no payment is needed.
D) The seller has the option to rescind or confirm the sale.

Key Takeaway
A condition not implied by the sale contract generally invalidates it, but the actual fulfillment of the freeing condition can cure the defect under Abu Hanifa's view, which is the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Sale of a Slave Woman Except Her Pregnancy: Invalidity of Excluding Dependent Parts
Ruling on the invalidity of selling a slave woman while excluding her fetus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that items which cannot be independently contracted cannot be validly excepted (istithna'), and explain why such an exception invalidates the sale.

Core Idea
The fetus is a dependent part (tab'); excluding it from sale is a condition not required by the contract, thus invalid.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on selling a slave woman while excluding her fetus. The text establishes a key legal principle: anything that cannot be the independent object of a contract cannot be validly excepted. Using the analogy of animal limbs, the commentator explains that the fetus, like a limb, is a dependent part automatically included in the sale. Attempting to exclude it inserts an invalid condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine the legal act being discussed and its hukm (classification). The act is selling a slave woman with an exception (istithna') of her pregnancy. The ruling is that the sale is invalid (fasid).
The ruling is explicitly stated in the text: the condition invalidates the sale.
2
State the governing principle
Extract the universal rule (al-asl) from the passage: 'Whatever cannot be independently contracted cannot be validly excepted from the contract.' This is because such items are dependent parts (tab') and are automatically included.
The principle is derived from the nature of dependent parts.
3
Trace the qiyas analogy
Understand the analogical reasoning: The fetus is compared to the limbs of an animal. Just as limbs cannot be independently contracted while attached, the fetus cannot be independently contracted because it is physically connected to the mother and considered a part of her.
The illa (effective cause) is physical connection and dependence.
4
Confirm the invalidation mechanism
Conclude that the exception is a 'condition not required by the contract' (shart la yaqtadihi al-'aqd). Such conditions invalidate the contract per the Hanafi rule, making the sale fasid.
The commentator explicitly states: 'every condition not required by the contract invalidates.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبيع أمة إلا حملها عطف على شرط لا يقتضيه العقد، والأصل في ذلك أن كل ما لا يصح إفراده بالعقدِ لا يصح استثناؤه من العقد، فإنَّ كلَّ ما لا يصح إفراده بالعقد فإنّه من توابع الشَّيء، فيكون داخلاً في المبيع تبعاً له، فاستثناؤه من العقد شرط لا يقتضيه العقد فيكون مفسداً.
قوله : والأصل؛ أي الأمر الكلي في مثل هذه المسألة أنَّ كلَّ ما لا يصح إفرادُهُ بالعقد لا يصح استثناءه من العقد، فإنّه من توابع الشيء فيكون داخلاً في المبيع، فلا حاجة إلى إيراد العقد، وبالاستثناء يعلم أنه مقصود، والحمل من هذا القبيل؛ لأنه بمنزلة أطراف الحيوان؛ لاتصاله به خلقة، فإنَّ الجنين ما دام في بطن أمه يكون في حكم جزء منها كاليد والرجل، وأطراف الحيوان لا يقبلُ العقد مقصودة، فلا تكون مقصودة بالاستثناء. فإن الاستثناء التصرف فيما يدخل تحت اللفظ قصداً لا تبعاً، والأطراف بمنزلة الأوصاف فيكون دخولها تبعاً، فاستثناؤه : أي الحمل من العقد أي عقد مع الأمة شرط لا يقتضيه العقد، وكلُّ شرط لا يقتضيه العقد مفسد، فيكون هذا الشرط مفسداً، فتدبَّر، وهاهنا مباحث لطيفة مودعة في المبسوطات.
And the sale of a slave woman except her pregnancy is an addition to (the category of) a condition not required by the contract. The principle in this is that whatever cannot be independently included in a contract cannot be validly excepted from the contract. For whatever cannot be independently included in a contract is among the appurtenances of the thing, so it is included in the sold item as a dependent. So excepting it from the contract is a condition not required by the contract, thus it invalidates. He said: "and the principle"; meaning the universal rule in such a matter is that whatever cannot be independently made the object of a contract cannot be validly excepted from the contract, because it is among the appurtenances of the thing and thus included in the sold item. There is no need to mention it in the contract, and by excepting it, it becomes known that it is intended. The pregnancy is of this category because it is like the limbs of an animal due to its connection by creation. For as long as the fetus is in its mother's womb, it is in the ruling of a part of her, like the hand and foot. The limbs of an animal are not validly made the object of a contract intending them independently, so they are not intended by exception. For exception is a disposition regarding what enters under the wording intentionally, not dependently. Limbs are like attributes, so their inclusion is dependent. So excepting it, i.e., the pregnancy, from the contract, i.e., the contract of the slave woman, is a condition not required by the contract, and every condition not required by the contract invalidates, so this condition invalidates. So reflect, and here there are subtle discussions deposited in the detailed works.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage rules on the sale of a slave woman with the exception of her pregnancy (بيع أمة إلا حملها). The commentator begins by stating that this is another instance of a condition not required by the contract (شرط لا يقتضيه العقد). The general principle (الأصل) is: anything that cannot be made the independent subject of a contract cannot be validly excepted from a contract. The reason is that such a thing is a dependent part (تبع) of the main object; it is automatically included in the sale as a follower. Excepting it introduces a condition that conflicts with the nature of the contract. The commentator likens the fetus to the limbs of a living being (أطراف الحيوان). Just as you cannot sell an animal excluding its leg (because the leg is part of the animal), you cannot sell a slave woman excluding her fetus, because the fetus is considered a part of her while in the womb. Limbs and fetus are not independently saleable; they are like attributes (أوصاف) that are included dependently. Therefore, such an exception is a condition not required by the contract and invalidates the sale. The passage also hints at deeper discussions in larger works (المبسوطات).

Key Terms
استثناء (istithna'): Exception: excluding something from the sold item that would otherwise be included by virtue of being a dependent part.
تبع (tab'): Dependent part: something that follows the main object in inclusion, not independently saleable.
أطراف الحيوان (atraf al-hayawan): Limbs of an animal: used as analogy for the fetus being a part of the mother.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school consider the sale of a slave woman 'except her pregnancy' invalid?
A) Because pregnancy is uncertain and cannot be known.
B) Because the fetus is a dependent part (tab') and cannot be independently contracted, so the exception is a condition not required by the contract.
C) Because the sale of a pregnant woman is prohibited entirely.
D) Because the buyer must intend to buy both mother and child.

Key Takeaway
The sale of a slave woman while excluding her pregnancy is invalid because the fetus is a dependent part (tab') that cannot be independently contracted, and the exception constitutes an invalid condition.

Sale of a Slave Girl Except Her Pregnancy: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on sale with exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling a slave girl while excepting her pregnancy, explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and apply the principle of istithna' to contractual exceptions of inseparable parts.

Core Idea
Excluding an inseparable part invalidates the sale, but Abu Hanifa allows cure by freeing the child.

In Hanafi fiqh, the sale of a slave girl with an exception of her pregnancy raises a critical question: can an inseparable part of a human being be excluded from a contract? The passage presents a general principle that anything which cannot be sold independently cannot be validly excepted. The pregnancy, being like a limb, renders the sale initially invalid (fasid). However, Imam Abu Hanifa offers a unique remedy: if the buyer later frees the child, the sale becomes valid. The Two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) object, maintaining that the original invalidity persists. This lesson dissects the reasoning behind each view and identifies the relied-upon position for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
State the general principle
Identify the rule: Anything that cannot be sold independently cannot be validly excepted from a sale. The exception of such an item is a condition not required by the contract, making the sale invalid.
This principle is universal in Hanafi sales law.
2
Apply the principle to pregnancy
Recognize that the pregnancy (fetus) is considered an accessory (tābi') like a limb. It cannot be sold separately, so excepting it from the sale of the slave girl is invalidating.
The fetus is legally part of the mother until birth.
3
Identify the initial ruling
The sale is initially fasid (invalid) because the exception is a condition that the contract does not necessitate.
Fasid means invalid but with potential for cure, unlike batil which is void ab initio.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa holds that if the buyer frees the child, the sale becomes valid because the freeing act terminates ownership in a way that harmonizes with the contract. The Sahibayn argue the sale remains invalid and the buyer must pay the slave girl's value.
The Sahibayn argue that an invalid contract cannot be cured by a subsequent act.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa, as the text indicates that the side of permissibility preponderates and the matter is suspended until emancipation.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa in the Hanafi school.

Book of Sale / Invalid and Disliked Sale. And the sale of a slave girl except her pregnancy. (151) (And the sale of a slave girl except her pregnancy is connected to a condition that the contract does not necessitate, and the principle in that is that everything that is not valid to be isolated in a contract is not valid to be excepted from the contract, for everything that is not valid to be isolated in a contract is part of the accessories of the thing, so it is included in the sold item as a subsidiary, so excepting it from the contract is a condition that the contract does not necessitate and thus it is invalidating. For ownership in a human being is established until emancipation, and the thing is determined by its termination. And everything that determines a thing validates it; for this reason if someone buys a slave and frees him, then learns of a defect, the buyer returns to the seller for the reduction, unlike if he sold him, for if he spoiled him, then the invalidity is established by the existence of the form of the condition without the ruling, so if he frees him, the suitability is realized by the ruling of emancipation, which is the termination of ownership by it, so the side of permissibility preponderates, and the state before that is suspended between remaining invalid as it was or turning into permissibility by emancipation. Thus in 'al-Kifaya'. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: It remains invalid as it was, so he must pay the value, because the sale occurred invalidly and does not turn into valid, like if he destroyed it in another way, such as death, killing, or sale. [1] His saying: 'the principle' means the universal rule in such a case that everything that is not valid to be isolated in a contract is not valid to be excepted from the contract, for it is part of the accessories of the thing and so is included in the sold item, so there is no need to mention it in the contract, and by the exception it is known that it is intended, and the pregnancy is of this kind because it is like the limbs of the animal due to its connection by creation, for the fetus as long as it is in its mother's womb is in the ruling of a part of her like the hand and foot, and the limbs of the animal are not accepted in a contract as intended, so they are not intended by the exception. For the exception is a disposition concerning what enters into the wording intentionally not subordinately, and the limbs are like descriptions so their inclusion is subordinate, so excepting it, i.e., the pregnancy, from the contract, i.e., the contract with the slave girl, is a condition that the contract does not necessitate, and every condition that the contract does not necessitate invalidates, so this condition invalidates. Reflect, and here are delicate discussions deposited in the larger works. (1) 'al-Kifaya' (6:80-82).
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the sale of a slave girl with the exception of her pregnancy. The author begins by stating the general principle: anything that cannot be sold independently cannot be validly excepted from a sale. The pregnancy of a slave girl is considered a part of her, like a limb or an appendage, and since limbs cannot be sold separately, the exception of the pregnancy is a condition that the contract does not necessitate, rendering the sale invalid (fasid). The text then presents the view of Imam Abu Hanifa, who holds that the sale can become valid if the buyer subsequently frees the child (the pregnancy). This is because freeing the child terminates ownership in a way that is compatible with the original contract, thus curing the invalidity. The two sahibayn, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, disagree: they argue that the sale remains invalid and the buyer must pay the slave girl's value, because the contract was concluded invalidly and cannot be transformed into a valid one, just as if the slave girl had died or been sold to another. The commentary emphasizes that the fetus is akin to a part of the mother, and its exception is a disruptive condition. The dispute hinges on whether the subsequent act (freeing) can remedy the defect or whether the original invalidity persists. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa, as indicated by the detailed argument and the concluding remark 'fa-tadabbar' (so reflect).

Key Terms
استثناء (istithna'): The act of excluding something from a contractual object; in this context, it refers to the exception of the pregnancy from the sale of the slave girl.
توابع (tawabi'): Accessories or things that naturally accompany and are subordinate to the main object of sale; here, the pregnancy is considered a tābi' of the slave girl.
فاسد (fasid): An invalid contract that is potentially curable, as opposed to batil (void).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the initial ruling of selling a slave girl with an exception of her pregnancy?
A) The sale is valid (sahih) because the exception is a right of the seller.
B) The sale is invalid (fasid) because the exception is a condition not required by the contract.
C) The sale is void (batil) because the exception makes the contract impossible.
D) The sale is disliked (makruh) but still valid.

Key Takeaway
The exception of an inseparable part from a sale renders the contract initially invalid (fasid), but Abu Hanifa holds that the defect can be cured by a subsequent act that aligns with ownership termination, such as freeing the child.

Invalid Sale with Deferment to Uncertain Festivals
Ruling on forbidden deferment to uncertain times

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for valid deferment of payment in a sale and apply the ruling to scenarios involving uncertain future events.

Core Idea
A sale is fasid if payment is deferred to an unknown time – uncertainty in the payment term invalidates.

In commercial transactions, clarity of terms is essential. When the time of payment is tied to a future event that is not fixed or known to both parties, the contract becomes invalid due to uncertainty (gharar). This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on deferring payment to uncertain festivals and events, illustrating with examples from the classical text.

Guided Path
1
Recognise the act
Identify the core issue: deferring the payment of the price to a future event rather than a fixed date.
The act is a sale with a deferred payment term.
2
Identify the condition
The payment time must be known (ma'lum) to both parties at the time of contracting.
Knowledge of the exact date or a clear period is required.
3
Note the examples
The text lists Nowruz, Mihrajan, Christian fasts, Jewish festivals, arrival of pilgrims, harvest, etc., as events with uncertain timings.
These events are not fixed to a specific calendar date known to the parties.
4
Apply the ruling
If the time is unknown, the sale is invalid (fasid). The contract must be rectified with a known term.
The ruling is unanimous in the Hanafi school.
5
Understand the rationale
Uncertainty (jahalah) in an essential term like time leads to gharar, which is prohibited in Islamic commercial law.
Gharar invalidates the contract due to risk of dispute.

And until Nowruz, and Mihrajan, and the fast of the Christians and the breaking of the fast of the Jews if they (the two contracting parties) do not know that, and the arrival of the pilgrim, and the harvest, and the threshing, and the picking of fruit, and the shearing. It is the first day of the sun's descent into the sign of Aries, and the beginning of spring. Thus in Majma' al-Anhur. The Khwarazmshahi Nowruz: the day when the sun is at noon at its twelfth degree of Aries. The common Nowruz: the first of the old Farvardin month. The special Nowruz: the sixth day of it. The Magian Nowruz: when the sun enters Pisces. And al-Mihrajan is also multiple: The common Mihrajan: the sixteenth day of the old Mihr month. Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement concerning the invalidity of sales where the payment of the price is deferred until uncertain or non-Islamic festivals and events. The author lists examples: Nowruz (Persian New Year), Mihrajan (an autumn festival), the fasting period of Christians, the breaking of the fast of Jews (i.e., their festivals), the arrival of pilgrims, harvest, threshing, fruit picking, and shearing. The key condition is that if the contracting parties do not know the exact timing of these events, the deferment is invalid because it involves uncertainty (gharar). The commentary explains the meanings of 'qitaf' (fruit picking) and 'jazz' (shearing) and provides etymological details about Nowruz and Mihrajan, noting that these are Persian festivals associated with the sun's entry into specific zodiac signs. The ruling: it is not permissible to defer the price to such unknown events. The reason is that the time of payment becomes unknown, which falls under prohibited uncertainty. The passage also mentions that Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) used the term 'Nowruz' to refer to every day, but the scholars clarify that the intended meaning here is the specific festival days. The Hanafi ruling is that such a sale is invalid (fasid) because the condition of a known payment term is not fulfilled.

Key Terms
نيروز (nayruz): Nowruz, the Persian New Year festival occurring at the spring equinox; considered an uncertain time for contractual deferment.
مهرجان (mihrajan): Mihrajan, an autumn Persian festival; similarly uncertain for payment terms.
جهالة (jahalah): Uncertainty or ignorance about the time of payment, which invalidates the sale.

Key Takeaway
Deferring the price to an unknown future event invalidates the sale; the payment term must be known and certain at the time of contract.

Invalid Deferment Terms in Sales and the Kafala Exception
Ruling with proof on invalid deferment terms and kafala exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which deferment terms invalidate a sale in the Hanafi school, explain the rationale based on jahalah, and distinguish the ruling for kafala.

Core Idea
A sale is invalid if the payment term is uncertain (jahalah), but kafala tolerates slight ignorance because it resembles a vow.

This lesson explores the Hanafi ruling on deferring the price in a sale to uncertain events such as festivals, harvest, or the arrival of pilgrims. The school holds such sales invalid due to the potential for dispute. However, the same uncertainty is tolerated in contracts like kafala (suretyship). We will examine the specific terms, the classification of ignorance, and the Companion disagreement underlying the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the deferment terms listed
Read the passage and list the events to which payment is deferred (e.g., Nowruz, Mehregan, Christian Lent, Jewish fasts, arrival of pilgrims, harvest, threshing, fruit-picking, shearing). Note why each is considered uncertain.
These events are not fixed to a precise day; they vary by calendar, astronomy, or natural conditions.
2
Understand the principle: jahalah invalidates sale
The ruling is that any deferment term that is unknown (majhul) leads to potential dispute and thus makes the sale fasid (invalid). This is based on the prohibition of gharar.
Jahalah (ignorance) is classified as slight (yasirah) if only timing varies, and gross (fahishah) if existence is uncertain. Here it is slight but still invalid in sales.
3
Analyze the Companion ikhtilaf
Aisha (RA) allowed sale with deferment to uncertain terms (like the government stipend), while Ibn Abbas (RA) prohibited it. The Hanafi school adopts Ibn Abbas's view to prevent dispute.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf; the tarjih is based on avoiding potential conflict.
4
Apply the exception for kafala
Kafala tolerates slight jahalah because it resembles a vow (nadhr) in being a mere obligation initially, and vows tolerate slight ignorance. Sale, being a strict exchange, does not.
This shows the Hanafi principle that different contracts have different tolerances for uncertainty.
5
Derive the practical ruling
If a sale contract includes a deferment to any of the listed uncertain events, the sale is fasid and must be rectified either by specifying a fixed date or by canceling the term. In kafala, such terms are permissible.
The mufta bihi is the prohibition of such deferments in sales.

(وإلى النيروز، والمَهْرَجَانِ، وصوم النَّصارى وفطر اليهود إن لم يعرفا ذلك، وقدوم الحاج، والحصاد، والدياس، والقِطَاف، والجزاز): القِطافُ: جَنْي الثمر عن الأشجار والجزازُ: قطع الصوف عن ظهر الغَنَم.
(1) قوله: وإلى؛ لا يجوز البيع بتأجيل الثمن إلى النيروز، أصله: نوروز؛ معرب، وقد تكلم به عمر، فقال: كل يوم لنا نوروز حين كان الكفار يبتهجون به. كذا في «النهاية»، وهو أول يوم من نزول الشمس في برج الحمل، وابتداء ربيع. كذا في «مجمع الأنهر».

ونيروز الخاصة: وهو اليوم السادس منه.

ومهرجان الخاصة: وهو اليوم الحادي والعشرون منه، وقد يسمى أوّلُ كلِّ يوم تكون الشمس في الميزان مهرجاناً. كذا في «شرح النقاية» للعلامة البرْجَنْدِي.

واليهود يصومون من أول شهرٍ إلى تمام عشرين من شهرٍ آخر، ثم يفطرون، فيوم صومهم وفطرهم مجهولان؛ لاختلافهما باختلاف عدة الشهر.
هذا إذا لم يعرفا هذه الآجال، وكذا إذا لم يعرف أحدهما، أما إذا كان ذلك معلوماً عندهما، فيجوزُ البيع لعدم النزاع.
وقدوم الحاج: أي وإلى مجيء الحاج.
والحصاد: بفتح الحاء، وكسرها: قطع الزرع: أي وقت قطع الزرع. والدياس: بكسر الدال المهملة، أصله الدواس، من الدَّوس، وهو شدَّةً وَطء الشيء بالقدم، فإنّ الدِّياسَ في الطَّعام أن يوطّأ بقوائم الدواب. كذا في «النهاية».
والقِطَافُ والجزاز؛ وإنّما لم يجز البيع إلى هذه المذكورات لعدم تيقن أوقاتها؛ لأنها تتقدم وتتأخَّرُ باختلاف الحر والبرد، فليس لها وقت معلوم؛ لأنها من أفعال العباد، فتثبت بحسب ما يبدوا لهم كما قالوا.
[١] قوله: الجهالة...الخ؛ قيل: الجهالة اليسيرة هي ما كان الاختلاف في التقدم والتأخر، وأما إذا اختلف في وجوده كهبوب الريح، كانت فاحشة، وهذه الجهالة يسيرة؛ لاختلافِ الصَّحابة الله في هذه الجهالات هل هي مانعة لجواز البيع أم لا، فقالت عائشة رضي الله عنها بالجواز؛ فإنّها تجيزُ البيعَ إلى العطاء، وكان ابنُ عبَّاس لا يجيز، ونحن نأخذ بقوله. كذا في «النهاية» وغيرها.
