And to Nowruz, Mehregan, the fast of Christians and the breaking of fast of the Jews if they (the contracting parties) do not know that, and the arrival of pilgrims, harvest, threshing, fruit-picking, and sheep-shearing. (And to Nowruz, Mehregan, the fast of Christians and breaking of fast of Jews if they do not know that, and arrival of pilgrims, harvest, threshing, fruit-picking, and sheep-shearing): al-qitaf is plucking fruit from trees, and al-jizaz is cutting wool from sheep's back. (1) His statement: 'and to' – sale with deferment of the price to Nowruz is not permissible. Its origin is Nawruz; an Arabized word. Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) spoke about it saying: 'Every day is a Nowruz for us since the disbelievers rejoice in it.' Thus in 'al-Nihayah'. It is the first day of the sun's descent into Aries, and the beginning of spring. Thus in 'Majma' al-Anhur'. And al-Mihrajan: it is Arabized, Mihrakan, and it is the first day of the sun's descent into Libra and the beginning of autumn. Nowruz: according to historians, it is multiple: The Sultanic Nowruz: it is the first day when the sun is at noon in the first degree of Aries. The Khwarazm Shahi Nowruz: it is the day when the sun is at noon in its twelfth degree of Aries. The commoners' Nowruz: it is the first day of the old Farvardin month. The special Nowruz: it is the sixth day of it. The Zoroastrians' Nowruz: it is when the sun enters Pisces. And al-Mihrajan is also multiple: The commoners' Mihrajan: it is the sixteenth day of the old Mehr month. The special Mihrajan: it is the twenty-first day of it, and sometimes the first day when the sun is in Libra is called Mihrajan. Thus in 'Sharh al-Niqayah' by Allamah al-Barjandi. 'And the fast of Christians and the breaking of fast of the Jews if they (the contracting parties) do not know' – meaning the two contracting parties (do not know) that mentioned (Nowruz, Mihrajan, the fast of Christians, and the breaking of fast of the Jews) because Nowruz and Mihrajan are not determined except by conjecture and practice of astronomy, so perhaps error occurs and it becomes unknown, leading to dispute. Similarly the fast of Christians and the breaking of fast of the Jews: the Christians begin from Nowruz and fast fifty days, so their fast day is unknown. As for their breaking of fast after they have begun their fast, it is known, so there is no ignorance (jahalah) and no invalidity. The Jews fast from the beginning of one month until the completion of twenty days of another month, then break their fast. Their fast day and breaking of fast are both unknown due to their variation according to the count of the month. This is when they do not know these terms; similarly if one of them does not know. If that is known to both, then the sale is permissible due to absence of dispute. 'And the arrival of pilgrims': that is, until the arrival of the pilgrims. 'And harvest' (al-hasad): with fatha on ha or kasra, it means cutting of crops, i.e., the time of cutting crops. 'And threshing' (al-diyas): with kasra of the important dal, its origin is al-dawas, from al-daws, which is the severity of trampling something with the foot, for threshing of grain is to tread it with the legs of animals. Thus in 'al-Nihayah'. 'And fruit-picking and sheep-shearing': and the sale to these mentioned (terms) is not permissible due to lack of certainty of their times, because they advance and delay according to differences in heat and cold, so they have no known time; because they are acts of servants, so they are established according to what appears to them as they said. [1] His statement: 'ignorance...etc.' It is said: slight ignorance is that in which the difference is in advance and delay. But if the difference is in its existence like the blowing of wind, it is gross ignorance. And this ignorance is slight because of the difference of the Companions (may Allah be pleased with them) regarding whether these ignorances prevent the permissibility of sale or not. Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) said it is permissible, for she permitted sale to the stipend, while Ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with him) did not permit, and we adopt his statement. Thus in 'al-Nihayah' and others. [2] His statement: 'tolerated in kafala' – because it resembles a vow (nadhr) in that it is a mere obligation initially, and a vow tolerates slight ignorance, and it resembles a sale in that it is a contract of exchange ultimately with regard to recourse against the principal debtor (makful anhu). A sale and other exchange contracts do not tolerate gross ignorance, so kafala...
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage from al-Hidayah's commentary deals with the validity of deferring payment in a sale to specific uncertain events. The author lists several terms that render the sale fasid (invalid) due to jahalah (ignorance) concerning the exact time: Nowruz (Persian New Year), Mehregan (autumn festival), Christian Lent, Jewish fasts and feasts, the arrival of pilgrims, harvest, threshing, fruit-picking, and shearing. The reasoning is that all these events are not fixed to a precise day; they vary by astronomical calculation or natural conditions, leading to potential dispute. For Nowruz and Mehregan, even their definitions vary among different calendars. For Christian Lent and Jewish fasts, the dates are uncertain because they depend on lunar months or on the start of Nowruz. However, if both parties actually know the exact date (e.g., a specific known day), the sale is permissible. The passage then clarifies that slight jahalah (such as a few days' difference in timing) is tolerated in kafala (suretyship) because kafala resembles a vow (nadhr) which tolerates slight ignorance, whereas sale as an exchange contract requires strict certainty. The text also notes a disagreement among Companions: Aisha (RA) allowed sale with deferment to uncertain terms (like the government stipend), while Ibn Abbas (RA) prohibited it; the Hanafi school follows Ibn Abbas's view, hence the ruling of prohibition. This demonstrates the Hanafi principle that jahalah leading to potential dispute invalidates a sale, but not all contracts.

Key Terms
النيروز (al-nayruz): The Persian New Year, a festive day whose exact date varies according to different calendars and astronomical calculations.
المهرجان (al-mihrajan): An autumn Persian festival, similarly uncertain as it depends on the sun's entry into Libra.
الجهالة (al-jahalah): Ignorance or uncertainty in a contract term that can lead to dispute; classified as slight (yasirah) if only timing varies, or gross (fahishah) if existence is uncertain.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is selling with deferment to 'harvest time' considered invalid in the Hanafi school?
A) Because harvest time is a forbidden occasion for transactions.
B) Because harvest time can vary with climate and is not precisely known, leading to jahalah.
C) Because the Quran explicitly prohibits such deferments.
D) Because harvest time is too short a period.

Key Takeaway
A sale with an uncertain deferment term is fasid in the Hanafi school due to jahalah; however, kafala tolerates slight ignorance because it is akin to a vow.

8 min
Kafalah to Uncertain Times: Permissible; Sale is Not
Commentary on the ruling of kafalah to uncertain times and its difference from sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on kafalah to uncertain times, explain why minor uncertainty is tolerated in kafalah but not in sale, and apply this distinction to new scenarios.

Core Idea
Kafalah tolerates minor timing uncertainty; sale does not, due to their different natures as gratuitous versus compensatory contracts.

This passage tackles a subtle point: if a contract refers to an uncertain future time (like harvest or pilgrimage), is it valid? The answer depends on the type of contract. Kafalah (suretyship) is permissible because it initially resembles a vow, which tolerates minor uncertainty. Sale, a strict exchange, does not tolerate any uncertainty about the term. The commentary traces this to a Companion-level disagreement and grounds it in a dual analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic ruling
The passage begins by stating that kafalah to uncertain times (e.g., harvest, pilgrims' arrival) is permissible, while sale to such times is not. Note that the ruling is specific to cases where the exact timing is unknown due to natural or human variability, not to cases where the event itself is uncertain.
2
Define minor vs. gross uncertainty
The commentary defines 'minor uncertainty' (jahalah yasirah) as variation only in advancement or delay of a known event. If the event's very existence is uncertain (e.g., wind blowing), the uncertainty is gross (fahishah). This distinction is key.
For example, harvest time varies but will surely happen; wind may not blow.
3
Analyze the dual analogy for kafalah
Kafalah resembles a vow (nadhr) in that it is a pure obligation from the start (gratuitous). Vows tolerate minor uncertainty. But kafalah also resembles sale at the end because the guarantor may seek recourse from the principal debtor, making it compensatory. Thus, kafalah takes the leniency of vows for minor uncertainty and the strictness of sale for gross uncertainty.
This is a Hanafi legal reasoning combining two shabah (resemblances).
4
Understand the Companion-level dispute and Hanafi stance
The Companions differed: Aisha (ra) permitted sale to uncertain times (e.g., until 'ata payment), but Ibn Abbas (ra) prohibited it. The Hanafi school follows Ibn Abbas's stricter view for sale, out of caution to avoid dispute.
This shows that Hanafi fiqh sometimes prefers a more conservative view to prevent conflict.
5
Apply the ruling to new cases
Test your understanding: Is kafalah valid if the guaranteed event's existence is uncertain (e.g., 'when the earthquake occurs')? No, that would be gross uncertainty. What if the parties both know the approximate time? Then sale would be valid because there is no uncertainty.
The passage explicitly says if both know the term, sale is valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع / البيع الفاسد والمكروه (والتكفل إليها جاز) : أي يجوز الكفالة إلى هذه الأوقات ؛ لأنَّ الجهالة اليسيرة متحملة في الكفالة دون البيع. بظن وممارسة بعلم النجوم ، فربّما يقعُ الخطأ فيكون مجهولاً ، فيؤدّي إلى النزاع. وكذا صومُ النّصارى وفطر اليهود ؛ فإنَّ النّصارى يبتدؤون من نيروز، ويصومون خمسين يوماً ، فيوم صومهم مجهول ، وأما يوم فطرهم بعدما شرعوا في صومهم فمعلوم، فلا جهالة فيه ولا فساد. واليهود يصومون من أول شهرٍ إلى تمام عشرين من شهرٍ آخر، ثم يفطرون، فيوم صومهم وفطرهم مجهولان ؛ لاختلافهما باختلاف عدة الشهر. هذا إذا لم يعرفا هذه الآجال، وكذا إذا لم يعرف أحدهما ، أما إذا كان ذلك معلوماً عندهما ، فيجوزُ البيع لعدم النزاع. وقدوم الحاج: أي وإلى مجيء الحاج. والحصاد : بفتح الحاء، وكسرها : قطع الزرع : أي وقت قطع الزرع. والدياس : بكسر الدال المهملة، أصله الدواس، من الدَّوس، وهو شدَّةً وطء الشيء بالقدم، فإنّ الدِّياسَ في الطَّعام أن يوطّأ بقوائم الدواب. كذا في «النهاية». والقِطَافُ والجزاز ؛ وإنّما لم يجز البيع إلى هذه المذكورات لعدم تيقن أوقاتها ؛ لأنها تتقدم وتتأخَّرُ باختلاف الحر والبرد، فليس لها وقت معلوم ؛ لأنها من أفعال العباد ، فتثبت بحسب ما يبدوا لهم كما قالوا. قوله : الجهالة...الخ ؛ قيل : الجهالة اليسيرة هي ما كان الاختلاف في التقدم والتأخر، وأما إذا اختلف في وجوده كهبوب الريح، كانت فاحشة، وهذه الجهالة يسيرة ؛ لاختلافِ الصَّحابة في هذه الجهالات هل هي مانعة لجواز البيع أم لا ، فقالت عائشة رضي الله عنها بالجواز ؛ فإنّها تجيزُ البيعَ إلى العطاء، وكان ابنُ عبَّاس لا يجيز، ونحن نأخذ بقوله. كذا في «النهاية» وغيرها. قوله : متحمّلة في الكفالة ؛ لأنها تشبه النذر من حيث إنّها إلزام محض ابتداء ، والنَّذر يتحمل الجهالة اليسيرة، وتشبه البيع من حيث إنّها معاوضة انتهاء باعتبار الرجوع على المكفول عنه، والبيع وسائر المعاوضات لا يتحمل الجهالة الثقيلة، فالكفالة بالاعتبار الأول تتحمل الجهالة اليسيرة، وبالاعتبار الثاني لا تتحمل الجهالة الثقيلة، عملا بالشبهين. كذا أفادني بعض مشايخي الأعلام أدام الله فيوضهم إلى يوم القيام.
And suretyship to them is permissible: i.e., suretyship (kafalah) to these times is permissible because minor uncertainty (jahalah yasirah) is tolerated in kafalah, unlike in sale. By conjecture and practice based on astrology, error may occur, so it becomes unknown, leading to dispute. Similarly, the fast of Christians and the breaking of the fast of Jews: Christians begin from Nowruz, fasting fifty days; their day of fasting is unknown, but their day of breaking the fast after they have begun their fast is known, so there is no uncertainty or invalidity. Jews fast from the first of a month to the complete twentieth of another month, then break; their day of fasting and breaking are both unknown due to variation based on the count of the month. This is when both parties do not know these terms, and similarly if one of them does not know. But if it is known to them, then the sale is permissible due to absence of dispute. 'And the arrival of the pilgrims': i.e., until the coming of the pilgrims. 'And the harvest' (al-hasad): with fatha of ha and kasra of ha? cutting of crops: i.e., the time of cutting crops. 'And the threshing' (al-diyas): with kasra of the unimportant dal, originally al-dawwas from al-daws, which is strong treading of something with the foot; indeed, threshing (al-diyas) of grain is that it be trodden by the feet of beasts. As in al-Nihaya. 'And the ripening' and 'the cutting' (of fruit). Verily, sale to these mentioned times is not permissible due to lack of certainty of their times; because they advance and recede according to heat and cold, so they have no known time; because they are acts of servants, so they are established according to what appears to them, as they said. His statement: 'jahalah...etc.': It was said: minor uncertainty (jahalah yasirah) is that in which the variation is in advancement and delay, but if the variation is in its very existence like the blowing of wind, then it is gross (fahishah). And this uncertainty is minor due to the difference of the Companions regarding these uncertainties: are they preventive for the permissibility of the sale or not? Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) said it is permissible; indeed she permitted sale until the payment (ata'). Ibn Abbas did not permit, and we adopt his statement. As in al-Nihayah and others. His statement: 'tolerated in suretyship': because it resembles vow (nadhr) in that it is a pure obligation from the beginning, and vow tolerates minor uncertainty; and it resembles sale in that it is a compensation at the end considering recourse against the principal debtor. Sale and other compensations do not tolerate gross uncertainty. Thus, kafalah, by the first consideration, tolerates minor uncertainty, and by the second consideration, does not tolerate gross uncertainty, acting on both resemblances. Thus one of my eminent teachers informed me, may Allah perpetuate their graces until the Day of Judgment.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the ruling that suretyship (kafalah) to uncertain future times is permissible, while sale with such terms is not. The commentary explains the reasoning: minor uncertainty is tolerated in kafalah because it resembles a vow (nadhr) in its initial pure obligation, but it also resembles a compensatory contract at the end, so it tolerates minor but not major uncertainty. The text provides examples of uncertain times: Christian fast starting from Nowruz (which is a Persian holiday with variable astronomical date), Jewish fast beginning from an unspecified month, and harvest time, threshing time, etc. These are uncertain because they vary by climate and human action. The commentator notes that the Companions disagreed: Aisha permitted sale with such terms, while Ibn Abbas did not, and the Hanafi school follows Ibn Abbas. The ruling also distinguishes between when both parties are ignorant of the precise time versus when the time is known to both—in the latter case, sale is valid as there is no dispute.

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafalah): Suretyship; a contract of guarantee where the kafil becomes liable for a debt or obligation of another.
الجهالة اليسيرة (al-jahalah al-yasirah): Minor uncertainty; here defined as uncertainty only in the timing (advance or delay) of a known event, not in its existence.
النذر (al-nadhr): Vow; an act of voluntary obligation made to Allah, which tolerates minor uncertainty in its fulfillment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school permit kafalah to uncertain times but not sale?
A) Because kafalah is a gratuitous contract like nadhr, while sale is compensatory; minor uncertainty is tolerated in gratuitous contracts.
B) Because kafalah is less important than sale.
C) Because sale requires certainty of consideration, while kafalah does not.
D) Because the Prophet (s) explicitly permitted it.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, kafalah tolerates minor uncertainty because it initially resembles a gratuitous vow, while sale, as a strict exchange, does not.

Waiving a Deferred Term Before It Falls Due Validates a Previously Invalid Sale
Commentary on the ruling that waiving an uncertain deferred term validates the sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on waiving an uncertain deferred term in a sale and distinguish defects in external conditions from defects in the substance of the contract.

Core Idea
An uncertain term can be cured by waiver before its due because the defect is in the condition, not the substance.

This passage examines a specific scenario in sales where an uncertain deferred term makes the contract fasid (corrupt). The commentator explains that if the buyer voluntarily waives that term before it falls due, the sale becomes valid. Understanding this requires distinguishing between defects in the substance of the contract (like riba) and defects in external conditions (like uncertain time). This lesson will guide you through the reasoning, key distinctions, and the intra-Hanafi disagreement with Zufar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
A sale with payment deferred to an uncertain time (e.g., 'until harvest') is originally fasid. If the buyer waives that term before it falls due, the sale becomes sahih according to the majority Hanafi view.
The key condition is that the waiver occurs before the term is due.
2
Understand the reasoning (talil)
The invalidity was due to the potential for dispute arising from uncertainty. By waiving the term before that dispute materializes, the cause of invalidity is removed, and the contract becomes valid.
The defect is in an external condition, not in the substance of the contract.
3
Distinguish from riba-based contracts
If someone sells one dirham for two dirhams (riba), waiving the extra dirham does not validate the contract because the riba is in the very substance (sulb al-'aqd) of the exchange.
Substance defects cannot be cured by subsequent action.
4
Distinguish from temporary marriage
Waiving the term in a temporary marriage (nikah mut'ah) does not make it valid because the contract itself is invalid; it is not a valid marriage contract.
The defect is in the essence of the contract, not an external condition.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Zufar disagreed, arguing that once a contract is concluded as fasid, it cannot become sahih by subsequent removal of the defect. The majority responded that here the invalidity was contingent on a future dispute, so removal before that resolves it.
The mufta bihi is the majority view that it becomes valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصح إن أسقط الأجل قبل حلوله : أي إن أسقط هذه الآجال المجهولة قبل حلولها ينقلب البيعُ صحيحاً . قوله وصح ؛ أي لو باع شيئاً بتأجيل الثمن إلى هذه الأوقات المذكورة ثم أسقط من له الأجل وهو المشتري هذا الأجل المفسد للبيع قبل حلوله : أي قبل الحصاد والدياس مثلاً صح البيع ؛ لأن الفساد كان للمنازعة، وقد ارتفع قبل تقرره، وهذه الجهالة هي شرط زائد لا في صلب العقد. بخلاف بيع الدرهم بالدرهمين لا ينقلب صحيحاً بإسقاط الدرهم الزائد ؛ لأن الفساد في صلب العقد، وبخلاف إسقاط الأجل في النكاح المؤقت ؛ لكونه متعة، وهو غير عقد النكاح. كذا في «المنح» وغيره، وقال : زفر لا يجوز؛ لأنه وقع فاسداً فلا ينقلب صحيحاً.
And it is valid if he waives the term before its falling due: i.e., if he waives these unknown terms before their falling due, the sale becomes valid. His statement: 'and it is valid': i.e., if one sells something with payment deferred to these mentioned times, then the one who owns the term (the buyer) waives this term that spoils the sale before its falling due (e.g., before harvest or threshing), the sale becomes valid; because the invalidity was due to potential dispute, and it has been removed before its establishment, and this uncertainty is an additional condition, not in the substance of the contract. Unlike the sale of one dirham for two dirhams, which does not become valid by waiving the extra dirham, because the invalidity is in the substance of the contract. And unlike waiving the term in a temporary marriage (nikah mu'ajjal), because that is mut'ah (pleasure marriage), which is not a valid marriage contract. As in al-Munyah and others. And Zufar said: It is not permissible because it occurred as invalid, so it does not become valid.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling when the deferred term in a sale is an uncertain time (e.g., until harvest) that renders the sale fasid (corrupt). The commentator clarifies that if the buyer (the one with the right to the term) voluntarily waives that term before it actually falls due, the sale becomes valid. The reasoning is that the invalidity was due to the potential for dispute arising from uncertainty; once the term is waived before any dispute materializes, the cause of invalidity is removed. This is distinguished from two cases: (1) A sale of one dirham for two dirhams (riba): waiving the extra dirham does not validate it because the riba is in the very substance of the contract. (2) Temporary marriage (nikah al-mut'ah): waiving the fixed term does not make it valid because the contract itself is invalid. Zufar, a Hanafi scholar, dissented, arguing that once a contract is concluded as invalid, it cannot become valid by subsequent removal of the defect. But the relied-upon position is that it does become valid.

Key Terms
إسقاط الأجل (isqat al-ajal): Waiving the deferred payment term; removing the condition of time from the contract.
صلب العقد (sulb al-'aqd): The substance or essence of the contract; the core elements without which the contract is inherently invalid.
فاسد (fasid): Corrupt; a contract that is valid in essence but invalid due to an external defect that can be removed.
صحيح (sahih): Valid; a contract that meets all conditions and pillars and produces its legal effects.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what happens if the buyer waives the uncertain deferred term before it falls due?
A) The sale remains invalid because the defect was in the substance of the contract.
B) The sale becomes valid because the cause of invalidity is removed.
C) The sale becomes valid only if the seller agrees.
D) The sale is considered void ab initio.

Key Takeaway
Defects in external conditions (like uncertain terms) can be cured by waiver before the term falls due, but defects in the substance (like riba) cannot.

Dropping an Unspecified Term Validates a Defective Sale
Ruling with proof: dropping an unspecified ajal validates a defective sale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on dropping an unspecified term before its due date and distinguish between defects in external conditions versus core contract terms.

Core Idea
Dropping an external defective condition before dispute arises validates the contract.

In Hanafi fiqh, a sale with an unspecified payment term (ajal majhul) is considered fāsid due to uncertainty that could lead to dispute. However, if the buyer waives the term before its due date, the sale becomes valid. This lesson unpacks the rationale behind this ruling and contrasts it with cases where the defect is inherent to the contract's core.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial defect
The sale was defective because the payment term was unspecified (e.g., 'at harvest time'), causing gharar. Recognize that this is a defect in an external condition, not in the core of the contract.
Unspecified ajal makes the sale fāsid but not bātil.
2
Understand the effect of dropping the term
When the buyer drops the term before it arrives, the cause of invalidity (potential dispute) is removed before it materializes. The sale becomes sahih.
The defect was conditional; once removed, the contract becomes valid.
3
Compare with core defects
Consider the counterexample of riba al-fadl (selling one dirham for two). That defect is in the core of the contract, so dropping the extra dirham does not validate it.
Also compare with temporary marriage (mut'ah), where the term is integral.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Zufar argues that a contract that occurred as fāsid cannot become valid later. The majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) hold that it can, because the defect was in an external condition.
The mufta bihi is the majority view.
5
Apply the principle
Generalize: For a defective sale caused by an external condition, if the condition is removed before any dispute arises, the sale becomes valid. This distinguishes between removable and irreparable defects.
Use this to analyze similar cases.

Source Text

وصح إن أسقط الأجل قبل حلوله : أي إن أسقط هذه الآجال المجهولة قبل حلولها ينقلب البيعُ صحيحاً، بالاعتبار الأول تتحمل الجهالة اليسيرة، وبالاعتبار الثاني لا تتحمل الجهالة الثقيلة، عملا بالشبهين. كذا أفادني بعض مشايخي الأعلام . قوله وصح ؛ أي لو باع شيئاً بتأجيل الثمن إلى هذه الأوقات المذكورة ثم أسقط من له الأجل وهو المشتري هذا الأجل المفسد للبيع قبل حلوله : أي قبل الحصاد والدياس مثلاً صح البيع ؛ لأن الفساد كان للمنازعة، وقد ارتفع قبل تقرره، وهذه الجهالة هي شرط زائد لا في صلب العقد. بخلاف بيع الدرهم بالدرهمين لا ينقلب صحيحاً بإسقاط الدرهم الزائد ؛ لأن الفساد في صلب العقد، وبخلاف إسقاط الأجل في النكاح المؤقت ؛ لكونه متعة، وهو غير عقد النكاح. كذا في «المنح » ، وغيره ، وقال : زفر لا يجوز؛ لأنه وقع فاسداً فلا ينقلب صحيحاً.
And it is valid if he drops the term before its arrival: meaning if he drops these unknown terms before their arrival, the sale becomes valid. By the first consideration, light ignorance is tolerated; by the second consideration, heavy ignorance is not tolerated, acting on both aspects. Thus informed me by some of my eminent teachers. His statement: 'And it is valid' means if one sold something with the payment deferred to these mentioned times, then the one who owns the term, i.e., the buyer, drops this term that corrupts the sale before its arrival, i.e., before harvest and threshing for example, the sale is valid, because the corruption was due to potential dispute, and it has been removed before it materialized, and this ignorance is an extra condition, not in the core of the contract. Unlike selling one dirham for two dirhams – it does not become valid by dropping the extra dirham, because the corruption is in the core of the contract. And unlike dropping the term in a temporary marriage, because it is mut'ah, and it is not a marriage contract. Thus in al-Manh and others. And Zufar said: It is not permissible, because it occurred as defective, so it does not become valid.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling that dropping an unspecified term before its due date validates an otherwise defective sale. The original sale was defective because the payment term was ambiguous (e.g., 'at harvest time'), leading to uncertainty. Since the cause of invalidity is potential dispute and it was removed before the term arrived, the sale becomes valid. The explanation distinguishes between a defect in an external condition (which can be removed) and a defect in the core of the contract (which cannot). Examples: selling one dirham for two dirhams – the defect of riba is in the core, so dropping the extra dirham does not make it valid. Temporary marriage (nikah mut'ah) – the term is integral to the contract (it is mut'ah, not nikah), so dropping it does not transform it into a valid marriage. The opinion of Zufar is that since the contract was initially defective (fāsid), it cannot become valid later; this is rejected by the majority in favor of the ruling that it becomes valid.

Key Terms
أجل (ajal): A deferred term for payment, which if unspecified (majhul) corrupts the sale.
تفسخ (tafaskh): Becoming invalid; used here to indicate the contract was fāsid (defective) initially.
شرط زائد (shart za'id): An extra condition outside the core contract, such as an unspecified payment term.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does dropping the extra dirham in a sale of one dirham for two dirhams not validate the contract, unlike dropping a deferred term?
A) Because the term is an external condition whereas the extra dirham causes riba in the core contract.
B) Because the extra dirham is a condition outside the contract.
C) Because the buyer does not own the extra dirham.
D) Because the contract was already valid.

Key Takeaway
A sale defective due to an unspecified term becomes valid if the term is dropped before its due date, because the defect was in an external condition, not the core contract.

Buyer's Liability in Defective Sale After Possession
Commentary on the ruling of defective sale possession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on buyer's ownership and liability after possession in a defective sale, and distinguish it from a void sale and from the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
In a defective sale, the buyer becomes owner upon possession with consent and is liable for its value.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the consequences of taking possession in a defective (fāsid) sale. It contrasts with void (bātil) sales and addresses an internal Hanafi dispute as well as a cross-madhhab difference with Imam al-Shafi'i. The commentator clarifies that consent may be explicit or implicit, and ownership transfers even though the contract is defective.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? 'If the buyer takes possession of the sold item in a defective sale with the seller's consent.' The ruling: the buyer becomes owner and is liable (damān) if it perishes.
This is the mufta bihi position.
2
Locate the primary proof (dalīl)
The commentary argues that the sale is an authorization (taslīṭ) from the seller; possession in the seller's presence without objection is like an explicit command, establishing ownership.
This is a dalīl 'aqlī (rational proof).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalālah
How does authorization imply ownership? The seller's consent to possession indicates transfer of control, and ownership follows from control with the owner's permission. The analogy to goods taken on approval (sawm al-shirā') further supports liability.
The liability view is strengthened by qiyās to sawm al-shirā'.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilāf
In a void (bātil) sale, there is a dispute: Abu Nasr (from Abu Hanifa) says amānah; Sarakhsi (from Muhammad) says damān. The mufta bihi is damān.
This does not affect the defective sale ruling.
5
Evaluate the cross-madhhab dispute
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that no ownership transfers in a defective sale because the prohibition revokes legality. The Hanafi response: the defect does not negate the owner's consent and possession, so ownership is established.
The practical difference (thamarah) is that Hanafis allow the buyer to use the item freely.

فصل في أحكامه ثم اعلم أن الحكم في البيعِ الباطل أنّ المبيعَ إن هلك في يد المشتري، فعند البعض : أمانة، وعند البعض : مضمون بالقيمة كالمقبوض على سومِ الشراء. وأما حكم البيع الفاسد ففي المتنِ شرع في أحكامه ، فقال : (فإن قبض المشتري المبيعَ بَيْعاً فاسداً برضى بائعه صريحاً ، أو دلالة كقبضه في مجلس عقده، وكل من عوضيه مالٌ يَمْلِكُهُ ) [١] قوله : فعند البعض ؛ وهو أبو نصر أحمد بن علي أستاذ خُوَاهِرَ زَادَه ، وهذا رواية الحسن له عن أبي حنيفة له أمانة فلو هلك عند المشتري لم يضمن ؛ لأنَّ العقد إذا بطل بقي مجرَّدُ القبض بإذن المالك، وهو لا يوجب الضمان إلا بالتعدي. [٢]قوله : وعند البعض ؛ وهو شمس الأئمةِ السَّرَخسي، وهذا رواية ابن سماعة عن محمد له ، وقيل : هذا قولهما مضمون بالقيمة ؛ لأنه يصير كالمقبوض على سوم الشراء، وهو أن يسمي الثمن فيقول : اذهب بهذا، فإنّي رضيت، أما إذا لم يسمه فذهب به فهلك عنده لا يضمن نص عليه الفقيه أبو الليث ، وقيل : وعليه الفتوى، كما في «العناية»(1). [٣) قوله : برضاء بائعه ؛ وهذا أولى مما قال بعضهم : بأمر البائع. فإنّه لا يشمل الرضاء دلالة مع أنه يكتفى به ؛ لأنَّ البيع تسليط من البائع على القبض، فإذا قبضه المشتري بحضرة البائع ولم يمنعه من القبض، فكان بحكم التسليط بالأمر الصريح ويثبت به الملك. [٤]قوله : يملكه ؛ أي يملك المشتري ذلك المقبوض بالبيع الفاسد، وقال الشافعي : لا يملكه، وإن قبضه ؛ لأنه حرام، فلا ينالُ به نعمة الملك ؛ لأنَّ النهي فسخ للمشروعيّة لتنافٍ بينهما ؛ ولهذا لا يفيده قبل القبض، وصار كما إذا باغ بالميتة أو باغ الخمر بالدراهم.
Section on its rulings: Then know that the ruling regarding a void sale (bātil) is that if the sold item perishes in the buyer's hand, according to some it is a trust (amānah), and according to some it is guaranteed with value (damān bil-qīmah) like something taken on the basis of purchase approval (sawm al-shirā'). As for the ruling of a defective sale (fāsid), the text begins its rulings: 'If the buyer takes possession of the sold item in a defective sale with the consent of the seller, explicitly or by indication like taking possession in the session of the contract, and both of its countervalues are owned property (māl).' [1] His statement: 'according to some' – he is Abu Nasr Ahmad ibn Ali, teacher of Khwahrizadeh, and this is the narration of al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa: it is a trust, so if it perishes with the buyer he does not guarantee it, because when the contract is void, mere possession with the owner's permission remains, and it does not necessitate liability except through transgression. [2] His statement: 'and according to some' – he is Shams al-A'immah al-Sarakhsi, and this is the narration of Ibn Sama'ah from Muhammad, and it is said this is their (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad's) view: it is guaranteed with value because it becomes like something taken on the basis of purchase approval, which is when he names the price and says: take this, for I am satisfied; but if he does not name it and takes it and it perishes with him, he does not guarantee – as stated by Fakhr al-Islam Abu al-Layth, and it is said the fatwa is upon it, as in al-'Inayah. [3] His statement: 'with the consent of its seller' – this is better than what some said: 'by the command of the seller' because it does not include implicit consent even though it is sufficient; because the sale is an authorization from the seller to take possession, so if the buyer takes possession in the presence of the seller and he does not prevent him from taking possession, it is like authorization by explicit command, and ownership is established. [4] His statement: 'he owns it' – meaning the buyer owns that which was taken possession in a defective sale. Al-Shafi'i said: he does not own it even if he takes possession, because it is forbidden, so he does not attain the blessing of ownership, because the prohibition revokes legality due to their incompatibility; and thus it does not benefit before possession, and it becomes like buying carrion or buying wine with dirhams.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on the rulings of a defective (fāsid) sale, specifically addressing the consequences if the buyer takes possession. The text first contrasts with a void (bātil) sale: in a void sale, there are two opinions on liability if the goods perish – some say it is a trust (no liability), others say it is guaranteed like goods taken on approval (damān). For a defective sale, the ruling is that if the buyer takes possession with the seller's consent (either explicit or implied, like taking in the same session), and both the item and the price are owned property (i.e., not impure like wine or carrion), then the buyer becomes owner. The commentary explains two internal Hanafi disputes: (1) Regarding a void sale, Abu Nasr holds there is no liability (amānah) based on a narration from Abu Hanifa through Hasan ibn Ziyad, while Sarakhsi holds liability (damān) based on a narration from Muhammad through Ibn Sama'ah – the latter is the relied-upon position for fatwa. (2) The condition 'consent' is preferred over 'command' because implied consent suffices. The passage then notes a cross-madhhab dispute with Imam Shafi'i: he holds that the buyer does not own the item in a defective sale because the prohibition makes the contract legally ineffective, and the prohibition invalidates the purpose of ownership. The Hanafi response is implicit through the earlier rulings that ownership does transfer with possession and consent.

Key Terms
البيع الفاسد (al-bay' al-fāsid): A sale that is defective due to an external condition or missing a necessary condition but not inherently forbidden; it can become valid if the defect is removed.
قبض (qabd): Taking physical possession of the sold item.
ضمان (damān): Liability to pay the value of the item if it perishes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a buyer takes possession of an item in a defective sale with the seller's implicit consent (e.g., in the same session), what is the ruling?
A) The buyer does not own the item and must return it.
B) The buyer owns the item and is liable for its value if it perishes.
C) The buyer owns the item but only as a trust; no liability if it perishes.
D) The contract is void and the item must be returned.

Key Takeaway
In a defective sale, the buyer becomes owner and assumes liability upon possession with the seller's consent, even though the contract is defective.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Ownership by Possession in a Fasid Sale
Commentary on buyer's possession in fasid sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a buyer's possession in a fasid sale and explain the proof for ownership despite prohibition.

Core Idea
In a fasid sale, the buyer owns the item by possession, but must return it to rescind; the prohibition does not nullify the contract's essence.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains a key Hanafi doctrine: although a fasid sale is defective, the buyer's possession with consent transfers ownership. The commentary resolves an intra-Hanafi dispute on liability and refutes the Shafi'i claim that prohibition nullifies the contract. Understanding this ruling hinges on the distinction between essence and attribute in legal acts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two intra-Hanafi views on liability
One view (from al-Hasan's narration from Abu Hanifa) holds the buyer holds as an amana (trust); the other (from Ibn Sama'a's narration from Muhammad) holds he is liable for the value (mamun bil-qima) like a price-quoted purchase (sawm al-shira). The mufta bihi is the liability view.
The conflicting narrations show the nuanced reasoning within the Hanafi school.
2
Understand the condition of seller's consent
The buyer's possession is effective only with the seller's explicit or implicit consent. Implicit consent is found when the buyer takes possession in the seller's presence without objection. This suffices to establish ownership.
The text prefers 'consent' (rida) over 'command' (amr) because it covers implicit permission.
3
Grasp the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that since the sale is haram, the buyer cannot own the item; prohibition negates legal validity entirely, analogous to selling wine or dead animals.
This stems from the Shafi'i view that fasid and batil are identical.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response distinguishes: the prohibition does not negate the legal possibility (mashru'iyyah) of the act. Sale is a legal action; prohibiting it implies it is legally possible. The prohibition merely attaches a defect, not to the essence but to an attribute. The analogy to wine/dead meat fails because those are not property (mal).
The proof uses the principle 'al-nahy yufidu al-mashru'iyyah' (prohibition implies legal possibility).
5
Note the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
In Hanafi law, the buyer owns the item upon possession and is liable if it perishes; the contract must be rescinded by mutual return. In Shafi'i law, no ownership transfers, and the item must be returned immediately regardless of loss.
This difference affects liability and the need to repeat the transaction.

فصل في أحكامه
فإن قبض المشتري المبيعَ بَيْعاً فاسداً برضى بائعه صريحاً ، أو دلالة كقبضه في مجلس عقده، وكل من عوضيه مالٌ يَمْلِكُهُ
ثم اعلم أن الحكم في البيعِ الباطل أنّ المبيعَ إن هلك في يد المشتري، فعند البعض أمانة، وعند البعض مضمون بالقيمة كالمقبوض على سومِ الشراء.
[١] قوله : فعند البعض؛ وهو أبو نصر أحمد بن علي أستاذ خُوَاهِرَ زَادَه، وهذا رواية الحسن له عن أبي حنيفة له أمانة فلو هلك عند المشتري لم يضمن؛ لأنَّ العقد إذا بطل بقي مجرَّدُ القبض بإذن المالك، وهو لا يوجب الضمان إلا بالتعدي.
[٢] قوله : وعند البعض؛ وهو شمس الأئمةِ السَّرَخسي، وهذا رواية ابن سماعة عن محمد له، وقيل : هذا قولهما مضمون بالقيمة؛ لأنه يصير كالمقبوض على سوم الشراء، وهو أن يسمي الثمن فيقول : اذهب بهذا، فإنّي رضيت، أما إذا لم يسمه فذهب به فهلك عنده لا يضمن نص عليه الفقيه أبو الليث، وقيل : وعليه الفتوى، كما في «العناية».
[٣] قوله : برضاء بائعه؛ وهذا أولى مما قال بعضهم : بأمر البائع. فإنّه لا يشمل الرضاء دلالة مع أنه يكتفى به؛ لأنَّ البيع تسليط من البائع على القبض، فإذا قبضه المشتري بحضرة البائع ولم يمنعه من القبض، فكان بحكم التسليط بالأمر الصريح ويثبت به الملك.
[٤] قوله : يملكه؛ أي يملك المشتري ذلك المقبوض بالبيع الفاسد، وقال الشافعي : لا يملكه، وإن قبضه؛ لأنه حرام، فلا ينالُ به نعمة الملك؛ لأنَّ النهي فسخ للمشروعيّة لتنافٍ بينهما؛ ولهذا لا يفيده قبل القبض، وصار كما إذا باغ بالميتة أو باغ الخمر بالدراهم.
فإن قيل : كلامنا في البيع الفاسد، فيكون كل من العوضين مالاً البتة، إذ لو لم يكن البيع مالاً لكان البيع باطلاً.
قلنا : قد يُذكرُ الفاسد ويراد به الباطل! ولنا : إنَّ ركن البيع صدر من أهله، ووقع في محله فوجبَ القولُ بانعقاده، ولا شك في الأهلية والمحلية، وركنه مبادله المال بالمال، وهو حاصل، والنهي عن الأفعال يقتضي تقرر المشروعية؛ لأنّه يقتضي تصور المنهي عنه، والنهي عن ما لا يتصور لغو. وتحقيقه : إنَّ مراد الأمر والنهي المقدورية، فالنَّهي عن الأفعال الحسية يقتضي كونها مقدورة حسّاً، وعن الأمور العقلية كونها مقدورة عقلاً، وعن الأفعال الشرعية يقتضي كونها مقدورة شرعاً. فإن الطيران مثلاً من الأفعال الحسيّة، فإذا قلت لإنسان : لا تطر. ينكره كل سامع؛ لانتفاء القدرة، وعلى هذا إذا قلت لأعمى : لا تبصر. والبيع من الأفعال الشرعية، فإذا نهى عنه وجب أن يكون مقدوراً شرعاً، وهذا مراد من قال من علمائنا : إنَّ النهي عن الفعل الشرعي يقتضي المشروعية بأصله، وغير المشروعية بوصفه، فإنَّ الأول ناظر إلى المقدورية شرعاً، والثَّاني إلى النهي، فنفس البيع مشروع، وبه ينال نعمة الملك. وإنما الحرمة لأمر عارض، وعدم ثبوت الملك قبل القبض، كيلا يؤدي إلى تقرير الفساد، يعني لو ثبت الملك قبل القبض؛ لوجب تسليم الثمن على المشتري، وتسليم المبيع على البائع؛ لأنها من موجب العقد، فتقرر الفساد، وهو لا يجوز؛ لأنه واجب الرفع بالاسترداد. وكل ما هو واجب الرفع بالاسترداد فاسد، وإذا كان واجب الرفع بالاسترداد، فلا يكون واجب الرفع بالامتناع عن مطالبة أحد المتعاقدين أولى؛ لكونه أسهل؛ لسلامته عن المطالبة والإحضار والتسليم، ثمَّ الرفعُ بالاسترداد. هذا وإن شئت زيادةً التفصيل فارجع إلى المبسوطات من كتب الأصول، فإنّ فيها ما يشفي العليل.
[١] قوله : فإن قيل ... الخ؛ حاصله أن قوله : وكلُّ من عوضيه مال، قيد زائد لا طائل تحته؛ لأنَّ الكلام في البيع الفاسد، وكلٌّ من عوضيه إذا لم يكن مالاً يكون البيع باطلاً، ولما قال سابقاً : بيعاً فاسداً، فلا يكون هذا القيد لإخراج البيع الباطل.
[٢] قوله : قلنا...الخ؛ حاصله : أنَّ استدراك هذا القيد مدفوع بوجهين:
Section on its rulings. If the buyer takes possession of the sold item in a fasid (defective) sale with the explicit or implicit consent of its seller, like taking possession in the session of the contract, and each of its two exchanges is property that he owns. Then know that the ruling in a batil (void) sale is that if the sold item perishes in the buyer's hand, then according to some it is an amana (trust), and according to some it is guaranteed by value like that taken possession of on the basis of negotiating a purchase. [1] His saying: 'according to some'—he is Abu Nasr Ahmad bin Ali, teacher of Khuwahir Zadah, and this is the narration of al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa: it is an amana, so if it perishes with the buyer he is not liable, because when the contract becomes void, only the taking possession with the owner's permission remains, and it does not necessitate liability except through transgression. [2] His saying: 'according to some'—he is Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi, and this is the narration of Ibn Sama'a from Muhammad: it is guaranteed by value because it becomes like that taken possession of on the basis of negotiating a purchase, which is that he names the price and says: 'Take this, for I am pleased.' But if he does not name it and takes it and it perishes with him, he is not liable—this was stated by al-Faqih Abu al-Layth, and it is said that the fatwa is upon it, as in al-'Inayah. [3] His saying: 'with the consent of its seller'—and this is more appropriate than what some said: 'by the command of the seller,' because it does not include implicit consent despite it being sufficient, because the sale is an authorization from the seller for taking possession, so if the buyer takes possession in the presence of the seller and he does not prevent him from taking possession, then it is effectively an authorization by explicit command, and ownership is established by it. [4] His saying: 'he owns it'—i.e., the buyer owns that which is taken possession of in the fasid sale. Al-Shafi'i said: he does not own it, even if he takes possession, because it is haram, so he does not attain the blessing of ownership, because the prohibition nullifies the legality due to conflict between them; hence it does not benefit him before possession, and it becomes as if he sold a dead animal or sold wine for dirhams. If it is said: Our discussion is about the fasid sale, so each of the two exchanges is definitely property, for if it were not property, the sale would be batil. We say: Sometimes 'fasid' is mentioned and 'batil' is intended by it! And for us: The pillar of sale originated from its qualified person and occurred in its proper place, so it is necessary to say it is contracted. There is no doubt about qualification and place, and its pillar is exchange of property for property, which is realized. The prohibition of actions necessitates confirmation of legality, because it necessitates conceptualization of the prohibited thing, and prohibiting what cannot be conceptualized is futile. Its verification: The intended meaning of command and prohibition is the possible. So the prohibition of physical actions necessitates that they be physically possible, of intellectual matters that they be intellectually possible, and of legal actions that they be legally possible. For example, flying is among physical actions; if you say to a person: 'Do not fly,' every listener would deny it due to lack of ability. Similarly, if you say to a blind person: 'Do not see.' Sale is among legal actions, so if it is prohibited, it must be legally possible. This is the meaning of what some of our scholars said: The prohibition of a legal action necessitates legality in its essence and non-legality in its attribute, because the first looks at legal possibility, and the second at the prohibition. So the sale itself is legal, and through it one attains the blessing of ownership. Rather, the prohibition is for an incidental reason, and the non-establishment of ownership before possession is to avoid confirming the defectiveness, meaning if ownership were established before possession, then the delivery of the price would be obligatory upon the buyer and the delivery of the sold item upon the seller, because they are from the requirements of the contract, thus confirming the defectiveness, which is not permissible because it is obligatory to be removed by reclaiming. And everything that is obligatory to be removed by reclaiming is defective. And if it is obligatory to be removed by reclaiming, then it is more appropriate that it is not obligatory to be removed by refraining from suing either of the two contractors, because it is easier, due to its being free from suing, summoning, and delivering. Then the removal is by reclaiming. If you desire more detail, refer to comprehensive works on usul, for in them is what cures the ill. [1] His saying: 'If it is said... etc.;' its summary is that his saying: 'and each of his two exchanges is property' is an extra restriction with no benefit under it, because the discussion is about the fasid sale, and if each of its two exchanges were not property, the sale would be batil, and since he said earlier 'a fasid sale,' this restriction is not for excluding the batil sale. [2] His saying: 'We say... etc.;' its summary is that the objection to this restriction is repelled by two points:
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling of a fasid (defective) sale regarding the buyer's possession. The base text (matn) states that if a buyer takes possession of an item in a fasid sale with the seller's consent (explicit or implicit, such as possession in the contract session), and both the sold item and the price are property that the buyer owns, then certain consequences follow. The commentator clarifies two points: First, there are two views on whether the buyer holds as an amana (trust) or is liable for the value. The first view (attributed to Abu Nasr Ahmad and a narration from Abu Hanifa) says it is amana, so if the item perishes, the buyer is not liable unless he transgresses. The second view (attributed to Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi and a narration from Muhammad) says he is liable for the value because it is like a purchase where a price is named (sawm al-shira). The fatwa is upon the second view. Second, the condition of the seller's consent is explained; implicit consent (e.g., not preventing possession in the presence) suffices. Third, the passage addresses the Shafi'i objection that the buyer does not own the item because the transaction is haram. The Hanafi response is that the prohibition does not negate the legality in essence; rather, the sale remains valid as a contract (its pillar is exchange of property), but the defect (fasad) is due to an incidental reason. Ownership is established by possession, but before possession it is not established to avoid confirming the defect (since mutual delivery would enforce the defective contract). The passage also refutes an objection that the condition 'both exchanges are property' is redundant, explaining that it guards against the case where the fasid term is used to mean batil. The commentator directs further inquiry to books of usul al-fiqh.

Key Terms
بيع فاسد (bay' fasid): A sale that is valid in essence (rukn and mahall) but defective due to a missing condition (e.g., uncertainty in price), requiring rescission.
قبض (qabd): Taking physical possession of the sold item, which transfers ownership of the item in a fasid sale.
مقبوض على سوم الشراء (maqbud 'ala sawm al-shira): An item taken by a buyer with a named price but without a final contract; the buyer is liable for its value.
أمانة (amanah): A trust; the buyer is not liable for accidental destruction unless he transgresses.
مضمون بالقيمة (mamun bil-qima): Liable for the value of the item if it perishes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a fasid sale, what is the legal status of the buyer's possession according to the relied-upon (mufta bihi) Hanafi position?
A) The buyer holds as an amana (trust) and is not liable for loss.
B) The buyer is liable for the value of the item if it perishes.
C) The buyer does not own the item and must return it immediately.
D) The buyer owns the item only after paying the full price.

Key Takeaway
In a fasid sale, ownership transfers to the buyer upon possession with consent, and the buyer is liable for the value if the item perishes, per the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Condition of Property in Fasid Sale: Sahibayn vs. Possible Views
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the need for the property condition in fasid sales.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school (Sahibayn) holds that a sale with unspecified price is fasid, and distinguish it from batil by applying the condition that both countervalues are property.

Core Idea
A sale is fasid when valid in origin but flawed incidentally; the condition 'both countervalues are property' excludes batil, not fasid.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether a sale with an unspecified price is fasid or batil. The text argues that the condition 'each countervalue is property' is necessary because the term 'fasid' can be used loosely to include batil, as seen in Quduri's ruling on carrion sale. We will unpack the rational argument that prohibition implies legal possibility, and see how the Sahibayn analogize to usurpation (ghasp) to determine liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Issue and the Objection
The objector says: since we are discussing fasid sales, both countervalues are necessarily property; otherwise the sale would be batil. So why add the condition? State the objection clearly.
This sets up the dispute: is the condition redundant?
2
Analyze the First Response: Fasid vs. Batil Usage
The reply says 'fasid' can be used to mean 'batil', as Quduri did for carrion sale. So the condition excludes those batil cases. This shows the Hanafi position that fasid and batil are distinct, but terminology can blur.
The condition protects against misunderstanding.
3
Examine the Second Response: The Unspecified Price Case
A sale where the seller is silent about the price is not batil but fasid according to the Sahibayn. The price is implicit (market value), so the sale is valid in origin but flawed. The condition ensures that such a case is not mistakenly classified as batil.
Practical example: sale without price is fasid, not batil.
4
Understand the Rational Argument (Dalil Aqli)
The text argues that prohibition of a legally relevant action implies its legal possibility. Since sale is prohibited in some cases (e.g., with uncertainty), the sale itself must be valid in origin, but the description makes it invalid. This supports the fasid classification.
This is a key usul argument used by Hanafi scholars.
5
Apply the Liability Rule: Analogy to Ghasp
If the sold item perishes in the buyer's hand in a fasid sale, the buyer must pay its equivalent (mithl or value). This is based on qiyas to usurpation: both involve possession without valid title, so liability is similar.
The commentary explains that mithli items require specific restitution, non-mithli require value.

أنَّ المثل صورة ومعنى أعدلُ من المثل معنى، فلا يعدل عنه.
If it is said: Our discussion is about the fasid sale, so both countervalues are definitely property, because if one were not property, the sale would be batil. We reply: Sometimes 'fasid' is mentioned and by it 'batil' is intended. And we have [the argument]: Indeed the pillar of sale originated from its qualified person and occurred in its object, so it must be said that it is validly concluded. There is no doubt about qualification and object, and its pillar is exchanging property for property, and that is achieved. And prohibition of actions implies the establishment of legality, because it implies the conceptualization of the prohibited thing, and prohibition of what is inconceivable is nonsense. Its verification: that command and prohibition are about what is possible. So prohibition of physical actions implies they are physically possible, of intellectual matters implies they are intellectually possible, and of legally relevant actions implies they are legally possible. For example, flying is a physical action; if you say to a person 'Do not fly', every listener would reject it due to lack of ability. Similarly, if you say to a blind person 'Do not see'. And sale is a legally relevant action, so if it is prohibited, it must be legally possible. This is what is meant by those of our scholars who said: Prohibition of a legally relevant action implies its legality in its origin, and its illegality in its description. For the first looks to legal possibility, and the second to the prohibition. So the sale itself is legal, and by it one attains the blessing of ownership. The corruption is only due to an incidental matter, and non-establishment of ownership prior to possession, so as not to lead to establishment: i.e., if ownership were established before possession, delivery of the price would be obligatory on the buyer and delivery of the sold item on the seller, because they are part of the contract's implications, so the corruption would be established, which is not permissible, because it is obligatory to lift it by restitution. And everything that is obligatory to lift by restitution is fasid. And if it is obligatory to lift by restitution, then it is not more appropriate to be obligatory to lift by abstention from demanding [restitution] from one of the two contractors, because it is easier, being free from demand, bringing, and delivery. Then the lifting is by restitution. This, and if you wish more detail, refer to the expanded works of usul, for therein is what cures the ill. His saying: 'If it is said... etc.'; its summary is that his saying: 'And each of its two countervalues is property' is an extra condition with no benefit, because the discussion is about the fasid sale, and if each of its two countervalues were not property, the sale would be batil. And since he said earlier 'a fasid sale', this condition is not for excluding the batil sale. His saying: 'We replied... etc.'; its summary: that the addition of this condition is refuted by two ways: First: His saying 'a fasid sale' does not convey what is intended, because 'fasid' may be used to mean 'batil', just as the scholar Quduri in the beginning of Kitab al-Bay' declared the sale of carrion as fasid while it is batil by consensus, so one might imagine that here also what is meant by his saying 'a fasid sale' is batil, so this condition excludes that. Second: The sale may be fasid while each of its two countervalues is not property, as when someone sells something and remains silent about the price, then it is not the case that each countervalue is property, so it should be batil, yet it is fasid according to the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad], resulting in the effect of a fasid sale, which is ownership for the buyer by possession, and obligation of the value upon him. And it should not escape you that the value, even if not mentioned actually, is mentioned implicitly. As stated in al-Dhakhira and others. 'And he is liable for its equivalent in reality or meaning': i.e., if it perishes in the buyer's hand, he must pay its equivalent in reality or meaning, because it is guaranteed by itself upon possession, so it resembles usurpation, and the ruling for usurpation is similar, on the basis that the equivalent in form and meaning is more just than the equivalent in meaning only, so one does not deviate from it.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the condition that both countervalues must be property in a fasid sale. The objection claims that since we are discussing fasid (invalid) sales, it is obvious that both must be property; otherwise it would be batil (void). The response clarifies that sometimes the term 'fasid' is used loosely to include 'batil', so the condition is necessary to exclude batil. The author provides a rational argument: prohibition of an action implies its legal possibility, so the sale is fundamentally valid but made invalid by an incidental feature. The commentary then gives two reasons for the condition: first, Quduri's use of 'fasid' for carrion sale (which is batil by consensus) could cause confusion; second, a sale with unspecified price is fasid according to the two (Sahibayn) though one countervalue (the price) is not explicitly property—it becomes property by implication. Finally, the text rules that if the sold item perishes in the buyer's hand, he must pay its equivalent (value or specific like item), analogizing to usurpation (ghasp). The commentary explains that the equivalent in form and meaning (i.e., mithli items) is preferred over value-only.

Key Terms
فاسد (fasid): A defective contract that is valid in its origin but invalid in description due to an incidental flaw, requiring restitution but allowing ownership by possession.
باطل (batil): A contract that is void ab initio due to missing a pillar or condition essential to its validity.
عوض (iwad): The countervalue in a contract, i.e., the price and the sold item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the condition that both countervalues be property necessary in defining a fasid sale?
A) Because otherwise the sale would be batil by default
B) Because the term 'fasid' can be used loosely to mean 'batil', so the condition excludes batil cases
C) Because all sales must involve property to be valid
D) Because the Quran requires it

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a sale with unspecified price is fasid, not batil, and the condition that both countervalues are property prevents confusion with batil sales.

Distinguishing Fāsid from Bāṭil: Both Countervalues Must Be Property
Commentary on why the matn specifies 'both countervalues are property' for a fāsid sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition that both countervalues must be property for a fāsid sale and explain why this condition distinguishes fāsid from bāṭil, including the intra-Hanafi dispute on silent price.

Core Idea
A fāsid sale requires property in both countervalues; otherwise it may be bāṭil or still fāsid by tacit price.

The matn in al-Hidayah defines a fāsid sale as one where both countervalues are property. This commentary from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains why this condition is crucial: it prevents confusion with bāṭil sales and covers cases where the price is unstated yet the sale is still fāsid. We will unpack the two reasons given by the commentator and explore the intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on such sales.

Guided Path
1
Understand the ambiguity of 'fāsid'
Recognize that the term 'fāsid' is sometimes used interchangeably with 'bāṭil' in early texts. The commentator cites al-Quduri's example of selling a dead animal, which is called fāsid but is actually bāṭil by consensus. This creates a potential misunderstanding that every fāsid sale here might be bāṭil.
The condition 'both countervalues are property' explicitly excludes bāṭil sales (like dead animal) from the ruling of ownership by possession.
2
Apply the condition to the silent price case
Consider a sale where the seller and buyer agree on the item but no price is stated. According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the sale is fāsid, not bāṭil. The buyer owns the item by taking possession (qabḍ) and must pay the market value (qīmah), which is considered as if it were the price. The condition 'both countervalues are property' is satisfied because the value is property in meaning, even if not explicitly mentioned.
Imam Abu Hanifa disagrees: he considers such a sale bāṭil because the price is unknown, making the contract void ab initio.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The difference stems from whether the unknown price renders the contract void (bāṭil) or merely defective (fāsid). The Sahibayn's view is relied upon for fatwa (mufta bihi) because it facilitates transactions and prevents hardship. The commentator cites al-Dhakhirah and other sources supporting this position.
This ikhtilaf is legitimate; both positions are based on valid usul principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَزِمَهُ مثله حقيقة أو معنى كما أن في أوّل كتاب القُدُورِيُّ ( جَعَلَ البيع بالميتة فاسداً، وهو باطل؛ فلهذا قال : وكلُّ من عوضيه مال ؛ احتياطاً حتى لو اشتمل الفاسد الباطل يكون هذا القيدُ مُخْرِجاً له عن هذا الحكم ، وهو أن يصير المبيعُ مُلكاً على أنه قد يكون البيع ا، مع أنه لا يكون كل من عوضيه مالاً ، كما إذا باع وسَكَتَ عن الثَّمن، فالبيع فاسد عندهما حتى يَمْلِك بالقبض ويجب الثمنُ ، وهو القيمة. (وَلَزمَهُ" مثله حقيقة أو معنى) : أي إن هَلَكَ في يد المشتري وجب عليه المثل الأول : إن قوله : بيعاً فاسداً لا يفيد ما هو المقصود، فإنَّ الفاسد قد يراد به فاسداً، [۱] أنه الباطل، كما أنَّ العلامة القُدُوري في أوَّل (كتاب البيع)) جعل البيع بالميتة فاسداً مع باطل ،بالاتفاق، فعسى أن يتوهم أنَّ المراد منها هاهنا أيضاً بقوله: بيعاً فاسداً هو الباطل، فأخرجه هذا القيد. والثاني : البيع قد يكون فاسداً مع أنَّ كلاً من عوضيه لا يكون مالاً، كما إذا باع شيئاً وسكت عن الثَّمن فانتفى أنَّ كلاً من عوضيه مال فينبغي أن يكون باطلاً، والحالُ أنه فاسد عندهما يترتب عليه أثر البيع الفاسد ، وهو الملك للمشترى بالقبض، ووجوب القيمة عليه، ولا يذهب عليك أنَّ القيمة وإن لم تكن مذكورة حقيقة لكنّها مذكورة. كما صرح به في «الذخيرة» وغيرها.
And he is liable for its equivalent in essence or meaning, just as at the beginning of Kitab al-Quduri (he made the sale of a dead animal fāsid, while it is bāṭil; so he said: 'and both of whose countervalues are property' as a precaution, so that if the fāsid includes the bāṭil, this restriction excludes it from this ruling, which is that the sold item becomes property, while there may be a sale where not both countervalues are property, such as when he sells and remains silent about the price, then the sale is fāsid according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) so that he owns by possession and the price becomes obligatory, which is the value. (And he is liable for its equivalent in essence or meaning): i.e., if it perishes in the buyer's hand, the equivalent is obligatory upon him. The first [reason]: his saying 'a fāsid sale' does not convey what is intended, for fāsid may be used to mean bāṭil, as the scholar al-Quduri at the beginning of (Kitab al-Buyu') made the sale of a dead animal fāsid while it is bāṭil by agreement, so it might be thought that his saying 'a fāsid sale' here also means bāṭil, so he excluded this by the restriction. The second: the sale may be fāsid even though not both of its countervalues are property, such as when he sells something and remains silent about the price, so it is not the case that both countervalues are property, so it should be bāṭil, while it is fāsid according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) so that the effect of a fāsid sale follows it, which is ownership for the buyer by possession, and obligation of the value upon him. And it is not hidden from you that the value, although not mentioned in essence, is mentioned as if it were. As stated in 'al-Dhakhirah' and others.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah expanding on the matn's definition of a fāsid sale. The commentator explains why the matn specifies 'both of whose countervalues are property' (كل من عوضيه مال). The first reason: the word 'fāsid' is sometimes used to mean 'bāṭil' (void), as in the beginning of al-Quduri's text where selling a dead animal is called fāsid although it is actually bāṭil by consensus. To prevent the misunderstanding that every fāsid sale here is bāṭil, the matn adds the condition that both countervalues must be property, because in a bāṭil sale (like selling a dead animal) the subject matter is not property. The second reason: there are sales that are fāsid yet one of the countervalues is not property, for example, when the seller remains silent about the price. In such a case, the price is not mentioned in essence, but the buyer takes possession and becomes liable for the market value (قيمة). According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, the buyer owns the item through possession (قبض) and must pay the value. The value, though not explicitly stated, is considered as if it were mentioned. This reinforces the condition that for a sale to be fāsid (and not bāṭil), the countervalues need not be actual property at the time of contract, as long as the transaction has some basis in property. The commentator affirms that the obligation of the equivalent (مثل) applies to fāsid sales, preparing the discussion for the subsequent ruling on compensation.

Key Terms
بيع فاسد (bayʿ fāsid): A sale that is valid in its essence (contract) but defective due to the absence of a condition (like specifying the price). It produces legal effects: ownership by possession and obligation of compensation.
كل من عوضيه مال (kull min ʿiwaḍayhi māl): The condition that both countervalues (the thing sold and the price) must be considered property (māl) according to the Sharīʿah. This distinguishes a fāsid sale from a bāṭil one where one countervalue is not property (e.g., dead animal).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), what is the ruling on a sale where the seller remains silent about the price but the buyer takes possession?
A) The sale is bāṭil and no ownership transfers.
B) The sale is fāsid; the buyer owns by possession and must pay the market value.
C) The sale is valid because the price is implied.
D) The sale is makrūh but valid.

Key Takeaway
The condition 'both countervalues are property' ensures that fāsid sales are not confused with bāṭil ones, allowing legal effects like ownership by possession even when the price is unstated.

Rescission in Fāsid Sales: Defects in the Substance
Commentary on rescission rights in fāsid sales before and after possession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify defects in a fāsid sale as substance-based or condition-based, and state the Hanafi ruling on the right of rescission for each category.

Core Idea
When the defect is in the countervalues, both parties rescind; when from a condition, only the beneficiary rescinds (Muhammad).

Fāsid sales require a legal remedy. The type of defect determines who can cancel the contract. This lesson explains the general rule for defects inherent in the price or object.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of defect
Determine whether the defect is in the substance of the contract (e.g., ribā) or from an extra condition (e.g., stipulating a gift). The passage distinguishes these two.
The term 'ṣulb al-ʿaqd' means the core of the contract, i.e., the countervalues.
2
Apply the general rule for substance defects
If the defect is in the substance, both parties have the right to rescind before possession, and after possession as long as the item remains in the buyer's ownership.
This is the agreed-upon position of the Hanafi school.
3
Note the exception for condition-based defects
If the defect is from a condition, there is a dispute: Imam Muhammad says only the party benefiting from the condition can rescind, while the other two Imams say both parties can.
The passage cites al-Dhakhīrah for this ikhtilāf.
4
Understand compensation after rescission
After rescission, if the item is fungible (mithlī), the equivalent (mithl) must be returned; if non-fungible (qimī), the value (qīmah) at the time of possession is due.
This applies whether rescission is exercised before or after possession.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

( ولكل منهما "" فسخه قبل القبض، وكذا بعدَهُ ما دام في ملك المشتري إن كان الفساد في صُلب العقدِ كبيع درهم بدرهمين)، أراد" بالفساد في صلب العقدِ الفساد الذي يكون في أحدٍ العوضين، ولِمَن له الشرط إن كان بشرط زائد كشرط أن يُهْدِى له هدية ) : ذَكَرَ في «الدَّخيرة»: إنّ هذا قول محمد له، وأما عندهما فلكل واحدٍ منهما حقُّ الفسخ ؛ لأنَّ الفسخ لحق الشرع لا لحـق أحـد المتبايعين، فإنَّهما راضيان بالعقد. إلا إذا تعدَّر، وإنّما تعتبر قيمته يوم القبض ، وإن زادت قيمته في يده فأتلفه فإنّه دخل في ضمانه بالقبض، فلا تعتبر كالمغصوب كذا في «الكافي»، والقول في القيمة للمشتري ؛ لأنَّ البائع يدعي عليه الزيادة، وهو يُنكر. [١] قوله : حقيقة ; أي صورة ومعنى فيما إذا كان من المثليَّاتِ فالكيلي والوزني والعددي المتقارب ايجب فيه المثل، وفيما إذا كان من ذوات القيم كالحيوان والعدديات المتفاوتة يجب القيمة. [٢] قوله : ولكلِّ منهما ; أي من المتعاقدين فسخه : أي ولاية فسخ البيع الفاسد ، وأما باعتبار الحقِّ فالفسخ حق الله تعالى ; لأنّ إعدام الفساد واجب قبل القبض، وهذا ظاهر ; لأنَّ البيع الفاسد لا يفيد حكمه ، وهو الملك ، فيكون الفسخ امتناعاً منه. وكذا ولاية الفسخ بكل منهما بعده أي بعد القبض ما دام في ملك المشتري ; احترازا عن المسألة الآتية، وهي قوله : «فإن باعه.... الخ ; ففيها سقوط حق الفسخ إن كان الفساد في صلب العقد، وإن لم يكن فيه فلا يكون الفسخ لكل منهما، بل لمن له الشرط ، كما سيأتي آنفاً. [٣] قوله : أراد.... الخ ; لأنَّ صلب الشيء ما يقوم به ذلك الشيء، وقيام العقدِ بالعوضين، فكل فساد تمكّن في أحدِ العوضين يكون فساداً في صلب العقد، كبيع درهم بدرهمين، وبيع ثوب بخمر أو خنزير. [٤]قوله : هذا قول محمد له ، ووجهه : أنَّ منفعة الشرط لما كانت عائدة إليه كان
And each of them has the right to rescind it before possession, and likewise after it as long as it remains in the buyer's ownership, if the defect is in the substance of the contract, like selling a dirham for two dirhams. And it is for the one who has the condition if it is due to an extra condition, like the condition that he give him a gift. In essence: regarding fungible items (mithliyyāt), the equivalent (mithl) is required; and the meaning is the value at the time of possession for non-fungible items (qimiyyāt). ( And each of them has the right to rescind it before possession, and likewise after it as long as it remains in the buyer's ownership, if the defect is in the substance of the contract, like selling a dirham for two dirhams), he intended by 'defect in the substance of the contract' the defect that occurs in one of the two countervalues. And for the one who has the condition if it is due to an extra condition, like the condition that he give him a gift. It is mentioned in al-Dhakhīrah: This is the saying of Muhammad alone. As for according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), each of them has the right to rescind, because rescission is for the right of the Sharīʿah, not for the right of either of the two contracting parties, for they are both satisfied with the contract, unless it is impossible. And only the value on the day of possession is considered, and if its value increases in his hand and he then destroys it, then it entered his guarantee by possession, so it is not considered like the usurped item. This is in al-Kāfī. And the say regarding the value is that of the buyer, because the seller claims an increase against him, and he denies it. [1] His saying: 'in essence': i.e., in form and meaning, when it is from fungible items, like kīlī, waznī, and similar countable items, the equivalent (mithl) is obligatory. And when it is from non-fungible items, like animals and dissimilar countable items, the value (qīmah) is obligatory. [2] His saying: 'And each of them': i.e., the two contracting parties have the right to rescind it, meaning the authority to rescind the fāsid sale. But in terms of right, rescission is the right of Allah, because eliminating the defect is obligatory before possession, and this is apparent, because the fāsid sale does not produce its ruling, which is ownership, so rescission is refraining from it. And likewise the authority to rescind for each of them after it, i.e., after possession, as long as it remains in the buyer's ownership, as a precaution regarding the upcoming issue, which is his saying: 'then if he sells it...' etc.; for in it, the right of rescission falls if the defect is in the substance of the contract, and if it is not in it, then rescission is not for each of them, but for the one who has the condition, as will come shortly. [3] His saying: 'He intended...' etc.; because the substance of a thing is that by which it subsists, and the subsistence of the contract is through the two countervalues, so every defect that takes place in one of the two countervalues is a defect in the substance of the contract, like selling a dirham for two dirhams, and selling a garment for wine or a pig. [4] His saying: 'This is the saying of Muhammad alone', and its reasoning is that since the benefit of the condition reverts to him, it is...
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah discusses the right of rescission (فسخ) in a fāsid sale. The commentator first distinguishes two types of defects: (1) defects in the substance of the contract (فساد في صلب العقد), such as selling one dirham for two dirhams (ribā), where the defect is inherent in the countervalues themselves. In such cases, both parties have the right to rescind the sale both before and after possession, as long as the item remains in the buyer's ownership. (2) Defects arising from an extra condition (بشرط زائد), such as stipulating a gift. In this case, only the party who benefits from the condition (the one who would receive the gift) has the right to rescind. The commentator then cites a report from al-Dhakhīrah stating that this second ruling (that only the party with the condition can rescind) is the opinion of Imam Muhammad alone. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (i.e., the Sahibayn), both parties have the right to rescind, because rescission is the right of Allah (i.e., a legal remedy to eliminate the defect), not a personal right of either party. The sale is defective, and the law intervenes. However, if the defect is in the substance, even the Sahibayn agree that both parties can rescind. The commentator also explains that for fungible items (mithliyyāt), the compensation is the equivalent (mithl), and for non-fungible items (qimiyyāt), it is the value (qīmah) at the time of possession. The value is assessed on the day of possession, and if the buyer increases the value and then destroys it, he is liable for the value at possession, like a usurper. The burden of proof regarding increased value lies with the buyer (denying) against the seller (claiming).

Key Terms
فسخ (faskh): Rescission or cancellation of a fāsid sale, which is a right to annul the contract due to its defect. It can be exercised before or after possession, depending on the type of defect.
فساد في صلب العقد (fasād fī ṣulb al-ʿaqd): A defect that is inherent in the substance of the contract, specifically in one of the countervalues (e.g., ribā or selling a forbidden item). This gives both parties the right to rescind.
شرط زائد (sharṭ zāʾid): An extra condition added to the sale, such as a stipulation to give a gift. The defect arises from this condition, not from the countervalues themselves.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a fāsid sale due to a defect in the substance of the contract (e.g., selling two dirhams for one), who has the right to rescind?
A) Only the buyer
B) Only the seller
C) Both parties
D) Neither party

Key Takeaway
In a fāsid sale with a defect in the substance of the contract, both parties always have the right to rescind.
