Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Rescission for Condition-Based Defects
Intra-Hanafi dispute on rescission rights for condition-based defects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the reasoning of Imam Muhammad and the Sahibayn regarding the right to rescind in a fāsid sale with a condition, and identify the muftā bihī position.

Core Idea
When the defect is from a condition, Imam Muhammad gives rescission only to the beneficiary; the Sahibayn give it to both.

The Hanafi school is not monolithic. This lesson explores a specific ikhtilāf between the eminent Imams on who can rescind a fāsid sale when the defect is a condition. Understanding this dispute sharpens your grasp of legal reasoning (istidlāl) within the madhhab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of defect
The defect is from a condition (sharṭ zāʾid), e.g., a stipulation to give a gift. This is distinct from a substance defect.
2
State Imam Muhammad's position
Only the party who benefits from the condition (the stipulator) has the right to rescind. The reason: the benefit of the condition belongs solely to him.
This is cited as 'قول محمد له' in al-Dhakhīrah.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Both parties can rescind. The reason: rescission is a legal right (ḥaqq al-Sharʿ) to remove the defect, not a personal right of either party.
The text says 'عندهما فلكل واحد منهما حق الفسخ' – according to the two Imams (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf).
4
Compare the proofs
Muhammad: benefit of condition returns to the stipulator. Sahibayn: the defect affects the entire contract, so both have standing to rescind.
5
Determine the practical consequence
If the condition is unfulfilled, under Muhammad only the beneficiary can rescind; under the Sahibayn both can. The muftā bihī is the latter view.
This thamarah al-khilāf affects who can sue for rescission in court.

Right of Rescission in Fasid Sale: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Expands the ruling on rescission in fasid sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on who has the right to rescind a fasid sale when the defect is an extra condition, and compare the views of Imam Muhammad with the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf).

Core Idea
Rescission is the right of Allah, not the parties — that determines who may rescind.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the rules for rescinding a voidable (fasid) sale. The key issue is who can rescind when the defect arises from an extra condition, such as a promise of a gift. Imam Muhammad holds that only the party benefiting from the condition can rescind, while Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that both parties can. The difference stems from whether the right of rescission is a private right or a religious duty (haqq Allah).

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the base ruling: both parties have the right to rescind a fasid sale before delivery, and after delivery as long as the item is with the buyer, provided the defect is in the essence of the contract. But if the defect is an extra condition, the text says only the one with the condition can rescind — this is Imam Muhammad's view.
The base text (matn) initially appears to give the right to both, but his qualification (condition case) introduces the dispute.
2
Identify the point of ikhtilaf
The dispute is over who has the right to rescind when the defect is a shart za'id (extra condition). Imam Muhammad: only the beneficiary of the condition. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: each party has the right.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; all three are authoritative.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Imam Muhammad's reasoning: the benefit of the condition returns to the one who stipulated it; allowing the other party to rescind would nullify that benefit. Therefore, only the beneficiary may rescind to preserve the condition's benefit.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf respond: rescission is for the right of Allah (haqq Allah) to eliminate the defect, not a private right. Both parties are content with the contract, but the Law requires nullification. Thus, the right belongs to both, regardless of the condition's benefit.
The phrase 'عندهما' (according to the two) signals their position. The commentary emphasizes that the contract is voidable and the defect must be removed.
5
Determine the thamarah and tarjih
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf): if the buyer stipulated a gift, under Muhammad only the buyer can rescind; under the two, both can. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) view is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, as indicated by the commentary and confirmed by al-Dhakhira.
Tarjih authority: al-Marghinani and al-Dhakhira prefer the view of the two.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

حقيقة" في ذوات الأمثال والمثلُ معنى : وهو القيمة وقت القبض في ذوات القيم. ( ولكل منهما "" فسخه قبل القبض، وكذا بعدَهُ ما دام في ملك المشتري إن كان الفساد في صُلب العقدِ كبيع درهم بدرهمين)، أراد" بالفساد في صلب العقدِ الفساد الذي يكون في أحدٍ العوضين، ولِمَن له الشرط إن كان بشرط زائد كشرط أن يُهْدِى له هدية ) : ذَكَرَ في «الدَّخيرة»: إنّ هذا قول محمد له، وأما عندهما فلكل واحدٍ منهما حقُّ الفسخ ؛ لأنَّ الفسخ لحق الشرع لا لحـق أحـد المتبايعين، فإنَّهما راضيان بالعقد. إلا إذا تعدَّر، وإنّما تعتبر قيمته يوم القبض ، وإن زادت قيمته في يده فأتلفه فإنّه دخل في ضمانه بالقبض، فلا تعتبر كالمغصوب كذا في «الكافي»، والقول في القيمة للمشتري ؛ لأنَّ البائع يدعي عليه الزيادة، وهو يُنكر.

[٢] قوله : ولكلِّ منهما ؛ أي من المتعاقدين فسخه : أي ولاية فسخ البيع الفاسد ، وأما باعتبار الحقِّ فالفسخ حق الله تعالى ؛ لأنّ إعدام الفساد واجب قبل القبض، وهذا ظاهر ؛ لأنَّ البيع الفاسد لا يفيد حكمه ، وهو الملك ، فيكون الفسخ امتناعاً منه. وكذا ولاية الفسخ بكل منهما بعده أي بعد القبض ما دام في ملك المشتري ؛ احترازا عن المسألة الآتية، وهي قوله : «فإن باعه.... الخ ؛ ففيها سقوط حق الفسخ إن كان الفساد في صلب العقد، وإن لم يكن فيه فلا يكون الفسخ لكل منهما، بل لمن له الشرط ، كما سيأتي آنفاً. [٣] قوله : أراد.... الخ ؛ لأنَّ صلب الشيء ما يقوم به ذلك الشيء، وقيام العقدِ بالعوضين، فكل فساد تمكّن في أحدِ العوضين يكون فساداً في صلب العقد، كبيع درهم بدرهمين، وبيع ثوب بخمر أو خنزير. [٤]قوله : هذا قول محمد الله له ، ووجهه : أنَّ منفعة الشرط لما كانت عائدة إليه كان الفسخ له دون الآخر؛ لأنَّ في فسخ الآخر إبطال حق من له منفعة الشرط، وهو تصحيح العقد، بإبطال ذلك الشرط. كذا في «كمال الدراية»).
And each of them has the right to rescind it before delivery, and likewise after it as long as it remains in the buyer's possession if the defect is in the essence of the contract, such as selling a dirham for two dirhams. And the one who has the condition [has the right] if it is due to an extra condition, such as the condition that he give him a gift. 'Reality' in fungible items, and similarity in meaning: it is the value at the time of delivery in non-fungibles. He said: '...and for each of them the right to rescind before delivery, and likewise after it as long as it remains in the buyer's possession if the defect is in the essence of the contract, like selling a dirham for two dirhams.' He intended by 'defect in the essence of the contract' the defect that occurs in one of the two considerations. 'And for the one who has the condition [the right] if it is due to an extra condition, like the condition that he give him a gift.' He mentioned in al-Dhakhira: This is the statement of Muhammad alone. As for according to the two [Imams], each of them has the right of rescission, because rescission is for the right of the Law, not for the right of either of the two parties, for they are both content with the contract, unless it becomes impossible. And only its value on the day of delivery is considered; if its value increases in his possession and he destroys it, then it enters his guarantee upon delivery, so it is not considered like a usurped item, as in al-Kafi. And the say regarding the value is with the buyer because the seller claims the increase against him and he denies it. [1] His saying: 'Reality' means form and meaning in fungibles, so in measurable, weighable, and similar countable items the like is required, and in non-fungibles like animals and varying countable items the value is required. [2] His saying: 'And for each of them' means from the two contractors: 'rescind it' i.e., the authority to rescind the voidable sale. As for in terms of right, the rescission is the right of Allah the Exalted because eliminating the defect is obligatory before delivery, and this is apparent because the voidable sale does not produce its effect, which is ownership, so rescission is abstention from it. Likewise, the authority to rescind for each of them after delivery as long as it remains in the buyer's possession is a safeguard against the upcoming issue, which is his saying: 'If the buyer sells it... etc.'; in that case the right of rescission is dropped if the defect is in the essence of the contract, and if it is not then the rescission is not for each of them, but for the one who has the condition, as will come shortly. [3] His saying: 'He intended... etc.' because the essence of a thing is that by which that thing stands, and the contract stands by the two considerations. So every defect that occurs in one of the two considerations is a defect in the essence of the contract, like selling a dirham for two dirhams, and selling a garment for wine or a pig. [4] His saying: 'This is the statement of Muhammad alone' and its justification is that since the benefit of the condition returns to him, the rescission is for him to the exclusion of the other, because in the other's rescission there is annulment of the right of the one who benefits from the condition, and it is rectification of the contract by annulling that condition. As in Kamal al-Diraya.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on the rights of rescission in a fasid (voidable) sale. The author first states the base ruling: both parties have the right to rescind a fasid sale before delivery, and also after delivery as long as the item is still in the buyer's possession—but only if the defect is in the core of the contract (e.g., selling a dirham for two dirhams, i.e., riba al-fadl). If the defect is due to an extra condition (e.g., a condition to give a gift), then only the party benefiting from that condition has the right to rescind. The commentary clarifies that 'reality' (haqiqah) refers to fungible items where the like (mithl) is owed; for non-fungibles, the value (qimah) at the time of delivery is owed. The text then reports an intra-Hanafi dispute: according to Imam Muhammad, only the one with the condition can rescind in a condition-based defect; according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the two), both parties have the right because rescission is a right of Allah (shar'), not of the individuals, unless the condition makes it impossible. The commentary explains that the rescission authority is for the benefit of the Law to eliminate the defect; it does not depend on the parties' consent. Further notes: if the buyer destroys the item after delivery, its value on delivery day is considered (not later increase), and the buyer's word is taken regarding the value because the seller claims an increase.

Key Terms
الفسخ (al-faskh): Rescission of a voidable (fasid) contract, undoing it from the beginning.
البيع الفاسد (al-bay' al-fasid): A sale that is valid in essence but defective in description (e.g., riba or condition), making it voidable.
فساد في صلب العقد (fasad fi sulb al-'aqd): A defect that lies in the very essence of the contract, specifically in the consideration (one of the two exchanged items).
حقيقة (haqiqah): In this context, 'reality' refers to the fact that in fungible items (mithliyat) the like is owed, and in non-fungibles (qimiyat) the value is owed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon (mufta bihi) view in the Hanafi school, who has the right to rescind a fasid sale when the defect arises from an extra condition (shart za'id)?
A) Only the party benefiting from the condition
B) Only the party not benefiting from the condition
C) Both parties
D) Neither party

Key Takeaway
In a fasid sale due to an extra condition, the relied-upon Hanafi view gives both parties the right of rescission because the right belongs to Allah, not to the individuals.

Buyer's Disposal in a Fasid Sale: Validity and the Right of Rescission
Rulings on buyer's disposal in fasid sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a buyer disposes of the item in a fasid sale and explain the rationale based on the precedence of human rights over divine rights.

Core Idea
A buyer's disposal in a fasid sale is valid; the human right overrides the rescission right, but the buyer owes the value.

When a sale is fasid (voidable), the buyer acquires ownership by delivery. If the buyer then disposes of the item—selling, gifting, or emancipating—the question arises: can the original seller still rescind? This passage rules that such dispositions are valid and the rescission right drops. The key legal principle is that once a third-party human right attaches, it prevails over the divine right (haqq Allah) embodied in the rescission remedy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disposition and its effect
The buyer sells, gifts with delivery, emancipates, endows, pawns, or bequeaths the item. Determine whether each disposition is valid (sahih) and how it affects the possibility of returning the item.
The disposition must be a valid (non-fasid) transfer to someone other than the original seller.
2
State the ruling on validity and rescission
The buyer's disposition is sahih (valid), the right of rescission (faskh) drops, and the buyer owes the market value (qimah) at the time of disposal.
If the impediment is later removed (e.g., the third party returns the item), the rescission right revives.
3
Understand the rationale: conflict of rights
The rescission is a right of Allah (shar'). The disposition creates a human right (haqq al-'abd) for the third party. When they conflict, the human right is given preponderance due to need (hajah).
This is a principle of istihsan: human need overrides the general legal remedy of rescission.
4
Note the seller's right to the price
After rescission, the seller cannot take back the item without first returning the price. The item is held in pledge (mahbus) for the price, analogous to a pawn (rahn) but with full value guarantee.
This ensures the seller does not unjustly retain both the item and the price.

Source Text

(If the buyer sells it, or gives it as a gift and delivers it, or emancipates it (a slave), it is valid, and he owes its value, and the right of rescission drops) because the right of the servant becomes attached to it, and he [the buyer] only rescinds a right of Allah the Exalted; when the right of Allah and the right of the servant coincide, the right of the servant is given preponderance due to his need. 'Or gives it as a gift and delivers it'—he restricted it with delivery because a gift does not confer ownership except by it, unlike a sale. 'Or emancipates it after taking possession'—and the consequences of emancipation like tadbir (promise of freedom upon death) and istilad (giving birth to a child of the master) are like emancipation. 'Or endows it (waqf) or pawns it or bequeaths it'—the sale is valid and effective in all the previous cases, and he owes its value due to impossibility of return, and the right of rescission drops; if the impediment is removed, the rescission returns. So the right of the servant is given preponderance due to his need over the right of the Law due to its independence; and because these dispositions are with the permission of the seller, so he cannot revoke them because a person's effort to revoke what has been done by his own authority is rejected. Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on what happens when the buyer in a fasid sale disposes of the item (e.g., sells it to a third party, gives it as a gift with delivery, emancipates a slave, etc.). The ruling states: such dispositions are valid, the buyer owes the value of the item (since returning the item is now impossible), and the right of rescission (faskh) drops. The commentary explains the rationale: once the buyer disposes of the item, a right of a human being (the third party, the donee, the freed slave) becomes attached. The rescission is a right of Allah (shar'), but when human rights compete with divine rights, human rights are given precedence due to need. The commentary also restricts the dispositions: the sale must be a valid (sahih) sale to a non-seller; if sold fasid or back to the original seller, it is different. For a gift, delivery is required. Emancipation and its derivatives (tadbir, istilad) are included. Waqf, pawn, bequest also are valid. In all cases, the buyer owes the market value at the time of disposal. The second part states that the seller cannot reclaim the item without first returning the price, because after rescission, the item is considered held in pledge (mahbus) for the price, similar to a pawn (rahn) but with full value guarantee.

Key Terms
حق العبد (haqq al-'abd): The right of a human being (e.g., the third-party buyer, donee, freed slave) that attaches to the item after disposition.
قيمة (qimah): The market value of the item at the time of (disposition), owed by the buyer to the seller when return of the specific item is impossible.
محبوس (mahbus): Held in pledge; the item is effectively a security for the return of the price after rescission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a fasid sale, the buyer emancipates the purchased slave. What happens to the original seller's right of rescission?
A) The right of rescission remains, and the seller can take back the slave despite emancipation
B) The right of rescission drops because emancipation creates a human right, and the buyer owes the value
C) The right of rescission transfers to the freed slave
D) The sale becomes valid automatically and rescission is no longer possible

Key Takeaway
A buyer's valid disposal in a fasid sale is upheld, and the seller's rescission is replaced by a claim for value, due to the preponderance of human rights over divine rights.

Disposition of Item by Buyer in a Void Sale
Explains effects of buyer's disposal in void sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the effect of a buyer's disposition in a void sale and explain why the seller's rescission right is dropped based on the principle of preferring haqq al-'abd over haqq Allah.

Core Idea
A buyer's valid disposition in a void sale cuts off the seller's rescission right because the servant's need outweighs the divine right.

When a buyer in a void sale disposes of the item before rescission, what happens? The Hanafi school holds that the disposition is valid and the seller's right to rescind is dropped. This lesson examines the reasoning based on the interplay between the right of the servant (haqq al-'abd) and the right of Allah (haqq Allah), and the practical consequences including liability for value and profit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the void sale and the buyer's actions
The passage covers void sales (fāsid) and buyer's actions: sale, gift with delivery, manumission, endowment, pledge, bequest.
Disposition must be valid and to a third party, not the original seller.
2
Determine the legal effect
The disposition is valid (sahih), the seller's rescission right is dropped, and the buyer must pay the item's value (qimah).
Value is market value at time of disposition.
3
Understand the underlying principle
The reasoning: haqq al-'abd (right of the servant) attaches through the disposition and is given precedence over haqq Allah (the rescission right) due to the servant's need.
This is a usul principle: haqq al-'abd is preferred over haqq Allah when they conflict.
4
Apply the principle to the seller's right to reclaim the item
The seller cannot reclaim without returning the price; the item is detained as security for the price, similar to a pledge.
After rescission, the item is like a collateral until price is returned.
5
Analyze the profit ruling
The seller's profit from the price is halal because the price is not tainted; the buyer's profit from the item is haram and must be given in charity because the item is impure from the void contract.
The difference: the item is specified and affected by the void contract, while the price is not.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

If the buyer sells it, or gifts it and delivers it, or frees it, it is valid, and he owes its value, and the right of rescission is dropped. The seller does not take it until he returns its price. If the buyer sells it, or gifts it and delivers it, or frees it, it is valid, and he owes its value, and the right of rescission is dropped; because the right of the servant becomes attached to it. Rescission is only a right of Allah Almighty. When the right of Allah Almighty and the right of the servant come together, the right of the servant is given precedence due to his need. The seller does not take it until he returns the price: meaning the seller, when he rescinds the void sale, does not take the sold item until he returns the price; because the sold item is detained for the price after the rescission. Rescission is for him, not for the other; because in the other's rescission there is nullification of the right of one who benefits from the condition, which is correcting the contract by nullifying that condition. Thus in 'Kamil al-Dirayah'. [1] His statement: 'If the buyer sells it' means the buyer sells the voidly purchased item to someone other than his seller, and we only restrict it with these two restrictions because if he sells it voidly it does not prevent revocation, like a sale with an option because it is not binding, and if he sells it to his seller it is a revocation of the sale, as established in its place. Or he gifts it and delivers it. And he restricted it with delivery because gift does not confer ownership except with it, unlike sale. Or he frees it after taking possession, and the consequences of freeing like tadbir and istilad are like freeing. Or he endows it, or pawns it, or bequeaths it; the sale is valid and effective in all the preceding cases, and he owes its value because return is impossible, and the right of rescission is dropped; if the impediment ceases, the rescission returns; details are in the extensive books. [2] His statement: 'because...' etc.; its clarification: the buyer owned the sold item by possession, so his disposition in it is effective, and after disposition the seller's right of recovery is cut off, whether the disposition is one that does not admit revocation, like manumission, or admits it, like sale; because by these dispositions the right of the servant becomes attached, and by the void sale the right of the law becomes attached, which is rescission. So the right of the servant prevailed due to his need over the right of the law due to its independence; and because these dispositions are by the authorization of the seller, so he does not have the power to revoke them; because a person's effort to revoke what has been completed by his own doing is rejected. [3] His statement: 'because the sold item is detained for the price' is like a pledge; for it becomes detained for the debt, except that the pledge is guaranteed for the lesser of its value and the debt, and here the sold item is guaranteed for all of its value, as in usurpation. Thus in 'al-Kifaya'. If the seller dies, the buyer is more entitled to it until he takes his price, and the seller's profit from his price is permissible after exchange, but the buyer's profit from his sold item is not permissible, so he gives it in charity. If the seller dies, the buyer is more entitled to it until he takes his price: meaning he sold something voidly and exchange occurred, then he rescinded the sale, then the seller died, so the buyer has the right to detain the sold item until he takes the price, and he is not equal with the seller's creditors. And the seller's profit from his price is permissible after exchange, but the buyer's profit from his sold item is not permissible, so he gives it in charity. The example: He sold a slave-girl voidly for dirhams or dinars and they exchanged, then the buyer sold the slave-girl and profited; the profit is not permissible for him. But if the seller profited in the price, the profit is permissible for him. And the difference: meaning that the profit is permissible for the seller of the slave-girl in the price, and not permissible for her buyer. The gist of the difference: that the slave-girl is something specified, so the contract attaches to her, so impurity takes effect in her.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling on what happens when the buyer of an item sold in a void (fasid) sale disposes of that item before rescission. The text, from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, a commentary on al-Wiqayah, explains that if the buyer sells, gifts with delivery, or frees the item, the disposition is valid. The buyer must pay the item's value, not return the specific item. The seller's right to rescind (faskh) falls away because the buyer's actions create a right of the servant (haqq al-'abd) that takes precedence over the right of Allah (haqq Allah) found in the rescission right. The reasoning is that the buyer's need outweighs the legal interest. The seller cannot reclaim the item without first returning the price, as the item is held as security for the price after rescission. The commentary then expands: the buyer must sell the item in a valid sale to a third party, not a void sale (which doesn't prevent revocation) and not back to the original seller (which would be a revocation). If the buyer gifts it, delivery is necessary for ownership transfer. Freeing and its analogues (tadbir, istilad) also prevent return. Waqf, pledge, or bequest are also valid. The value (qimah) is owed because the specific item cannot be returned. The right to rescind revives if the impediment is later removed. The second part discusses the scenario where the seller dies after rescission but before the buyer recovers the price; the buyer has priority over other creditors, similar to a pledgee's priority. Lastly, the text addresses profits: the seller's profit from the price (after exchange) is permissible (tayyib) for him, but the buyer's profit from the item is not permissible and must be given in charity. The difference is that the item (slave-girl) is specified and impure from the void contract, while the price is not specified in the same way.

Key Terms
البيع الفاسد (al-bay‘ al-fāsid): A sale that is defective in a condition not essential to its validity, such as a sale with an unknown price, which is invalid but can be corrected.
الفسخ (al-faskh): The cancellation of a contract, here referring to the seller's right to rescind a void sale.
حق العبد (haqq al-‘abd): The right of a human being, such as ownership or freedom, which takes precedence over purely religious obligations.
القيمة (al-qīmah): Market value of the item, which the buyer must pay when he cannot return the specific item.
الحبس (al-ḥabs): Detention of an item as security for a debt or claim, here the seller detains the item until price is returned.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a void sale, if the buyer sells the item to a third party before rescission, what happens to the seller's right of rescission?
A) The seller can still rescind and reclaim the item from the third party.
B) The seller's right of rescission is dropped and the buyer owes the value.
C) The seller can rescind only if the buyer returns the price.
D) The rescission is transferred to the third party.

Key Takeaway
In a void sale, the buyer's valid disposition cuts off the seller's rescission and the buyer owes the item's value; the seller's profit from the price is halal, the buyer's profit from the item is haram.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Profit in a Fasid Sale: The Buyer's Charity and Seller's Permission
Ruling with proof and distinction based on property type

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on profit in a fasid sale for the buyer versus the seller and explain the distinction based on the determinate nature of the property.

Core Idea
In a fasid sale, the buyer's profit on the purchased good is tainted and must be given in charity; the seller's profit on the price is permissible because currency is non-determinate.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah tackles a seemingly contradictory ruling: after a fasid (voidable) sale, the buyer cannot keep any profit from reselling the purchased item, but the seller can keep profit earned from the price money. The key lies in the nature of the property—determinate goods like a specific slave-girl versus non-determinate currency like dirhams. Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying the Hanafi position correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
Locate the two rulings in the matn: the buyer's profit on the sold item is not permissible and must be given in charity; the seller's profit on the price is permissible after taking possession.
The matn states: 'ولا للمشتري ربح مبيعه فيتصدق به' and 'وطاب للبائع ربح ثَمَنِهِ بعد التقابض'.
2
Understand the scenario
The scenario involves a fasid sale of a slave-girl (determinate good) for dirhams/dinars (non-determinate price). Both parties take possession. The buyer resells the slave-girl at a profit; the seller uses the dirhams and profits.
The fasid sale could be due to a missing condition (e.g., unknown delivery time).
3
Trace the distinction (farq)
The commentator explains that the slave-girl is determinate (muta'ayyin), so the contract attaches to her specific entity. The corrupt ownership (fasid milk) taints any profit derived from her. Dirhams are non-determinate, so the contract does not attach to specific coins; the taint is only a weak doubt (shubhah) that does not prohibit profit.
The distinction is based on two types of khubth (impurity): due to lack of ownership and due to corrupt ownership.
4
Examine the dalil
The hadith 'نهى عن شبهة الربا والريبة' (forbade the doubt of riba and suspicion) is cited as a proof that any proximity to riba is prohibited. The buyer's profit from a fasid sale resembles riba because it comes from corrupt ownership.
This hadith is zanni both in transmission and meaning, but it supports the Hanafi principle of blocking means to riba.
5
Apply to other cases
The commentator extends the same principle to cases of deposit and usurpation: a depositor or usurper who profits from either determinate or non-determinate goods must give the profit in charity due to lack of ownership. However, in a fasid sale (corrupt ownership), only profit from determinate goods is tainted.
This shows the gradation: lack of ownership > corrupt ownership; determinate > non-determinate.

Dirhams and dinars are not determinate in the contract, so the contract is not attached to their specific entities, so impurity is not established in their profit. That is because impurity is of two types: 1. Impurity due to apparent non-ownership. 2. Or impurity due to corruption of ownership. And wealth is also of two types: 1. Determinate wealth like commodities. 2. Non-determinate wealth like currency. So impurity due to non-ownership operates on both types, just as a depositor or usurper, if he disposes of commodities or currency and profits, must give charity from the profit according to Imam Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Because the contract regarding determinate property is attached to another's wealth apparently, so the reality of impurity is established. And regarding non-determinate property, it is attached to another's wealth in terms of the soundness of the sold item and valuation of the price, so another's ownership becomes a means to profit in some respect, so a doubt (shubhah) of impurity is established. As for impurity due to corruption of ownership, it operates on determinate property but not on non-determinate, because corruption of ownership is less than non-ownership, so the reality of impurity there turns into a doubt here, and it is considered? And a doubt of impurity attached to non-determinate there is a doubt of a doubt here, so it is not considered. [1] His statement: 'determinate' – the meaning of determinacy is that if he sells a specific slave-girl, for example, he cannot give another slave-girl, and the contract is attached to her, and the profit came from this purchased slave-girl, so the profit came from the substitution of something owned with corrupt ownership, so impurity is established, unlike dirhams and dinars, because he can give other than what was in his wallet at the time of the contract, and the contract is not attached to them, so impurity is not established in them. [2] His statement: 'forbade...etc.' – from Umar ibn al-Khattab: 'Indeed the last verse revealed was the verse of riba, and indeed...'
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage from 'Zubdat al-Nihayah' and its commentary 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah' deals with the ruling on profit in a fasid (voidable) sale after possession (qabd). It begins by stating a related ruling: if the seller dies after a rescinded sale, the buyer has the right to detain the sold item until he receives his price, and he is not treated equally with the seller's other creditors. The commentary explains this by analogy to a pledgee's right over a pledgor's estate. The main issue, however, is the permissibility of profit. The matn states: 'And the seller's profit on his price is permissible after taking possession, but the buyer's profit on his sold item is not permissible, so he must give it in charity.' The commentary provides the scenario: a person sells a slave-girl in a fasid sale for dirhams or dinars, and both parties take possession. The buyer then sells the slave-girl and makes a profit. That profit is not permissible (la yatibu) for the buyer; he must give it in charity. However, if the seller profits in the price (e.g., by using the dirhams in a trade), that profit is permissible. The commentary then explains the distinction (farq) between the two cases. The key is that the slave-girl is a determinate item (muta'ayyin) – the contract is attached to her specific entity. Because the original sale was fasid (corrupt ownership), any profit derived from her is tainted with khubth (impurity/impermissibility). In contrast, dirhams and dinars are non-determinate (ghayr muta'ayyin) – the contract does not attach to their specific coins but to the generic type. Therefore, profit from the price does not carry the same taint. The commentary further elaborates on two types of khubth: (1) due to lack of ownership (e.g., deposit or usurpation) and (2) due to corruption of ownership (fasid sale). The first type operates on both determinate and non-determinate goods, as seen in the case of a depositor or usurper who profits – the profit is not permissible and must be given in charity according to Imam Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani. The second type (fasid sale) operates only on determinate goods, because corruption of ownership is a lesser degree than lack of ownership. For non-determinate goods, the taint is only a doubt (shubhah), and a doubt of a doubt is not sufficient to prohibit the profit. A hadith is cited: the Prophet forbade 'the doubt of riba and suspicion.' This supports the principle that any proximity to riba is prohibited. Overall, the passage demonstrates the Hanafi method of distinguishing rulings based on the nature of the property (determinate vs. non-determinate) and the type of legal impediment.

Key Terms
الخبث (al-khubth): Legal impurity or impermissibility tainting a transaction or profit due to lack of ownership or corrupt ownership.
المتعين (al-muta'ayyin): Determinate property that is specified and cannot be replaced by another of the same type in a contract (e.g., specific slave-girl).
شبهة (shubhah): A doubt or resemblance that brings a ruling close to prohibition, e.g., profit from non-determinate goods in a fasid sale is only a shubhah, not full khubth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a fasid sale where the buyer profits by reselling the purchased item (determinate good), what must the buyer do with that profit according to the Hanafi school?
A) Keep it because profit follows ownership.
B) Return it to the original seller.
C) Give it in charity (sadaqah).
D) Destroy it to avoid any trace of riba.

Key Takeaway
In a fasid sale, the buyer's profit on a determinate good is tainted and must be given in charity, while the seller's profit on the non-determinate price is permissible.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Shubha in Profit from Invalid Sales: Determinate vs. Indeterminate Goods
Ruling with proof: explains how shubha affects profit from fasid sales.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between khi khubuth arising from lack of ownership versus defective ownership, and apply the Hanafi distinction between determinate and non-determinate goods to determine whether profit from an invalid sale must be given in charity.

Core Idea
Profit from invalid sale of determinate goods is impure and must be given in charity; for dirhams/dinars, it's only a weak semblance and ignored.

In this passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, we learn that not all profits from invalid (fasid) sales are treated equally. The key lies in two variables: the type of khi khubuth (impurity) involved—whether due to lack of ownership or defective ownership—and whether the sold item is determinate (like a specific commodity) or indeterminate (like currency). Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the hadith that prohibits both riba and doubt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of khi khubuth
The passage distinguishes between impurity due to absolute lack of ownership (e.g., deposit, usurpation) and impurity due to defective ownership (e.g., fasid sale). Each type has a different scope.
2
Identify the two types of wealth
Wealth is either determinate (like commodities, specific items) or non-determinate (like dirhams/dinars which are not specified in the contract).
3
Apply khi khubuth li 'adam al-milk to both types
Impurity from lack of ownership applies to both determinate and non-determinate goods. For determinate, the contract attaches to another's property clearly; for non-determinate, it attaches indirectly, creating a shubha.
4
Apply khi khubuth li fasad al-milk to determinate only
Impurity from defective ownership only applies to determinate goods. For non-determinate goods, the impurity becomes a shubha, then a shubha al-shubha, which is disregarded.
5
Understand the rationale for ignoring shubha al-shubha
If shubha al-shubha were considered, nearly all trade would be tainted, closing the door of commerce. The principle is that original permissibility prevails unless clear impurity exists.

Source Text

البيع الفاسد والمكروه
أن المبيع متعين في العقد فيكون فيه خبث ؛ بسبب فسادِ الملك، وفي فسادِ الملكِ عدم الملك ، فالشبهة ملحقة بالحقيقة في الحرمة ؛ فإن النَّبيَّ صلى الله عليه وسلم : «نَهَى عن الربا والريبة» فيتصدقُ به، والدراهم والدنانير لا يتعيَّنُ في العقد، فلا يتعلق العقد بعينها، فلا يتمكن الخبث في ربحها ؛ وذلك لأنَّ الخبث على نوعين :
١. خبث لعدم الملك ظاهر.
٢. أو خبث لفساد الملك.
والمالُ أيضاً على نوعين :
١. مال يتعين كالعروض.
٢. ومال لا يتعين كالنقود.
فالخبث لعدم الملك يعملُ في النوعين، كما أنَّ المودع أو الغاصب إذا تصرف في العروض أو النقود وربح يتصدق بالربح عند الإمام الله ومحمد الله ؛ لأنَّ العقد فيما يتعين يتعلق بمال غيره ظاهراً ، فتمكن حقيقة الخبث ، وفيما لا يتعين يتعلق بمال غيره من حيث سلامة المبيع به، وتقدير الثمن، فصار ملك الغير وسيلة إلى الربح من وجه، فتمكن فيه شبهة الخبث. وأما الخبث لفساد الملك، فيعمل فيما يتعيّن لا فيما لا يتعيَّن ؛ لأنَّ فساد الملكِ دون عدم الملك، فينقلب حقيقة الخبث هناك شبهة هاهنا، فيعتبر ويتعلق شبهة الخبث فيما لا يتعين هناك شبهة الشبهة هاهنا فلا يعتبر.
[ ١ ] قوله : متعين ؛ ومعنى التعيّن أنه إذا باع جارية معينة مثلاً ليس له أن يعطي جارية أخرى، وما تعلق العقد بها ، وحصل الربح من هذه الجارية المشتراة، فقد كان الربح حاصلاً من بدل المملوكِ ملكاً فاسداً ، فيتمكَّنُ الخبث بخلاف الدراهم والدنانير، فإن له أن يعطي غير ما هو في كيسه وقت العقد من الدراهم والدنانير، وما لم يتعلق العقد بهما، فلا يتمكن الخبث فيها. [٢]قوله : نهى... الخ ؛ عن عمر بن الخطاب : إنَّ آخر ما نزلت آية الربا، وإنَّ رسول الله ، قبض ولم يفسرها : فدعوا الربا والريبة ، رواه ابن ماجه ، والدَّارِمي ، يعني : هي ثابتة غير منسوخة ، لكنَّ رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قبض ولم يفسرها بحيث يحيط بجميع جزئياته وموادّها ، فينبغي لكم أن تدَعُوا الرِّبا الصَّريح وما يشبه الأمر فيه تورعاً واحتياطاً، هذا ما يفهم من ظاهر سوق العبارة. وقال الطيبي ) : يعني إنَّ هذه الآية ثابتةٌ غير منسوخة، غير مشتبهة ؛ فلذلك لم يفسرها النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فأجروه على ما هي عليه ولا ترتابوا فيها واتركوا الحيلة في حل الربا. كذا في «اللمعات».
[1 ] قوله : فغير متعيّنة ؛ لأنَّ المشتري لو أشار إليهما بهذه الدراهم والدنانير له أن يتركها ويدفع غيرها إلى البائع بلا نكير. (٢) قوله : فيكون فيها شبهة الشبهة؛ لأنَّ تعلّق سلامة المبيع، أو تقدير الثَّمن اللذين كانا شبهة خبث ؛ لحصولهما بمالِ الغيرِ من كلِّ وجه لم يبق كذلك ، بل بما له فيه شائبة ملك. كذا في «العناية»(۳).
[۳] قوله : ولا اعتبار لها ؛ لأنها لو كانت معتبرةً لكان ما دونها معتبرة أيضاً، فلا يكون البيع خالياً عن شبهة الشبهة ، فيفسد بابُ التِّجارة ألا وهو مفتوح ؛ ولأنَّ الأصل في الكسب الشبهة، فقد عدل عن هذا الأصل في حق الشبهة، فبقي ما انحط عنها على الأصل. كذا في «النهاية».
The invalid and disliked sale [chapter]. That the sold item is determinate in the contract, so there is impurity in it due to defective ownership, and in defective ownership there is lack of ownership. So the doubt is attached to reality in prohibition, for the Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited riba and doubt. So one gives it in charity. But dirhams and dinars are not determinate in the contract, so the contract does not attach to their specific entities, so impurity does not take hold in their profit. That is because impurity is of two types: 1. Impurity due to apparent lack of ownership. 2. Or impurity due to defective ownership. And wealth is also of two types: 1. Determinate wealth like commodities. 2. Non-determinate wealth like currencies. Impurity due to lack of ownership applies to both types, just as if a depositee or usurper transacts in commodities or currencies and gains profit, he gives the profit in charity according to Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad, because the contract regarding determinate goods attaches to someone else's property apparently, so the reality of impurity takes hold. Regarding non-determinate goods, it attaches to someone else's property from the perspective of the soundness of the sold item and the setting of the price, so someone else's property becomes a means to profit in one respect, so a semblance of impurity takes hold. As for impurity due to defective ownership, it applies to determinate goods, not to non-determinate ones, because defective ownership is below lack of ownership. So the reality of impurity turns into a semblance here, so it is considered. And a semblance of impurity attached to non-determinate goods there becomes a semblance of a semblance here, so it is not considered.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a ruling with proof (hukm with dalil). It addresses the concept of shubha (doubt/ambiguity) in profit from an invalid (fasid) sale. The author explains that khi khubuth (corruption/impurity) in profit arises from two sources: (1) actual lack of ownership (as in a deposit or usurpation), and (2) defective ownership (as in a fasid sale). The ruling distinguishes between determinable goods (like a specific slave or commodity) and non-determinable goods (like dirhams and dinars). For determinable goods, the contract attaches to a specific item; if that item was owned defectively, the khi khubuth is real and the profit must be given in charity. For non-determinable goods, the contract does not attach to specific coins; the khi khubuth is only a semblance (shubha) of impurity, and a further semblance (shubha al-shubha) is ignored. The hadith 'He prohibited riba and riba (doubt)' is quoted as proof, with interpretation from the commentary that the verse on riba was revealed but not fully explained by the Prophet, so one must avoid both clear riba and anything resembling it out of precaution. The text then elaborates that ignoring shubha al-shubha prevents the whole door of trade from being closed, and the original principle in earnings is permissibility unless there is a clear impurity.

Key Terms
الخبث (al-khubth): Impurity or corruption in the profit due to lack of ownership or defective ownership.
الشبهة (al-shubhah): Doubt or ambiguity that resembles a prohibited element; here, it refers to a suspicion of riba that taints the profit.
التعين (al-ta'ayyun): The quality of being specifically identified in the contract; a sold item that is determinate cannot be substituted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does khi khubuth due to defective ownership not apply to profit from dirhams and dinars?
A) Because dirhams and dinars are not owned defectively.
B) Because they are not determinate in the contract, so the khi khubuth is only a semblance of a semblance (shubha al-shubha) and is ignored.
C) Because the Prophet explicitly allowed profit from dirhams and dinars in fasid sales.
D) Because khi khubuth only applies to lack of ownership, not defective ownership.

Key Takeaway
Determinacy of the sold item determines the ruling on profit from fasid sales: determinate goods require charity, non-determinate goods do not.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Shubhat al-Shubha: Why a Weak Suspicion Does Not Affect Profit
Ruling on the effect of specification on suspicion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the difference between a direct suspicion (shubha) and a weak suspicion (shubhat al-shubha) in sales, and apply the rule that the latter does not invalidate a contract or render profit impure.

Core Idea
A suspicion of a suspicion is too weak to be legally considered; otherwise, all trade would be closed.

This lesson examines the opening part of the passage, which defines the status of dirhams and dinars in sales. The author explains that these currencies are not specified in the contract, meaning the buyer can pay with any similar coins. If they were specified, a direct suspicion of corruption would arise. But when unspecified, only a 'suspicion of a suspicion' exists, which is legally irrelevant. The hadith 'Leave usury and suspicion' is cited to caution against direct shubha, not weak doubts. By the end, you will understand how the Hanafi school keeps trade open by disregarding remote possibilities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the baseline ruling on dirhams and dinars
The passage states that dirhams and dinars are 'non-specified' (ghayr muta'ayyinah) in a sale. This means the buyer may deliver any coins, not necessarily the ones shown. This is the default Hanafi position.
Contrast: if the parties explicitly specify the coins (e.g., 'these exact coins'), the ruling changes.
2
Understand the two types of khubth (impurity)
Khubth can arise from 'faulty ownership' (fasad al-milk) or 'lack of ownership' ('adam al-milk). The first is weaker; the second is stronger. The passage first deals with khubth due to fasad al-milk.
The later section on ghasb will address 'adam al-milk.
3
Analyze the concept of shubhat al-shubha
When dirhams are unspecified, the suspicion of impurity is indirect: the contract attaches to a generic liability, not to the specific usurped coins. This double remove creates a 'suspicion of a suspicion', which is too weak to affect purity.
The text quotes al-Nihayah: if such weak doubts were considered, no sale would be free from suspicion, trade would be impossible.
4
Examine the hadith and its interpretation
The hadith 'So leave usury and suspicion' is cited. Al-Tibi's commentary explains that the verse on riba is clear, so the Prophet did not elaborate; the command is to avoid clear riba and obvious suspicion, not every remote doubt.
This supports the Hanafi position that only direct or strong shubha is prohibited.

As for dirhams and dinars, they are not specified in the contract. If they were specified, there would be a suspicion of impurity due to corruption. So when they are not specified, there is a suspicion in the contract's attachment to them, thus there is a suspicion of suspicion, and it is not considered. This concerns impurity due to corruption of ownership. The Prophet, peace be upon him, accepted and did not explain it: 'So leave usury and suspicion.' Reported by Ibn Majah and al-Darimi. Meaning: This verse is established and not abrogated, but the Prophet accepted and did not explain it comprehensively, so you should avoid clear usury and what resembles it out of piety and caution. This is understood from the apparent context. Al-Tibi said: It means this verse is established, not abrogated, not ambiguous; therefore the Prophet did not explain it, so proceed upon it as it is, do not doubt it, and avoid tricks to legalize usury. Thus in al-Lum'at. [1] His statement: 'non-specified' because if the buyer pointed to them as 'these dirhams and dinars', he could leave them and pay others to the seller without objection. [2] His statement: 'suspicion of suspicion' because the attachment of soundness of the sale or estimation of price, which were a suspicion of impurity due to their being acquired from another's property entirely, is no longer so, but rather with a share of ownership. Thus in al-'Inayah. [3] His statement: 'it is not considered' because if it were considered, then what is below it would also be considered, so the sale would never be free from suspicion of suspicion, and the door of trade—which is open—would be closed. And because the origin in earning is suspicion, so this origin was set aside regarding suspicion, and what is lower remains on the origin. Thus in al-Nihayah. As for impurity due to lack of ownership, it includes both types. This is according to Abu Hanifa, meaning profit from usurped property is not pure whether the usurped item is specified, like a slave girl, or unspecified, like dirhams and dinars. Even if one sells usurped dirhams or dinars and makes a profit, it is not pure because in the first there is actual impurity, and in the second there is suspicion, and suspicion is treated as actual impurity. [1] His statement: 'this' refers to the mentioned difference between specified and unspecified, where profit is not pure in the first and is pure in the second. [2] His statement: 'according to Abu Hanifa' and also Muhammad, as stated by al-'Ayni and the author of al-Hidayah and others. In some copies it is 'according to Abu Hanifa', so the reference is to its inclusion of both types. Abu Yusuf said: profit is absolutely pure for him because in his view the condition for purity is liability (daman), which exists. According to Zufar and al-Shafi'i: it is not pure in all cases. Thus in Majma' al-Anhur. [3] His statement: 'in the second' i.e., what is unspecified, even if it is not a substitute for another's property because the contract is not attached to it but to its like in the liability, but the profit is reached by paying the usurped item, so there is a suspicion of impurity, and suspicion is treated as actual impurity in prohibition due to the emphasis on avoiding it. Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary that addresses multiple interrelated rulings concerning suspicion (shubha) in sales. The passage type is a composite: it begins with a ruling statement on dirhams and dinars being unspecified, provides a hadith as proof, expands on the concept of shubhat al-shubha (suspicion of suspicion), then discusses impurity (khubth) due to lack of ownership, including an intra-Hanafi dispute on profit from usurped goods, and concludes with an example of profit from a wrongly claimed debt. The author follows the order of the base text (al-Wiqayah), explaining each term. The key principle is that dirhams and dinars are not 'ayn (specified) in a sale contract; the buyer can pay with any similar coins. If they were specified, a direct suspicion of corruption would arise. The hadith 'Leave usury and suspicion' is cited, with commentary from al-Tibi clarifying that the verse on riba is clear and uncomplicated, so the Prophet did not elaborate on all details. The term 'shubhat al-shubha' means a doubt about a doubt—it is too weak to invalidate the contract. The commentary in 'al-Nihayah' explains that ignoring such weak doubts keeps the door of trade open. The second section distinguishes impurity (khubth) due to faulty ownership (in the earlier case) from impurity due to lack of ownership (ghasb). In the latter, profit from usurped property is not pure, whether the property is specified (like a slave) or unspecified (like money). The intra-madhhab dispute is recorded: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that profit is impure in both types; Abu Yusuf holds it is absolutely pure if the usurper had liability (daman); Zufar and al-Shafi'i say it is impure in all. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is not explicitly stated here but is typically the view of Abu Hanifa. The final example: if a person claims a debt and is awarded payment, then later both realize the debt was nonexistent, the profit from using that money is pure. This is analogized to the purity of profit from a valid sale after exchange. All examples are within the Hanafi tradition.

Key Terms
شبهة (shubha): A state of doubt or uncertainty about the permissibility of an act due to a conflict of evidence or potential impurity. In this passage, it refers to a suspicion of corruption that affects the purity of profit.
شبهة الشبهة (shubhat al-shubha): A 'suspicion of a suspicion'; a doubt that is indirect or remote. In this context, it arises when the contract attaches to a generic liability rather than specific corrupt goods.
خبث (khubth): Impurity or corruption in the context of earnings, rendering profit unlawful. It arises from either faulty ownership (fasad al-milk) or lack of ownership ('adam al-milk).
متعينة (muta'ayyinah): Specified or particularized (feminine plural). Refers to dirhams and dinars that are designated as specific coins in the contract, as opposed to being unspecified and payable from the liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school ignore shubhat al-shubha in the case of unspecified dirhams/dinars?
A) Because the hadith on riba does not apply.
B) Because if it were considered, all trade would be closed due to ubiquitous weak suspicions.
C) Because dirhams and dinars are never specified in any contract.
D) Because the suspicion is treated as actual impurity, but it is too weak to be actionable.

Key Takeaway
A suspicion of a suspicion (shubhat al-shubha) arising from unspecified dirhams/dinars in a sale with faulty ownership (fasad al-milk) is legally ignored, keeping the profit pure.

Profit from Usurpation: The Hanafi Intra-Madhhab Dispute
Intra-madhhab dispute on purity of profit from ghasb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on profit from usurped property according to the major Hanafi authorities, explain the basis of the dispute, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Profit from usurped property is impure in the dominant Hanafi view, but Abu Yusuf allows it if the usurper assumes liability (daman).

This lesson covers the second category of khubth: impurity due to lack of ownership ('adam al-milk), exemplified by usurpation (ghasb). The passage records a significant intra-Hanafi dispute. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that profit from ghasb is never pure, whether the item is specified (like a slave girl) or unspecified (like dirhams). Abu Yusuf disagrees, ruling that if the usurper is liable (daman), the profit becomes pure. Zufar and al-Shafi'i take a stricter view, prohibiting profit in all cases. The lesson also includes an example of profit from a wrongly claimed debt, which becomes pure after mutual agreement, contrasting with the ghasb case.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of khubth in the passage
The earlier part dealt with fasad al-milk; this section deals with 'adam al-milk. Recognize that ghasb falls into the latter, which is considered more severe.
The passage explicitly says 'أما الخبث بسبب عدم الملك فيشمل النوعين' (As for impurity due to lack of ownership, it includes both types).
2
State the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad on profit from ghasb
For specified items (like a slave girl), there is 'actual impurity' (haqiqat al-khubth). For unspecified items (like dirhams), there is a suspicion (shubha), but it is treated as actual impurity. Therefore, profit is impure in both cases.
The hadith's emphasis on avoiding suspicion supports this strict approach.
3
Contrast with Abu Yusuf's position and his dalil
Abu Yusuf says profit is absolutely pure for the usurper because he is liable (daman) to return the item or its value. In his view, liability purifies the profit, as it does in other cases (e.g., a borrower using the borrowed item).
This is a minority view within the Hanafi school; it relies on qiyas with other fiduciary relationships.
4
Contrast with Zufar and al-Shafi'i
Zufar and al-Shafi'i hold that profit is always impure from ghasb, regardless of specification or liability. They consider ghasb itself a sin that taints all resulting profit.
This is a stricter view; the Hanafi school generally does not follow it.
5
Apply the rule to the example of a wrongly claimed debt
The passage ends with a contrasting case: a person falsely claims a debt, is awarded payment, then later both agree the debt was nonexistent. The profit from using that money is pure. This is because the suspicion of khubth is removed by mutual agreement (al-tasaduq).
This shows that purity depends on the nature of the underlying khubth; here the initial suspicion was based on a claim, but after acknowledgment of error, it becomes clear that the money was actually the claimant's own, so profit is pure.

كما طاب ربح ما ادعاه فقضى بالمال، ثم ظهر عدمه بالتصادق : أي ادَّعَى على رجل مالاً ،فقضاه ، فرَيحَ فيه المدَّعي ، ثم تصادقا على أن هذا المال لم يكن على المدَّعَى عليه، فالربح طيب [1] قوله : هذا ؛ أي المذكور من الفرق بين ما يتعيّن وبين ما لا يتعين، حيث لا (1)4 يطيب الربح في الأَوَّلِ ويطيبُ في الثَّاني. [٤]قوله : كما طاب....الخ ؛ أي كما طاب للبائع ربح ثمنه بعد التقابض، كذلك طاب ربح مال ادّعاهُ مدَّع فقضى المدعى عليه بذلك المال، ثمَّ ظهرَ بالتَّصادقِ أنّ هذا المال لم يكن على المدعى عليه.
As for dirhams and dinars, they are not specified in the contract. If they were specified, there would be a suspicion of impurity due to corruption. So when they are not specified, there is a suspicion in the contract's attachment to them, thus there is a suspicion of suspicion, and it is not considered. This concerns impurity due to corruption of ownership. The Prophet, peace be upon him, accepted and did not explain it: 'So leave usury and suspicion.' Reported by Ibn Majah and al-Darimi. Meaning: This verse is established and not abrogated, but the Prophet accepted and did not explain it comprehensively, so you should avoid clear usury and what resembles it out of piety and caution. This is understood from the apparent context. Al-Tibi said: It means this verse is established, not abrogated, not ambiguous; therefore the Prophet did not explain it, so proceed upon it as it is, do not doubt it, and avoid tricks to legalize usury. Thus in al-Lum'at. [1] His statement: 'non-specified' because if the buyer pointed to them as 'these dirhams and dinars', he could leave them and pay others to the seller without objection. [2] His statement: 'suspicion of suspicion' because the attachment of soundness of the sale or estimation of price, which were a suspicion of impurity due to their being acquired from another's property entirely, is no longer so, but rather with a share of ownership. Thus in al-'Inayah. [3] His statement: 'it is not considered' because if it were considered, then what is below it would also be considered, so the sale would never be free from suspicion of suspicion, and the door of trade—which is open—would be closed. And because the origin in earning is suspicion, so this origin was set aside regarding suspicion, and what is lower remains on the origin. Thus in al-Nihayah. As for impurity due to lack of ownership, it includes both types. This is according to Abu Hanifa, meaning profit from usurped property is not pure whether the usurped item is specified, like a slave girl, or unspecified, like dirhams and dinars. Even if one sells usurped dirhams or dinars and makes a profit, it is not pure because in the first there is actual impurity, and in the second there is suspicion, and suspicion is treated as actual impurity. [1] His statement: 'this' refers to the mentioned difference between specified and unspecified, where profit is not pure in the first and is pure in the second. [2] His statement: 'according to Abu Hanifa' and also Muhammad, as stated by al-'Ayni and the author of al-Hidayah and others. In some copies it is 'according to Abu Hanifa', so the reference is to its inclusion of both types. Abu Yusuf said: profit is absolutely pure for him because in his view the condition for purity is liability (daman), which exists. According to Zufar and al-Shafi'i: it is not pure in all cases. Thus in Majma' al-Anhur. [3] His statement: 'in the second' i.e., what is unspecified, even if it is not a substitute for another's property because the contract is not attached to it but to its like in the liability, but the profit is reached by paying the usurped item, so there is a suspicion of impurity, and suspicion is treated as actual impurity in prohibition due to the emphasis on avoiding it. Book of Sales/Corrupt and Disliked Sales. Just as the seller's profit from his price after exchange becomes pure, similarly the profit from money that a claimant claimed and the defendant was ordered to pay, then it became clear through mutual agreement that the money was not owed, the profit is pure. [4] His statement: 'just as...' i.e., just as the seller's profit from his price after exchange becomes pure, similarly the profit from money that a claimant claimed and the defendant was ordered to pay, then it became clear through mutual agreement that the money was not owed.

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary that addresses multiple interrelated rulings concerning suspicion (shubha) in sales. The passage type is a composite: it begins with a ruling statement on dirhams and dinars being unspecified, provides a hadith as proof, expands on the concept of shubhat al-shubha (suspicion of suspicion), then discusses impurity (khubth) due to lack of ownership, including an intra-Hanafi dispute on profit from usurped goods, and concludes with an example of profit from a wrongly claimed debt. The author follows the order of the base text (al-Wiqayah), explaining each term. The key principle is that dirhams and dinars are not 'ayn (specified) in a sale contract; the buyer can pay with any similar coins. If they were specified, a direct suspicion of corruption would arise. The hadith 'Leave usury and suspicion' is cited, with commentary from al-Tibi clarifying that the verse on riba is clear and uncomplicated, so the Prophet did not elaborate on all details. The term 'shubhat al-shubha' means a doubt about a doubt—it is too weak to invalidate the contract. The commentary in 'al-Nihayah' explains that ignoring such weak doubts keeps the door of trade open. The second section distinguishes impurity (khubth) due to faulty ownership (in the earlier case) from impurity due to lack of ownership (ghasb). In the latter, profit from usurped property is not pure, whether the property is specified (like a slave) or unspecified (like money). The intra-madhhab dispute is recorded: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that profit is impure in both types; Abu Yusuf holds it is absolutely pure if the usurper had liability (daman); Zufar and al-Shafi'i say it is impure in all. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is not explicitly stated here but is typically the view of Abu Hanifa. The final example: if a person claims a debt and is awarded payment, then later both realize the debt was nonexistent, the profit from using that money is pure. This is analogized to the purity of profit from a valid sale after exchange. All examples are within the Hanafi tradition.

Key Terms
غصب (ghasb): Usurpation; taking another's property without right and without consent.
ضمان (daman): Liability; responsibility to compensate for loss or return property. Abu Yusuf considered daman as a condition that purifies profit.
تصادق (tasaduq): Mutual agreement or acknowledgment. In the passage, both parties agree that the debt was false, which removes the suspicion of khubth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school, what must be done with profit earned from selling a usurped item?
A) The profit may be kept because the usurper is liable.
B) The profit must be given in charity (sadaqah).
C) The profit must be returned to the original owner.
D) The profit becomes pure if the usurper repents.

Key Takeaway
Profit from usurped property is impure in the dominant Hanafi view; the minority opinion of Abu Yusuf (pure if liable) is recognized but not relied upon for fatwa.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Profit from Usurped Goods: The Role of Specificity and Shubhah
Intra-Hanafi dispute on profit permissibility in usurped goods and false claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on profit from usurped goods and explain how the nature of the goods (specific vs. non-specific) and the concept of shubhah determine the ruling.

Core Idea
Specific usurped goods carry actual impurity; non-specific carry a semblance—but when the semblance is weak, profit becomes permissible.

This passage presents a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute over whether profit from a sale involving usurped property is permissible. The ruling hinges on whether the property is specific (e.g., a slave girl) or non-specific (e.g., dirhams), and how the legal principle of shubhah (semblance of prohibition) is applied. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to trace how Hanafi jurists use analogical reasoning and gradations of impurity to derive rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of goods
Note the distinction between specific goods (like a slave girl) and non-specific goods (like dirhams and dinars). This distinction is central to the ruling.
2
State the Hanafi position by Abu Hanifa and Muhammad
For specific usurped goods, profit is not permissible due to actual impurity (khabath haqiqi). For non-specific usurped goods, profit is permissible because only a semblance (shubhah) exists, but in the case of direct usurpation, the semblance is annexed to actual impurity—so profit is still not permissible.
The commentator clarifies that for usurped goods, even non-specific goods are not permissible because the shubhah is strong.
3
Note the opposing views within the Hanafi school
Abu Yusuf holds that profit is permissible in all cases because the condition of daman (guarantee) is met, while Zufar (and al-Shafi'i) hold that profit is never permissible.
These differences arise from how each scholar weighs the shubhah.
4
Analyze the case of a false claim
When a claimant falsely claims a debt and later the truth emerges, profit from the money received is permissible. The money is a substitute for a debt that was validly claimed, so the impurity is weakened—it becomes a 'semblance of a semblance' (shubhah al-shubhah) and is therefore not considered.
5
Resolve the apparent contradiction about dirhams becoming specific
The passage addresses a potential contradiction: in an invalid sale, dirhams can become specific by specification if still extant. The commentator reconciles this by stating that the contract has two aspects—if the goods exist, the semblance of usurpation dominates; if not, the semblance of sale dominates, so corruption does not transmit.
This shows the layered use of shubhah.

Book of Sales / Invalid and Disliked Sales. As permissible is the profit of what he claimed and the judge decreed the payment, then its non-existence appeared by mutual agreement. As for the impurity due to lack of ownership, it includes both types according to Abu Hanifa — meaning: profit from usurped property is not permissible, whether the usurped property is specific like a slave girl for example, or non-specific like dirhams and dinars, even if he sold the usurped dirhams or dinars and made a profit on them, the profit is not permissible; because in the first there is actual impurity, and in the second there is a semblance (shubha), and the semblance is annexed to the actual. As for the phrase 'as permissible is the profit of what he claimed and the judge decreed the payment, then its non-existence appeared by mutual agreement': it means a person claimed money from a man, the judge decreed it, so the claimant profited from it, then they agreed mutually that this money was not owed by the defendant, so the profit is permissible. His statement: 'this' refers to the mentioned distinction between what is specific and what is non-specific, where profit is not permissible in the first and is permissible in the second. His statement: 'according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad also, as clarified by al-Ayni and the author of al-Hidayah and others; and in some copies this is according to Abu Hanifa, so the indication is that it encompasses both types. And Abu Yusuf said: profit is absolutely permissible for him, because according to him the condition for permissibility is guarantee (daman), and it existed. And according to Zufar and al-Shafi'i: it is not permissible in all cases. Thus in Majma' al-Anhur. His statement: 'and in the second': i.e. regarding what is non-specific, even if it is not a substitute for another's property; because the contract does not pertain to it, but to the like of it in the dhimmah, but one reaches the profit only by delivering the usurped item, so a semblance of impurity occurs in it, and the semblance is annexed to the actual in prohibition, because emphasis has come on avoiding it. His statement: 'as permissible is the profit...' etc.: i.e. just as the seller's profit from its price after receipt (taqabud) is permissible, similarly the profit of money claimed by a claimant and the defendant was decreed to pay that money, then it appeared by mutual agreement that this money was not owed by the defendant. Because the money decreed is a substitute for the debt which is the right of the claimant, and the claimant sold his debt for what he took; so when they agreed on the non-existence of the debt, it became as if he (the claimant) had reclaimed the seller's property, and the substitute for the reclaimed property is owned with an invalid ownership, so the sale with respect to the substitute is an invalid sale, so the impurity does not affect what is not specific by specification. If it is said: mentioned in al-Hidayah in the previous issue: 'then if the dirhams of the price are still in existence, he takes them specifically; because they become specific by specification in an invalid sale, and this is the most correct; because it is like usurpation.' This contradicts what you have said about dirhams and dinars not being specific. We say: reconciliation is possible between them by saying that this contract has two semblances: 1. the semblance of usurpation, 2. the semblance of sale. If the goods are still in existence, the semblance of usurpation is considered in an effort to remove the invalid sale; if they are not in existence and he purchased something with them, the semblance of sale is considered, so that corruption does not transmit to its substitute, as we mentioned regarding the semblance of the semblance. Also, the circulation of hands has an effect in removing prohibition, as is known. His statement: 'because...' etc. The summary: the money paid is a substitute for the debt which is the right of the claimant by the defendant's admission; for a person is held accountable by his admission legally, so the claimant becomes a seller of his debt for what he took. When they agreed that the debt was not owed by the defendant, the claimant becomes as if he reclaimed the debt, so it follows that the debt is owned by an invalid sale; because the sold item here is invalid with respect to the substitute, and it is not in existence. As if he sold a slave girl for a thousand dirhams and they exchanged, then she was reclaimed, the price becomes owned by the seller, because it must be returned, but it is not voided, so a semblance of non-ownership occurs in it. And if profit occurs in dirhams not owned, a semblance of impurity occurs in the profit. And if it occurs from dirhams having a semblance of non-ownership, then there is a semblance of a semblance of impurity, so it is not considered, so impurity does not affect what is not specific by specification among dirhams and dinars. His statement: 'we say...' etc. He said in al-Durar: indeed what Sadr al-Shari'ah mentioned does not effect reconciliation. End of quote from al-Hidayah. Durar al-Hukkam.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the permissibility of profit (ribh) from sales involving usurped goods (maghsab) or false claims. The original matn states that profit claimed from a false debt is permissible if the parties later agree the debt did not exist. The commentator first explains a general principle: impurity (khabath) due to lack of ownership includes both specific items (e.g., a slave girl) and non-specific items (e.g., dirhams and dinars). For usurped goods, profit is not permissible in either case, because in specific items there is actual impurity, and in non-specific there is a semblance (shubhah) of impurity, and shubhah is annexed to actuality. However, in the case of a false claim, profit is permissible because the money paid is treated as a substitute for a debt that was validly claimed. The commentator then reports the school positions: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say profit is not permissible for specific goods but is permissible for non-specific goods, whereas Abu Yusuf says profit is permissible absolutely (relying on the condition of daman/guarantee), and Zufar and al-Shafi'i say it is never permissible. A potential contradiction is raised regarding dirhams: in a previous ruling in al-Hidayah, dirhams become specific by specification in an invalid sale, but here dirhams are considered non-specific. The commentator resolves this by explaining that the contract has two aspects: if the dirhams still exist, the semblance of usurpation dominates; if they are gone, the semblance of sale dominates, so corruption does not transmit to the substitute. This is a subtle legal reasoning using shubhah analysis.

Key Terms
غصب (ghasb): Unlawful taking of another's property without ownership or permission.
شبهة (shubhah): A legal doubt or semblance that affects the ruling; here it refers to a situation that resembles the prohibited but is not identical, yet is treated like the real due to strong emphasis on avoidance.
تعين (ta'ayyun): The quality of being specific (e.g., a specific slave girl) vs. non-specific (e.g., dirhams which are like fungibles). In the discussion, it affects how shubhah is applied.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, in the case of a false claim (mutual agreement that the debt never existed), profit from the money received is:
A) Not permissible because the money was not rightfully owned
B) Permissible because the money is non-specific and there is a shubhah of ownership
C) Not permissible because all usurped goods are treated the same
D) Permissible only if the claimant did not know the claim was false

Key Takeaway
Profit from usurped goods is not permissible for specific items due to actual impurity, but permissible for non-specific items in cases of false claim due to weakened shubhah (shubhah al-shubhah).

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Currency Specification in a Corrupt Sale: Resolving the Contradiction in al-Hidayah
Resolving a seeming contradiction in al-Hidayah about currency specification in corrupt sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that currency becomes specified (muta'ayyin) in a corrupt sale when the price is still in hand, and apply the shubhah principle to resolve the apparent contradiction within the school.

Core Idea
In a corrupt sale, currency becomes specified only when still in hand — the dominant shubhah determines the ruling.

This passage addresses a key inner-Hanafi dispute: do dirhams and dinars become specifically identified (tata'ayyan bi al-ta'yin) in a corrupt sale (bay' fasid)? Al-Hidayah initially states they do when the price is still in existence, but later seems to say otherwise. Our author reconciles the two statements by noting that a corrupt sale has two legal similarities (shubhah): one to usurpation (ghasb) and one to a valid sale. Which similarity governs depends on whether the price is still in hand. Understanding this resolution is crucial for correctly applying the rulings on returning the price in corrupt sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent contradiction
State the two seemingly contradictory statements from al-Hidayah: one that the price becomes specified by specification in a corrupt sale (qa'im case), and another that it does not (ghayr qa'im case).
2
Understand the two shubhahs
Recognize that a corrupt sale resembles both ghasb (usurpation) and a valid sale. Each similarity exerts a legal effect.
Shubhah is a key Hanafi concept for extending or restricting rulings.
3
Apply the conditional rule
When the price is still in existence (qa'im), the ghasb shubhah dominates → currency becomes specified and must be returned in kind. When not qa'im, the sale shubhah dominates → no specification, only value is owed.
4
Note the riwayat difference
There are two transmissions from Abu Hanifa: Abu Suleiman's (specification) and Abu Hafs's (no specification). The soundest (asahh) is Abu Suleiman's.
The asahh is relied upon in al-Hidayah.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The operative ruling is that currency becomes specified when the price is still in hand, following the riwayah of Abu Suleiman. This is the position used for fatwa.
This is the view adopted by al-Hidayah and later Hanafi scholarship.

Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of a seeming contradiction in al-Hidayah regarding whether dirhams and dinars become specified (muta'ayyin) in a corrupt sale (bay' fasid). The author first explains the original ruling: when a plaintiff and defendant agree that the debt was never owed, the money paid becomes a corruptly owned substitute, and impurity (khabath) does not affect items that are not specified by specification (like currency that is not inherently identified). Then a question is raised: In an earlier section of al-Hidayah, it was stated that if the dirhams of the price are still in existence (qa'imah), the buyer takes them specifically because they become specified in a corrupt sale. This seems to contradict the present text asserting non-specification. The resolution is that a corrupt sale has two similarities (shubhah): one to usurpation (ghasb) and one to sale. When the price is still in hand, the similarity to usurpation dominates, aiming to undo the corrupt contract, so the price is taken back specifically. When the price is no longer in hand (e.g., used to buy something), the similarity to sale dominates, so the corruption does not spread to the substitute, and the price remains unspecified. The commentary then delves into various attempts at reconciliation from later scholars (al-Durar, al-Inayah, al-Fara'id, Majma' al-Anhur). The final conclusion notes that there are two riwayat: one from Abu Suleiman (that they become specified) which is the soundest (asahh), and one from Abu Hafs (that they do not). The passage teaches that the ruling depends on whether the price is still in existence, and that the internal Hanafi difference stems from different transmissions.

Key Terms
تتعين بالتعيين (tata'ayyan bi al-ta'yin): The process by which otherwise non-specific items (like dirhams) become specifically identified and must be returned in kind.
بيع فاسد (bay' fasid): A sale that is flawed in one of its necessary conditions but not in its essence; it requires cancellation and return of the price.
شبهة (shubhah): A resemblance that gives a contract some legal effect; in this context, the corrupt sale resembles both a valid sale and usurpation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Based on the reconciliation in this passage, when does the price in a corrupt sale become specified (muta'ayyin)?
A) Always, because all corrupt sales require return of the exact price.
B) Only when the price is no longer in hand (ghayr qa'im) due to similarity to sale.
C) Only when the price is still in hand (qa'im) due to similarity to usurpation.
D) Never, because dirhams and dinars are never specified by ta'yin.

Key Takeaway
In a corrupt sale, currency becomes specified only when the price is still in hand (qa'im) due to the dominant shubhah of usurpation.
